I take the ax from the wall and hold the wooden handle, scraping my finger across the head. Can’t see anything, but it feels like it hasn’t been sharpened in years—deep chips in the blade, which is no longer sharp.

Blinking to clear the sweat, I carefully open the door.

Not a sound comes in.

I push it open a bit more, until I can see the backyard again.

It’s empty.

In this moment of quiet, Occam’s razor whispers to me—the simplest answer is usually the right one. Does the idea that I was drugged and kidnapped by a secret group for mind control fit that? Not really. They would’ve had to brainwash me to believe my house wasn't mine, or in a few hours, get rid of my family and change the inside so I didn't recognize it.

Or—is it more likely a brain tumor has turned my world upside down?

That it’s been growing silently in my head for months or years and is now messing with my mind, changing how I see everything.

The idea feels true.

What else could have hit me so hard and fast?

What else could make me lose myself and reality in hours, making me question everything?

I wait.

And wait.

And wait.

Finally, I step outside onto the grass.

No more voices.

No more footsteps.

No shadows.

No car sounds.

The night feels strong and real again.

I already know where I’m going next.

—

Chicago Mercy is a ten-block walk from my house, and I limp into the bright light of the ER at 4:05 a.m.

I hate hospitals.

I watched my mother die in one.

Charlie spent the first weeks of his life in a NICU.

The waiting room is almost empty. Besides me, there’s a construction worker holding his bleeding arm, and a worried family of three, the father holding a crying baby.

The woman at the front desk looks up from her paperwork, surprisingly awake for this hour.

She asks through the glass, “How can I help you?”

I haven’t thought of what to say, or how to explain what I need.

When I don’t answer right away, she says, “Were you in an accident?”

“No.”

“You have cuts all over your face.”

“I’m not feeling good,” I say.

“What do you mean?”

“I think I need to speak to someone.”

“Are you homeless?”

“No.”

“Where’s your family?”

“I don’t know.”

She looks me up and down—a quick, practiced look.

“Your name, sir?”

“Jason.”

“One moment.”

Rising from her chair, she disappears around the corner.

Thirty seconds later, a buzzing sound signals the door beside her station unlocks and opens.

The nurse smiles. “Come on back.”

She leads me to a patient room.

“Someone will be right with you.”

As the door closes behind her, I sit on the examination table and shut my eyes to block the bright lights. I have never been so tired.

My chin dips.

I straighten up.

I almost fell asleep sitting up.

The door opens.

A stout young doctor walks in with a clipboard. Behind him is a different nurse—a blonde in blue scrubs, looking as tired as someone up since four in the morning.

“You’re Jason?” the doctor asks, not shaking my hand or pretending to care, like most people on the night shift.

I nod.

“Last name?”

I don’t want to tell him my full name, but maybe that’s just the tumor talking, or whatever is wrong in my head.

“Dessen.”

I spell it out as he writes on what looks like an admission form.

“I’m Dr. Randolph, the main doctor here. Why did you come to the ER tonight?”

“I think something is wrong with my mind. Like a tumor or something.”

“What makes you say that?”

“Things aren’t right.”

“Okay. Can you explain more?”

“I…okay, this will sound crazy. I know that.”

He looks up from the clipboard.

“My house isn’t my house.”

“I don’t understand.”

“It’s just what I said. My house isn’t my house. My family isn’t there. Everything’s much…nicer. It’s all been fixed up and—”

“But it’s still your address?”

“Right.”

"So you're saying the inside is different, but the outside is the same?" He says it like he's talking to a child.

"Yeah."

"Jason, how did you get the cuts on your face? The mud on your clothes?"

"People were chasing me."

I shouldn't have said that, but I'm too tired to think straight. I must sound completely crazy.

"Chasing you."

"Yes."

"Who was chasing you?"

"I don't know."

"Do you know why they were chasing you?"

“Because…it’s complicated.”

His careful, doubtful look is much more subtle and practiced than the front-desk nurse’s. I almost don’t notice it.

“Have you taken any drugs or alcohol tonight?” he asks.

“Some wine earlier, then whisky, but that was hours ago.”

“Again, I’m sorry—it’s been a very long shift—but what makes you think something is wrong with your mind?”

“Because the last eight hours of my life don’t make sense. It all feels real, but it can’t be.”

“Have you suffered a recent head injury?”

“No. Well. I mean, I think someone hit me in the back of the head. It hurts when I touch it.”

“Who hit you?”

“I’m not sure. I’m not really sure of anything right now.”

“Okay. Do you use drugs? Now or ever?”

“I smoke weed a few times a year. But not recently.”

The doctor turns to the nurse. “I’m going to have Barbara take some blood.”

He drops the clipboard on a table and pulls a penlight from his lab coat pocket.

“Mind if I check you out?”

“No.”

Randolph moves in until our faces are very close, close enough for me to smell the old coffee on his breath, to see the fresh cut on his chin. He shines the light straight into my right eye. For a moment, there’s only a bright spot in the middle of what I see, which briefly wipes out everything else.

“Jason, are you thinking about hurting yourself?”

“I’m not suicidal.”

The light hits my left eye.

“Have you been in a mental hospital before?”

“No.”

He gently takes my wrist in his soft, cool hands, measures my pulse.

“What’s your job?” he asks.

“I teach at Lakemont College.”

“Married?”

“Yes.” I automatically reach down to touch my wedding band.

Gone.

Jesus.

The nurse begins to roll up the left sleeve of my shirt.

“What’s your wife’s name?” the doctor asks.

“Daniela.”

“Are you two doing okay?”

“Yes.”

“Don’t you think she’s wondering where you are? I think we should call her.”

“I tried.”

“When?”

“An hour ago, at my house. Someone else answered. It was the wrong number.”

“Maybe you dialed wrong.”

“I know my wife’s phone number.”

The nurse asks, “Are needles okay with you, Mr. Dessen?”

“Yes.”

As she cleans the underside of my arm, she says, “Dr. Randolph, look.” She points to the needle mark from a few hours ago when Leighton took my blood.

“When did this happen?” he asks.

“I don’t know.” It’s probably better not to mention the lab I think I just got away from.

“You don’t remember someone giving you a shot?”

“No.”

Randolph nods to the nurse, and she warns me, “This will sting a little.”

He asks, “Do you have your cell phone?”

“I don’t know where it is.”

He grabs the clipboard. “Tell me your wife’s name and phone number again. We’ll try to reach her for you.”

I spell Daniela’s name and quickly say her cell number and our home number as my blood goes into a plastic vial.

“You’re going to scan my head?” I ask. “See what’s happening?”

“Absolutely.”

—

They give me a private room on the eighth floor.

I wash my face in the bathroom, take off my shoes, and get into bed.

I feel sleepy, but I can't turn off my brain.

I can’t stop thinking.

Making guesses and proving them wrong.

Struggling to make sense of everything that’s happened.

Right now, I can’t tell what’s real and what’s not. I can’t even be sure I was ever married.

No. Wait.

I lift my left hand and look closely at my ring finger.

The ring is gone, but the faint mark it left is still there around my finger. It was there. It left a mark. That means someone took it.

I touch the mark, feeling both the horror and the comfort of what it means—the last bit of my reality.

I wonder—

What will happen when this last bit of my marriage is gone?

When there’s nothing left to hold on to?

As the sky over Chicago slowly turns to dawn—a dull, cloudy purple—I fall asleep.

Chapter 4

Daniela’s hands are deep in the warm, soapy water when she hears the front door slam. She stops scrubbing the saucepan she’s been working on for the last thirty seconds and looks up from the sink, glancing back as footsteps come closer.

Jason appears in the doorway between the kitchen and the dining room, grinning like an idiot, as her mother would say.

Turning back to the dishes, Daniela says, “There’s a plate for you in the fridge.”

In the blurry reflection of the window above the sink, she watches her husband set the canvas grocery bag on the island and walk toward her.

He puts his arms around her waist.

She says, half joking, “If you think a couple pints of ice cream are going to get you out of this, I don’t know what to tell you.”

He presses against her and whispers in her ear, his breath hot with the taste of whatever whisky he’s been drinking, “Life’s short. Don’t be mad. It’s a waste of time.”

“How did forty-five minutes become almost three hours?”

“The same way one drink becomes two, then three, and so on. I feel awful.”

His lips on the back of her neck sent a soft shiver down her spine.

She says, “You’re not getting out of this.”

Now he kisses the side of her neck. It’s been a while since he touched her like this.

His hands slide into the water.

He weaves their fingers together.

“You should eat something,” she says. “I’ll warm up your plate.”

She tries to walk past him to the fridge, but he stops her.

Facing him now, she looks up into his eyes, and maybe it’s because they’ve both been drinking, but the air between them feels electric, as if every tiny part has been charged.

He says, “My God, I’ve missed you.”

“Exactly how much did you drink to—?”

He kisses her suddenly, pushing her against the cabinets, the counter pressing into her back as he slides his hands over her hips and untucks her shirt, his hands burning against her skin.

She pushes him toward the island.

“Jesus, Jason.”

She looks at him in the dim kitchen light, trying to understand the energy he’s brought back home.

“Something happened while you were out,” she says.

“Nothing happened, other than I lost track of time.”

“So you didn’t flirt with some young woman at Ryan’s party who made you feel young again? And now you’re back here wanting sex, pretending—”

He laughs. A lovely sound.

“What?” she says.

“That’s what you think?” He steps closer. “When I left the bar, I was distracted. I wasn’t paying attention. I walked into traffic and a cab almost hit me. It terrified me. I can’t explain it, but ever since—in the store, walking home, here in our kitchen—I’ve felt so alive. Like I truly see my life. All the things I’m thankful for. You. Charlie.”

She feels her anger toward him starting to fade.

He says, “It’s like we get so stuck in our routines, so deep in those patterns, we stop seeing our loved ones as they really are. But tonight, right now, I see you again, like when we first met, when your voice and your scent were a whole new world to me. I’m talking too much.”

Daniela goes to him, holds his face in her hands, and kisses him.

Then she takes his hand and leads him upstairs.

The hallway is dark, and she can’t remember the last time her husband did something that made her heart race like this.

At Charlie’s room, she pauses and listens at the closed door, noting the faint sound of music blasting through headphones.

“All clear,” she whispers.

They move down the squeaky hallway as quietly as they can.

In their bedroom, Daniela locks the door and opens the top drawer of her dresser, looking for a candle to light, but Jason doesn’t wait.

He pulls her to the bed and pulls her down onto the mattress, and then he’s on top of her, kissing her, his hands moving under her clothes, exploring her body.

She feels wetness on her cheek, her lips.

Tears.

His.

Holding his face between her hands, she asks, “Why are you crying?”

“I felt like I’d lost you.”

“You have me, Jason,” she says. “I’m right here, baby. You have me.”

As he undresses her in the darkness of their bedroom, she has never wanted anyone so desperately. The anger is gone. The wine-sleepiness has vanished. He has taken her back to the first time they made love, in her Bucktown loft with the downtown lights glowing through the giant windows that she’d cracked open so the cool October air could drift in, carrying the late-night sounds of people walking home from bars and distant sirens and the hum of the huge city at rest—not completely silent, never off, just a comforting, steady background.

As she climaxes, she tries not to cry out in their bedroom, but she can’t stop it, and neither can Jason.

Not tonight.

Because something is different; something is better.

They haven't been unhappy these last few years, not at all. But it's been a long, long time since she felt that giddy love that bubbles in your stomach and completely turns the world upside down.

Chapter 5

“Mr. Dessen?”

I jolt awake.

“Hi. Sorry to startle you.”

A doctor is looking at me—a short, green-eyed redhead in a white coat holding a cup of coffee and a tablet.

I sit up.

It’s daytime outside the window by my bed, and for a few seconds, I have no idea where I am.

Through the glass: low clouds cover the city, hiding the skyline above one thousand feet. From here, I can see the lake and two miles of Chicago, everything softened by a sad, midwestern gray.

“Mr. Dessen, do you know where you are?”

“Mercy Hospital.”

“That’s right. You came to the ER last night, quite confused. Dr. Randolph, one of my coworkers, checked you in, and when he left this morning, he gave me your file. I’m Julianne Springer.”

I look down at the IV in my wrist and follow the tube up to the bag hanging above me on a metal stand.

“What’s in that?” I ask.

“Just plain water. You were really dehydrated. How do you feel now?”

I quickly check how I’m doing.

Nauseous.

Head throbbing.

Mouth completely dry.

I point through the window. “Like that,” I say. “Like a bad hangover.”

More than just feeling sick, I feel incredibly empty, like my soul is getting rained on.

Like I’ve been hollowed out.

“I have your MRI results,” she says, turning on her tablet. “Your scan was normal. There was some light bruising, but nothing serious. Your tox screen results are more telling. We found traces of alcohol, as you told Dr. Randolph, but also something else.”

“What?”

“Ketamine.”

“Don’t know it.”

“It’s a surgical painkiller. One side effect is short-term memory loss. That could explain some of your confusion. The tox screen also showed something I’ve never seen. A mind-altering substance. A really strange mix.” She sips her coffee. “I have to ask—you didn’t take these drugs, did you?”

“Of course not.”

“Last night, you gave Dr. Randolph your wife’s name and a couple of phone numbers.”

“Her cell and our home phone.”

“I’ve been trying to find her all morning, but her cell number belongs to a guy named Ralph, and your home phone just goes to voicemail.”

“Can you read her number back to me?”

Springer reads off Daniela’s cell-phone number.

“That’s right,” I say.

“You’re sure about that?”

“Hundred percent.” As she looks back at the tablet, I ask, “Could these drugs you found in my system cause lasting mental changes?”

“You mean delusions? Hallucinations?”

“Exactly.”

“To be honest, I don’t know what this chemical is, so I can’t say for sure what effect it might have had on your nervous system.”

“So it could still be affecting me?”

“Again, I don’t know how long it stays in your body. But you don’t seem to be under the influence of anything right now.”

Memories from last night are coming back.

I see myself walking naked into an empty building, with someone pointing a gun at me.

A shot in my neck.

In my leg.

Bits of a weird talk with a man in a geisha mask.

A room full of old machines and moonlight.

And while thinking about last night feels like a real memory, it also feels like a dream, or a nightmare.

What happened to me in that old building?

Springer pulls a chair over and sits next to my bed. Up close, I see freckles all over her face like light sand.

“Let’s talk about what you told Dr. Randolph. He wrote down…” She sighs. “Sorry, his writing is terrible. ‘Patient says: It was my house, but not my house.’ You also said you got the cuts and bruises on your face because people were chasing you, but when we asked why, you couldn’t answer.” She looks up from the screen. “You’re a professor?”

“Correct.”

“At…”

“Lakemont College.”

“Listen, Jason. While you were asleep, and after we couldn’t find any sign of your wife—”

“What do you mean you couldn’t find any sign of her?”

“Her name is Daniela Dessen, right?”

“Yes.”

“Thirty-nine years old?”

“Yeah.”

“We couldn’t find anyone with that name and age in Chicago.”

That knocks me flat. I look away from Springer, back out the window. It’s so gray that even the time of day is hidden. Morning, noon, evening—it’s impossible to tell. Tiny drops of rain stick to the other side of the glass.

Now, I’m not even sure what scares me more—this reality that might be true, or the chance that my mind is falling apart. I preferred thinking a brain tumor caused everything. At least that was an explanation.

“Jason, we looked you up. Your name. Job. Everything we could find. I want you to answer carefully. Do you really think you’re a physics professor at Lakemont College?”

“I don’t think it. It’s what I am.”

“We checked the faculty pages for science departments at every university and college in Chicago. Including Lakemont. You weren’t listed as a professor at any of them.”

“That’s impossible. I’ve been teaching there since—”

“Let me finish, because we did find some information about you.” She types something on her tablet. “Jason Ashley Dessen, born 1973 in Denison, Iowa, to Randall and Ellie Dessen. It says here your mother died when you were eight. How? If you don’t mind me asking.”

“She had a heart problem, caught a bad flu, which became pneumonia.”

“Sorry to hear that.” She keeps reading. “Bachelor’s degree from University of Chicago, 1995. PhD from the same university, 2002. So far so good?”

I nod.

"You won the Pavia Prize in 2004, and that same year, Science magazine put you on the cover, calling your work the ‘breakthrough of the year.’ You’ve been a guest speaker at Harvard, Princeton, and UC Berkeley.” She looks up, sees I’m confused, and turns the tablet so I can see she’s reading from Jason A. Dessen’s Wikipedia page.

The heart monitor I’m hooked up to shows my heart is beating faster.

Springer says, “You haven’t put out any new papers or taken any teaching jobs since 2005, when you became chief science officer at Velocity Laboratories, a jet propulsion lab. It also says your brother reported you missing eight months ago, and you haven’t been seen in public for over a year.”

This hits me so hard I can hardly breathe.

My blood pressure sets off an alarm on the heart monitor, and it starts beeping loudly.

A large nurse comes to the door.

“We’re okay,” Springer says. “Can you turn that off?”

The nurse goes to the monitor and stops the alarm.

After he leaves, the doctor reaches over the railing and touches my hand.

“I want to help you, Jason. I can tell you’re scared. I don’t know what’s happened to you, and I think you don’t either.”

The wind off the lake is strong enough to push the rain sideways. I watch the drops race across the glass, turning the world outside into a blurry, gray cityscape with distant taillights and headlights.

Springer says, “I’ve called the police. They’re sending a detective to take your statement and start figuring out what happened last night. That’s our first step. I couldn’t reach Daniela, but I found your brother Michael’s contact information in Iowa City. I’d like your permission to call him, let him know you’re here, and talk about how you’re doing.”

I don’t know how to respond. I haven’t talked to my brother in two years.

“I’m not sure I want you to call him,” I say.

“That’s fine, but understand that under HIPAA, if I think a patient can’t agree or disagree to sharing information because they’re unable to or in an emergency, I can decide if telling a family member or friend is best. I believe your current mental state counts as an inability to decide, and I think talking to someone who knows you and your past is in your best interest. So, I will call Michael.”

She looks at the floor, like she doesn’t want to say what’s next.

“Third thing, last thing,” she says. “We need a psychiatrist’s help to understand what’s going on with you. I’m transferring you to Chicago-Read, a mental health center further up on the North Side.”

“I know I don’t fully understand what’s happening, but I’m not crazy. I’d be glad to talk to a psychiatrist. I’d even like that. But I’m not agreeing to be locked up, if that’s what you’re asking.”

“That’s not what I’m asking. With all respect, Jason, you don’t have a choice.”

“Excuse me?”

“It’s called an M1 hold, and by law, if I think you’re a danger to yourself or others, I can force you to stay here for seventy-two hours. Look, this is what’s best for you. You’re in no shape—”

“I walked into this hospital on my own, because I wanted to know what was wrong with me.”

“And that was the right thing to do, and that’s exactly what we’re going to do: find out why you’re losing touch with reality, and get you the treatment you need to get better.”

I watch my blood pressure go up on the monitor.

I don’t want to set off the alarm again.

Closing my eyes, I breathe in.

Let it out.

Take another breath of oxygen.

My levels go down.

I say, “So you’re going to put me in a padded room, no belt, no sharp objects, and drug me until I’m senseless?”

“It’s not like that. You came to this hospital because you wanted to get better, right? Well, this is the first step. I need you to trust me.”

Springer gets up from the chair and pulls it back across the room under the television. “Just keep resting, Jason. Police will be here soon, and then we’ll get you moved to Chicago-Read tonight.”

I watch her leave, feeling like I’m about to fall apart, the feeling pressing down on me.

What if all the things I believe and remember that make me who I am—my job, Daniela, my son—are just a sad mistake in my brain? Will I keep trying to be the man I think I am? Or will I reject him and everything he loves, and become the person this world wants me to be?

And if I have lost my mind, what then?

What if everything I know is wrong?

No. Stop.

I am not losing my mind.

There were drugs in my blood from last night and bruises on my body. My key opened the door to that house that wasn’t mine. I don’t have a brain tumor. There’s a mark from a wedding band on my finger. I am in this hospital room right now, and all of this is really happening.

I am not allowed to think I’m crazy.

I'm only allowed to solve this problem.

—

When the elevator doors open to the hospital lobby, I push past two men in cheap suits and wet coats. They look like cops, and when they step into the elevator and we make eye contact, I wonder if they’re coming to see me.

I walk past a waiting area, toward the automatic doors. Since I wasn’t in a locked ward, getting out was easier than I thought. I just got dressed, waited for the hall to be empty, and walked past the nurses’ station without anyone even noticing.

As I get to the exit, I keep expecting alarms to go off, someone to call my name, guards to run after me through the lobby.

Soon I’m outside in the rain, and it feels like early evening, the busy traffic suggesting it’s around six p.m.

I rush down the steps, reach the sidewalk, and don’t slow down until I’m on the next block.

I look back.

No one is following me, as far as I can see.

Just a lot of umbrellas.

I’m getting wet.

I have no idea where I’m going.

At a bank, I step off the sidewalk and take shelter under the entrance roof. Leaning against a limestone column, I watch people walk by as rain pounds the pavement.

I dig my money clip out of my pants. Last night’s cab ride took a big chunk out of my small savings. I’m down to $182, and my credit cards are useless.

Going home is impossible, but there’s a cheap hotel in my neighborhood a few blocks from my brownstone, and it’s just dirty enough to make me think I might be able to pay for a room there.

I step back out into the rain.

It’s getting darker every minute.

Colder.

Without a coat or jacket, I’m soaked to the bone within two blocks.

The Days Inn should be across the street from Village Tap. But it isn’t. The awning is the wrong color, and the whole front of the building looks strangely fancy. These are high-end apartments. I even see a doorman under an umbrella, trying to get a taxi for a woman in a black coat.

Am I on the right street?

I look back at my corner bar.

VILLAGE TAP should be shining neon in the front window, but instead there’s a heavy wooden sign with brass letters hanging from a pole that’s swinging over the entrance, creaking in the wind.

I keep walking, faster now, the rain hitting my eyes.

Past—

Noisy bars.

Restaurants getting ready for dinner—shiny wineglasses and silverware quickly set on white tablecloths as servers learn the specials.

A coffee shop I don’t know, filled with the sound of an espresso machine grinding beans.

Daniela’s and my favorite Italian place looking just as it should, reminding me I haven’t eaten in almost a day.

But I keep walking.

Until my socks are soaked.

Until I'm shaking hard.

Until night falls and I’m standing outside a three-story hotel with bars on the windows and a huge, annoying sign above the entrance:

HOTEL ROYALE

I step inside, leaving a puddle on the cracked checkerboard floor.

It isn’t what I expected. Not sleazy or dirty in a shocking way. Just forgotten. Past its best days. Like my great-grandparents’ living room in their shaky Iowa farmhouse. As if the worn furniture has been here forever, stuck in time while the world moved forward. The air smells old, and big-band music plays softly from somewhere hidden. Something from the 1940s.

At the front desk, the old clerk in a tuxedo doesn’t even blink at how wet I am. He just takes $95 in wet money and gives me a key to a room on the third floor.

The elevator is small, and I stare at my weird reflection in the bronze doors as it struggles to the third floor, making noise and moving like a heavy man climbing stairs.

Down a dim hallway, barely wide enough for two people to walk side by side, I find my room number and struggle to unlock the old lock with the key.

It isn’t much.

A single bed with a thin metal frame and a bumpy mattress.

A bathroom the size of a closet.

A dresser.

An old television.

And a chair next to a window, where something glows on the other side of the glass.

Walking around the end of the bed, I pull back the curtain and look outside, finding myself level with the top of the hotel sign and close enough to see the rain falling through the green light.

Down on the sidewalk, I see a man leaning against a lamppost, smoke rising into the rain, the ash of his cigarette glowing and fading in the darkness under his hat.

Is he waiting there for me?

Maybe I’m just being paranoid, but I go to the door and check the lock and hook the chain.

Then I kick off my shoes, take off my clothes, and dry myself with the bathroom’s only towel.

The best thing about the room is the old cast-iron radiator under the window. I turn it up high and hold my hands in the waves of heat.

I hang my wet clothes over the chair and push it close to the radiator.

In the bedside table drawer, I find a Gideon Bible and a large Chicago Metro phone book.

Stretching out on the creaky bed, I flip to the D’s and start looking for my last name.

I quickly find my listing.

Jason A. Dessen.

Right address.

Right number.

I pick up the phone on the bedside table and call my home phone.

It rings four times, then I hear my voice: “Hi, you’ve reached Jason, but not really, because I can’t take your call. This is a recording. You know what to do.”

I hang up before the beep.

That isn’t our home voicemail message.

I feel like I'm going crazy again, like I'm about to curl up in a ball and break into a million pieces.

But I stop it, and go back to my new rule.

I'm not allowed to think I'm crazy.

I'm only allowed to solve this problem.

Experimental physics—all of science—is about solving problems. But you can’t solve them all at once. There’s always a bigger question—the main goal. But if you worry too much about how big it is, you lose focus.

The key is to start small. Focus on problems you can solve. Find some solid ground to stand on. And after you’ve worked hard, and if you’re lucky, the big question becomes answerable. Like slowly stepping back from a photomontage to see the whole picture.

I have to ignore the fear, the paranoia, the terror, and just work on this problem like I’m in a lab—one small question at a time.

Find solid footing.

The main question bothering me right now: What's become of me? There's no answer to that. Not yet. I have some ideas, of course, but ideas lead to prejudice, and prejudice doesn't lead to truth.

Why weren't Daniela and Charlie at our place last night? Why did it seem like I live by myself?

No, that's still too broad, too complicated. Focus the information.

Where are Daniela and Charlie?

Better, but make it even simpler. Daniela will know where my son is.

So, this is where I'll begin: Where is Daniela?

The drawings I saw last night on the walls of the house that isn't mine—Daniela Vargas made them. She signed them with her maiden name. Why?

I hold up my ring finger to the neon light from the window.

The mark from my wedding ring is gone.

Was it ever there?

I pull a loose thread from the curtain and tie it around my ring finger to feel connected to the world and my life.

Then I go back to the phone book, flipping to the V's and stopping at the only Daniela Vargas listed. I tear out the page and dial her number.

Hearing her voice on the recording touches me, even though the message makes me uneasy.

"You've reached Daniela. I'm away painting. Leave a message. Ciao."

—

In an hour, my clothes are warm and almost dry. I wash, dress, and take the stairs to the lobby.

Outside, the wind is blowing, but the rain has stopped.

The smoking man by the streetlamp is gone.

I feel faint from hunger.

I pass six restaurants before finding one I can afford—a bright, dirty pizza place selling huge, deep-dish slices. There’s nowhere to sit inside, so I stand on the sidewalk, stuffing my face and wondering if this pizza is as amazing as I think, or if I’m just too hungry to judge.

Daniela’s address is in Bucktown. I have about $75 left, so I could take a cab, but I want to walk.

The number of people and cars suggests it’s Friday night, and the air feels lively.

I head east to find my wife.

—

Daniela’s building is yellow brick with ivy growing on the front that’s turning red with the recent cold. The buzzer system is an old brass panel, and I find her maiden name near the bottom of the first column.

I press the buzzer three times, but she doesn’t answer.

Through the tall windowpanes around the door, I see a woman in an evening gown and coat, her heels clicking down the hallway as she comes closer. I step away from the window and turn as the door opens.

She’s on her phone, and the smell of alcohol tells me she’s already started celebrating the night. She doesn’t see me as she rushes down the steps.

I grab the door before it closes and take the stairs to the fourth floor.

Daniela’s door is at the end of the hall.

I knock and wait.

No answer.

I head back down to the lobby, wondering if I should just wait here for her to come back. But what if she’s away? What would she think if she returned to her apartment and found me hanging around outside like some stalker?

As I walk toward the entrance, I see a bulletin board full of flyers for things like art shows, book readings, and poetry nights.

The biggest notice in the middle of the board grabs my attention. It’s a poster for a show by Daniela Vargas at a gallery called Oomph.

I stop and look for the opening date.

Friday, October 2.

Tonight.

—

Back down on the street, it’s raining again.

I flag a cab.

The gallery is a dozen blocks away, and I feel my nerves stretch tight as we crawl down Damen Avenue, stuck in a sea of cabs at rush hour.

I get out of the cab and join the crowd of hipsters walking through the freezing drizzle.

Oomph is an old packing plant that’s been turned into an art gallery, and the line to get in stretches halfway down the block.

Forty-five miserable, shivering minutes later, I’m finally out of the rain, paying my $15 to get in, and being ushered with a group of ten people into a small room with Daniela’s full name in huge, graffiti-style letters on the wall all around us.

In our fifteen years together, I’ve been to many shows and openings with Daniela, but I’ve never seen anything like this.

A thin, bearded man comes out of a hidden door in the wall.

The lights go down.

He says, “I’m Steve Konkoly, the producer of what you’re about to see.” He tears a plastic bag off a dispenser by the door. “Phones go in the bag. You get them back on the other side.”

The bag of gathering phones is passed around.

“A quick note about what’s coming. The artist wants you to stop thinking so much and try to feel her art. Welcome to ‘Entanglement.’ ”

Konkoly takes the bag of phones and opens the door.

I’m the last to enter.

For a moment, our group is squeezed in a dark, small space that goes completely black as the loud slam of the door shows a huge, warehouse-like room.

I look up as lights appear above us.

Stars.

They look very real, each one glowing softly.

Some are near, some are far, and now and then one shoots across the darkness.

I see what’s coming.

Someone in our group whispers, “Oh my God.”

It’s a maze made of Plexiglas, which looks like it goes on forever under the starry sky.

Waves of light move through the panels.

Our group slowly moves ahead.

There are five ways into the maze, and I’m standing where they all meet, watching the others go their own ways.

A quiet sound that’s been there the whole time gets my attention—it’s more like TV static than music, a hiss over a long, deep note.

I pick a path, and as I enter the maze, the clear walls disappear.

The Plexiglas glows with bright light, even under my feet.

After a minute, some of the panels start showing repeating images.

Birth—baby crying, mother crying with happiness.

A man about to die, struggling at the end of a rope.

A snowstorm.

The ocean.

A desert going by.

I keep going.

Into paths that lead nowhere.

Around turns where I can't see.

The images coming more often, faster and faster.

The wrecked remains of a car crash.

A couple having intense sex.

The cross.

The Buddha.

The pentagram.

The peace sign.

A nuclear explosion.

The lights go out.

The stars return.

I can see through the Plexiglas again, but now there’s a digital filter on the clear surface—like static, swarming bugs, and falling snow.

It makes the others in the maze look like shadows moving through a huge wasteland.

And despite the confusion and fear of the last twenty-four hours, or perhaps precisely because of all I’ve experienced, what I’m seeing now breaks through and hits me hard.

While I can see the others in the labyrinth, it doesn’t feel like we’re in the same room, or even the same space.

They seem far away and lost in their own paths.

I’m struck for a moment by the overwhelming sense of loss.

Not sadness or pain, but something more basic.

A realization and the terror that comes with it—terror of the vast indifference around us.

I don’t know if that’s what Daniela intended her installation to mean, but it’s certainly what it means to me.

We’re all just wandering through the emptiness of our lives, giving value to things that have none, when all that we love and hate, all we believe in and fight for and kill for and die for is as meaningless as images projected onto Plexiglas.

At the labyrinth’s exit, there’s one last loop— a man and a woman each hold the tiny hand of their child as they run together up a grassy hill under a clear, blue sky —with the following words slowly appearing on the panel—

Nothing exists.

All is a dream.

God—man—the world—the sun, the moon, the wilderness of stars—a dream, all a dream; they have no existence.

Nothing exists save empty space—and you….

And you are not you—you have no body, no blood, no bones, you are but a thought.

MARK TWAIN

I step into another small room, where the rest of my group huddles around the plastic bag, getting their phones.

Onward, into a large, bright gallery with shiny wood floors, art on the walls, a violin trio…and a woman in a beautiful black dress, standing on a platform, speaking to the crowd.

It takes me five seconds to realize this is Daniela.

She’s glowing, holding a glass of red wine and gesturing with her other hand.

“—just the most amazing night, and I’m so grateful to all of you for coming to support my new project. It means so much.”

Daniela raises her wineglass.

“¡Salud!”

The crowd responds, and as everyone drinks, I move toward her.

Up close, she’s electric, so full of life that I have to stop myself from calling out to her. This is Daniela with the energy of our first meeting fifteen years ago, before life—the ordinary, the joy, the sadness, the give-and-take—changed her into the woman who shares my bed: amazing mother, amazing wife, but always fighting the thoughts of what could have been.

My Daniela carries a weight and a distance in her eyes that sometimes scare me.

This Daniela is so happy she's practically floating.

I’m now standing less than ten feet away, my heart thumping, wondering if she’ll spot me, and then—

Eye contact.

Her eyes widen and her mouth opens, and I can’t tell if she’s horrified or delighted or just surprised to see my face.

She pushes through the crowd, throws her arms around my neck, and pulls me in tight with, “Oh my God, I can’t believe you came. Is everything all right? I’d heard you left the country for a while or were missing or something.”

I’m not sure how to respond to that, so I just say, “Well, here I am.”

Daniela hasn’t worn perfume in years, but she’s wearing it tonight, and she smells like Daniela without me, like Daniela before our separate scents merged into us.

I don’t want to let go—I need her touch—but she pulls away.

I ask, “Where’s Charlie?”

“Who?”

“Charlie.”

“Who are you talking about?”

Something twists inside me.

“Jason?”

She doesn’t know who our son is.

Do we even have a son?

Does Charlie exist?

Of course, he exists. I was there when he was born. I held him right after he came squirming and crying into the world.

“Yeah. I just came through the maze.”

“What did you think?”

“It almost made me cry.”

God yes.

The gallery has gone all out—waiters in tuxedos carrying trays of appetizers and Champagne, and a cash bar on the other side of the room under three Daniela self-portraits.

He smiles mysteriously, and for three seconds, I want to kill him.

—

At one in the morning, I’m sitting on a sofa at Daniela’s place, watching as she says goodbye to the last of her guests. The last few hours have been hard—trying to have sensible conversations with Daniela’s art friends while waiting to get a real moment alone with her. I sip the last of my scotch from a heavy glass, not drunk but pretty buzzed, the alcohol keeping my mind separate from this mess I’ve gotten into.

This crazy thing that’s supposed to be my life.

I wonder if Daniela wants me to go. If I'm that clueless guest who doesn't realize I've stayed too long.

She shuts the door and puts on the chain lock.

That seems to get their attention.

He sits up straight in the chair.

"What do you mean?" Daniela asks.

"Do you trust me?"

She reaches over and touches my hand. Like a shock. "Of course, honey."

Ryan says, "Even when we haven't gotten along, I've always respected that you're a good person."

Daniela looks worried. "Is everything okay?"

But I'm going to.

And why would anyone do that? He’s just a regular guy. Why would anyone want to mess with him so much?”

“I’m not insane.”

It gets very quiet in the loft.

“It did.”

“Can you tell me about the talk?”

“Please, Daniela.”

There’s a long pause as she looks into my eyes, maybe to see if I’m serious.

She finally says, “It was spring, I think. We hadn’t seen each other for some time, and we hadn’t really talked since we split up all those years ago. I had been following your success, of course. I was always so proud of you.

And I realized that you hadn’t come by to catch up. You had come to say goodbye. Then you told me that our life is all about choices and that you had messed some of them up, but none as badly as with me. You said you were sorry for everything. It was very emotional. You left, and I didn’t hear from you or see you again until tonight. Now I have a question for you.”

One of the things I love most about Daniela is her honesty. She has a direct line from her heart to her mouth. No filter, no editing. She says what she feels, without any deceit or trickery. She plays no games.

So when I look into Daniela’s eyes and see that she’s completely sincere, it almost breaks me.

“It doesn’t matter,” I say.

“Obviously, it does. We haven’t seen each other in a year and a half and that’s the first thing you ask me?”

I finish my drink, chewing the last melting ice.

Her face turns pale.

“Wait a minute,” Ryan says, his voice tight. “I thought we were just joking around. What’s going on? Is this a joke?” He looks at Daniela, then at me.

“No, it’s not.”

Daniela says, “We don’t have a son, and you know that. We haven’t been together in fifteen years. You know this, Jason. You know this.”

I guess I could try to convince her now. I know so much about her—childhood secrets she only told me in the last five years of our marriage. But I’m afraid these “secrets” would backfire. That she wouldn’t see them as proof, but as tricks. Like a magic show. I think the best way to persuade her I’m telling the truth is to be honest and sincere.

I say, “Here’s what I know, Daniela. You and I live in my house in Logan Square. We have a fourteen-year-old son named Charlie. I’m an okay professor at Lakemont. You’re a wonderful wife and mother who gave up her art career to be at home. And you, Ryan, you’re a famous brain scientist. You won the Pavia Prize. You’ve given talks all over the world. And I know this sounds completely insane, but I don’t have a brain tumor, no one is playing a trick on me, and I haven’t gone crazy.”

Ryan laughs, but he seems uneasy. “Let’s say, just for the sake of argument, that everything you just said is true. Or at least that you think it is. The thing we don’t know is what you’ve been working on these last few years. This secret project. What can you tell us about it?”

“Nothing.”

Ryan gets to his feet with effort.

“You’re leaving?” Daniela asks.

“It’s late. I’m done.”

I say, “Ryan, it’s not that I don’t want to tell you. I can’t tell you. I don’t remember it. I teach physics. I woke up in this lab, and everyone thought I belonged, but I don’t.”

Ryan grabs his hat and walks to the door.

Halfway out, he turns to me and says, “You’re not okay. Let me take you to the hospital.”

“I already went. I’m not going back.”

He looks at Daniela. “Do you want him to go?”

She looks at me, thinking—I guess—about whether she wants to be alone with a crazy person. What if she decides I’m not trustworthy?

She finally shakes her head, says, “It’s okay.”

“Ryan,” I say. “What did you make for me?”

He just glares at me, and for a moment I think he’s going to answer. The tension leaves his face, as if he’s trying to decide if I’m crazy or just a stoned jerk.

And suddenly, he makes up his mind.

He hardens again.

He says with no warmth in his voice, “Good night, Daniela.”

Then turns.

Leaves.

Slams the door behind him.

—

Daniela walks into the guest room wearing yoga pants and a tank top and carrying a cup of tea.

I’ve had a shower.

I don’t feel any better, but at least I’m clean, the hospital smell of sickness and bleach gone.

Sitting on the edge of the mattress, she hands me the mug.

“Chamomile.”

I hold the warm mug, say, “You didn’t have to do this. I have a place I can go.”

“You’re staying here with me. That’s it.”

She crawls over my legs and sits beside me, her back against the headboard.

I sip the tea.

It’s warm, calming, a little sweet.

Daniela looks over.

“When you went to the hospital, what did they think was wrong with you?”

“They didn’t know. They wanted to admit me.”

“To a mental hospital?”

“Yeah.”

“And you wouldn’t agree to that?”

“No, I left.”

“So it would have been against your will.”

“That’s right.”

“Are you sure that’s not the best thing now, Jason? I mean, what would you think if someone said the things to you that you’re saying to me?”

“I’d think he was crazy. But I’d be wrong.”

“Then tell me,” she says. “What do you think is happening to you?”

“I’m not completely sure.”

“But you’re a scientist. You have a theory.”

“I don’t know enough yet.”

“What do you feel?”

I sip the chamomile tea, enjoying the warmth as it goes down.

“We all live unaware that we’re part of a much bigger and stranger world than we realize.”

She takes my hand, and even though she isn’t the Daniela I know, I can’t hide how much I love this woman, even here and now, in this bed, in this wrong world.

I look at her, her Spanish eyes bright and focused. It takes all my strength to not touch her.

“Are you afraid?” she asks.

I think back to the man who held me at gunpoint. To that lab. To the team that followed me back to my brownstone and tried to catch me. I think of the man smoking a cigarette under my hotel room window. Besides all the things about my identity and this reality that don’t make sense, there are real people out there, beyond these walls, who want to find me.

Who have hurt me before and might want to hurt me again.

A serious thought hits me—could they find me here? Have I put Daniela in danger?

No.

If she isn’t my wife, if she’s just a girlfriend from fifteen years ago, why would anyone care about her?

“Jason?” And she asks again, “Are you afraid?”

“Very.”

She reaches up, gently touches my face, says, “Bruises.”

“I don’t know how I got them.”

“Tell me about him.”

“Who?”

“Charlie.”

“This must be strange for you.”

“I can’t say it isn’t.”

“Well, I told you, he’s fourteen. Almost fifteen. His birthday is October twenty-first, and he was born early at Chicago Mercy. Just one pound, fifteen ounces. He needed a lot of help his first year, but he was a fighter. Now he’s healthy and as tall as I am.”

Tears fill her eyes.

“He has dark hair like you and a great sense of humor. Gets mostly Bs in school. Very creative, like his mom. He likes Japanese comics and skateboards. Loves to draw these wild landscapes. I think he might have your talent for it.”

“Stop it.”

“What?”

She closes her eyes, and tears come out and run down her cheeks.

“We don’t have a son.”

“You swear you don’t remember him?” I ask. “This isn’t a joke? If you tell me now, I won’t—”

“Jason, we broke up fifteen years ago. Actually, you broke up with me.”

“That’s not true.”

“I told you I was pregnant the day before. You needed time to think. You came to my apartment and said it was the hardest choice you’d ever made, but you were busy with your research, the research that would win you that big award. You said the next year would be spent in a lab and that I deserved better. That our child deserved better.”

I say, “That’s not how it happened. I told you it wouldn’t be easy, but we’d make it work. We got married. You had Charlie. I lost my funding. You stopped painting. I became a professor. You became a full-time mother.”

“And here we are tonight. Not married. No children. You just came from the opening of the art show that’s going to make me famous, and you did win that prize. I don’t know what you’re thinking. Maybe you have mixed-up memories, but I know what’s real.”

I stare at the steam rising from the tea.

“Do you think I’m crazy?” I ask.

“I don’t know, but you’re not doing well.”

And she looks at me with the kindness that has always been her way.

I touch the thread tied around my finger like a charm.

I say, “Look, maybe you believe me, maybe you don’t, but I need you to know that I do. I would never lie to you.”

This might be the strangest moment I’ve had since waking up in that lab—sitting in bed in the guest room of the apartment of the woman who is my wife but isn’t, talking about the son we never had, about the life that wasn’t ours.

—

I wake up alone in bed in the middle of the night, my heart racing, the darkness swirling, my mouth horribly dry.

For a whole scary minute, I don’t know where I am.

This isn’t from the alcohol or the pot.

It’s a much worse kind of confusion.

I pull the covers tight around me, but I can’t stop shaking, and my whole body aches more and more, my legs jumpy, my head pounding.

The next time I wake up, the room is bright with day, and Daniela is standing over me, looking worried.

“You’re really hot, Jason. I should take you to the hospital.”

“I’ll be fine.”

“You don’t look fine.” She puts a cold washcloth on my forehead. “How’s that?” she asks.

“Good, but you don’t have to do this. I’ll get a cab back to my hotel.”

“Just try to leave.”

—

In the early afternoon, my fever goes away.

Daniela makes me chicken noodle soup from scratch, and I eat sitting up in bed while she sits in a chair in the corner, her eyes holding a distant look I know too well.

She’s lost in thought, thinking something over, and doesn’t see that I’m watching her. I don’t mean to stare, but I can’t look away. She is still completely Daniela, except—

Her hair is shorter.

She’s in better shape.

She’s wearing makeup, and her clothes—jeans and a tight T-shirt—make her look much younger than thirty-nine.

“Am I happy?” she asks.

“What do you mean?”

“In our life that you say we share…am I happy?”

“I thought you didn’t want to talk about this.”

“I couldn’t sleep last night. It’s all I could think about.”

“I think you’re happy.”

“Even without my art?”

“You miss it, I’m sure. You see old friends doing well, and I know you’re happy for them, but I also know it hurts a little. Just like it does for me. It's something we share.”

“You mean we’re both failures.”

“We are not failures.”

“Are we happy? Together, I mean.”

I set the bowl of soup aside.

“Yeah. We’ve had some hard times, like any marriage, but we have a son, a home, a family. You’re my best friend.”

She looks straight at me and asks with a sly smile, “How’s our sex life?”

I just laugh.

She says, “Oh God, did I actually make you blush?”

“You did.”

“But you didn’t answer my question.”

“I didn’t, did I?”

“What’s wrong, don’t you like it?”

She’s flirting now.

“No, it’s great. You’re just making me blush.”

She gets up and walks over to the bed.

Sits on the edge of the mattress and looks at me with those huge, deep eyes.

“What are you thinking?” I ask.

She shakes her head. “That if you’re not crazy or lying, then we just had the weirdest talk ever.”

I sit in bed watching the daylight fade over Chicago.

Whatever storm brought the rain last night has passed, and now the sky is clear, the trees have changed color, and the light is stunning as evening approaches—bright and golden—a feeling I can only describe as loss.

Robert Frost's gold that cannot stay.

Out in the kitchen, pots are banging, cabinets are opening and closing, and the smell of cooking meat drifts down the hall into the guest room with a scent that seems strangely familiar.

I get out of bed, steady on my feet for the first time all day, and go to the kitchen.

Bach is playing, red wine is open, and Daniela stands at the island, chopping an onion on the countertop wearing an apron and swimming goggles.

“Smells amazing,” I say.

“Would you mind stirring it?”

I walk to the stove and lift the lid off a large pot.

The steam on my face brings me home.

“How are you feeling?” she asks.

“Like a different man.”

“So…better?”

“Much.”

It’s a typical Spanish meal—a bean stew with different local beans and meats. Chorizo, pancetta, black sausage. Daniela makes it once or twice a year, usually on my birthday, or when it snows on a weekend and we want to drink wine and cook together all day.

I stir the stew, put the lid back on.

Daniela says, “It’s a bean stew from—”

It comes out before I can stop it: “Your mother’s recipe. Actually— her grandmother’s.”

Daniela stops cutting.

She looks back at me.

“Give me something to do,” I say.

“What else do you know about me?”

“Look, from where I’m standing, we’ve been together fifteen years. So I know almost everything.”

“And from mine, it was only two and a half months, and that was a lifetime ago. Yet you know this recipe was passed down through my family for generations.”

For a moment, it gets eerily quiet in the kitchen.

Like the air between us is charged, buzzing just beyond what we can sense.

She finally says, “If you want to help, I’m getting the stew toppings ready. I could tell you what they are, but you probably already know.”

“Grated cheddar, cilantro, and sour cream?”

She smiles slightly and raises an eyebrow. “Like I said, you already know.”

We have dinner at the table by the big window, candlelight reflecting in the glass, city lights glowing beyond—our own constellation.

The food is amazing, Daniela looks beautiful in the warm light, and I feel steady for the first time since I left that lab.

Dinner ends—our bowls are empty, the second bottle of wine finished—she reaches across the glass table and touches my hand.

“I don’t know what’s happening to you, Jason, but I’m happy you came to me.”

I want to kiss her.

She helped me when I was lost.

When nothing made sense anymore.

But I don’t kiss her. I just squeeze her hand and say, “You don’t know how much you’ve done for me.”

We clear the table, fill the dishwasher, and wash the rest of the dishes in the sink.

I wash. She dries and puts away. Like an old married couple.

Out of the blue, I say, “Ryan Holder, huh?”

She stops wiping the inside of the big pot and looks at me.

“Do you have something to say about that?”

“No, it’s just—”

“What? He was your roommate, your friend. You don’t like it?”

“He always liked you.”

“Are you jealous?”

“Of course.”

“Oh, come on. He’s a handsome man.”

She goes back to her drying.

“So how serious is it?” I ask.

“We’ve gone out a few times. Nobody’s left their toothbrushes at anyone’s place yet.”

“Well, I think he’d like to. He seems really taken with you.”

Daniela smirks. “How could he not be? I’m amazing.”

—

I lie in bed in the guest room with the window open a bit so the city noise can lull me to sleep like a sound machine.

Staring out the tall window, I watch the sleeping city.

Last night, I wanted to answer a simple question: Where is Daniela?

And I found her—a successful artist, living alone.

We’ve never been married, never had a son.

Unless I’m being fooled in a very complicated way, what Daniela is seems to prove what the last two days have been leading to….

This is not my world.

Even as I think those words, I’m not sure what they mean, or how to truly understand them.

So I say it again.

I test it out.

See if it feels right.

This is not my world.

—

A soft knock at my door wakes me up suddenly.

“Come in.”

Daniela enters, climbs into bed beside me.

I sit up, ask, “Everything okay?”

“I can’t sleep.”

“What’s wrong?”

She kisses me, and it isn’t like kissing my wife after fifteen years, it’s like kissing her for the first time fifteen years ago.

Just energy and impact.

As I’m on top of her, my hands running up her thighs, pushing the satin nightgown over her bare hips, I stop.

She says, breathless, “Why are you stopping?”

And I almost say, I can’t do this, you’re not my wife, but that’s not even true.

This is Daniela, the only person in this crazy world who has helped me, and, yes, maybe I am trying to explain it, but I’m so confused, scared, and hopeless that I don’t just want this, I need it, and I think she does too.

I stare into her eyes, dark and shining in the light coming through the window.

Eyes you can fall into and never stop falling.

She isn’t my son’s mother, she isn’t my wife, we haven’t built a life together, but I love her anyway, and not just the idea of Daniela that exists in my mind, in my past. I love the real woman under me in this bed right now, wherever this is, because it’s the same body—same eyes, same voice, same smell, same taste….

It isn’t like married people making love.

We have clumsy, grabbing, back-of-the-car, careless, protons-smashing-together sex.

—

Soon after, sweaty and shaky, we lie together and look out at the lights of our city.

Daniela’s heart is pounding in her chest, and I can feel it beating against my side, slowing down now.

Slower.

Slower.

“Everything okay?” she whispers. “I can hear you thinking hard.”

“I don’t know what I would’ve done if I hadn’t met you.”

“Well, you did. And whatever’s going on, I’m here for you. You know that, right?”

She runs her fingers across my hands.

They stop at the piece of thread tied around my ring finger.

“What’s this?” she asks.

“Proof,” I say.

“Proof?”

“That I’m not losing it.”

It quiets down again.

I don’t know what time it is, but it’s after two in the morning for sure.

The bars will be closed now.

The streets are as quiet and calm as they get, except when it snows.

The air coming through the crack in the window is the coldest it’s been this year.

It crawls across our sweaty skin.

“I have to go home,” I say.

“Your place in Logan Square?”

“Yeah.”

“Why?”

“I guess I have a home office. I want to check my computer, see what I’ve been doing. Maybe I’ll find papers, notes, something to explain what’s happening to me.”

“I can drive you over first thing in the morning.”

“You probably shouldn’t.”

“Why?”

“It might be dangerous.”

“Why would it—”

A loud bang shakes the door in the living room, like someone is pounding on it. It sounds like how I think cops knock.

I ask, “Who the hell is that this late?”

Daniela gets out of bed and walks out of the room naked.

It takes me a minute to find my boxer shorts in the tangled comforter, and by the time I put them on, Daniela is coming from her bedroom in a terrycloth robe.

We go into the living room.

The banging on the door keeps up as Daniela gets closer.

“Don’t open it,” I whisper.

“Duh.”

She looks through the peephole, and the phone rings.

We both jump.

Daniela walks across the living room to the cordless phone on the coffee table.

I peek through the peephole and see a man in the hall with his back to the door.

He’s on a cell phone.

Daniela answers, “Hello?”

The man is dressed in black—Doc Martens, jeans, a leather jacket.

Daniela says into the phone, “Who is this?”

I move toward her and point to the door, mouthing, It’s him?

She nods.

“What does he want?”

She points at me.

Now I can hear the man’s voice coming both through the door and through the speaker on her cordless phone.

She says into the phone, “I don’t know what you’re talking about. I’m here alone, and I live alone, and I’m not letting a strange man into my home at two in the—”

The door bursts open, the chain snaps and flies across the room, and the man steps in, raising a pistol with a long black tube attached to the barrel.

He aims it at both of us, and as he kicks the door closed, I smell old and fresh cigarette smoke drifting into the loft.

“You’re here for me,” I say. “She has nothing to do with any of this.”

He’s an inch or two shorter than I am, but stronger. His head is shaved, and his eyes are gray and not so much cold as distant, as if he doesn’t see me as a person, but as information. Just data. The way a machine might.

My mouth has gone dry.

There’s a strange gap between what’s happening and how I understand it. A disconnect. A delay. I should do something, say something, but I feel frozen by how suddenly the man appeared.

“I’ll go with you,” I say. “Just—”

He aims slightly away from me and up.

Daniela says, “Wait, no—”

She’s stopped by a burst of fire and a quiet shot, not quite as loud as a normal gunshot.

A fine, red mist blinds me for a moment, and Daniela sits on the sofa, a hole right in the middle of her big, dark eyes.

I start toward her, screaming, but every bit of my body locks up, muscles clenching uncontrollably with terrible pain, and I crash down through the coffee table, shaking and grunting in broken glass and telling myself this isn’t real.

The smoking man lifts my useless arms behind my back and fastens my wrists together in a cross shape with a zip tie.

Then I hear something ripping.

He sticks a piece of duct tape over my mouth and sits behind me in the leather chair.

I’m screaming through the tape, begging for this not to be happening, but it is, and I can’t do anything about it.

I hear the man’s voice behind me—calm and higher than I expected.

“Hey, I’m here…No, why don’t you come around back…Exactly. Where the recycling and Dumpsters are. The back gate and rear door to the building are both open…Two should be fine. We’re in pretty good shape up here, but you know, let’s not stay long…Yep…Yep…Okay, sounds good.”

The awful feeling from what I think was a Taser is finally fading, but I’m too weak to move.

All I can see is the bottom half of Daniela’s legs. I watch blood run down her right ankle, across her foot, between her toes, and start to pool on the floor.

I hear the man’s phone buzz.

He answers, “Hey, baby…I know, I just didn’t want to wake you…Yeah, something came up…I don’t know, might be morning. How about I take you to breakfast at the Golden Apple when I’m done?” He laughs. “Okay. Love you too. Sweet dreams.”

My eyes fill with tears.

I shout through the tape, shout until my throat burns, hoping he’ll shoot me or knock me out, anything to stop the awful pain of this moment.

But it doesn’t seem to bother him at all.

He just sits there quietly, letting me rage and scream.

Chapter 6

Daniela sits in the bleachers under the scoreboard, above the ivy-covered outfield wall. It’s Saturday afternoon, the last home game of the regular season, and she’s with Jason and Charlie, watching the Cubs get beat badly in their sold-out ballpark.

The warm autumn day is cloudless.

Windless.

Timeless.

The air smells of—

Roasted peanuts.

Popcorn.

Plastic cups filled to the brim with beer.

Daniela finds the roar of the crowd strangely comforting, and they’re far enough back from home plate to notice a delay between swing and bat-crack—speed of light versus speed of sound—when a player sends a pitch flying beyond the wall.

They used to come to games when Charlie was a boy, but it’s been ages since they last came to Wrigley Field. When Jason suggested it yesterday, she didn’t think Charlie would want to, but it must be stirring up some old memories for him, because he actually wanted to come, and now he seems relaxed and happy. They’re all happy, a trio of near-perfect contentment in the sun, eating Chicago-style hot dogs, watching the players run around on the bright grass.

As Daniela sits between the two most important men in her life, a little tipsy from her lukewarm beer, it strikes her that this afternoon feels different. She doesn't know if it's Charlie or Jason or her. Charlie is in the moment, not checking his phone all the time. And Jason looks as happy as she’s seen him in years. Weightless is the word that comes to mind. His smile seems wider, brighter, more freely given.

And he can’t keep his hands off her.

Then again, maybe the difference is her.

Maybe it’s this beer and the clear autumn light and the shared energy of the crowd.

Which is to say maybe it’s just being alive at a baseball game on a fall day in her city.

—

Charlie has plans after the game, so they drop him at a friend’s house in Logan Square, stop at the brownstone to change clothes, and then head out into the evening, just the two of them—going downtown, no plans, no place in mind.

A Saturday-night walkabout.

Driving in slow evening traffic down Lakeshore Drive, Daniela looks across the center console of the old Suburban, says, “I think I know what I want to do first.”

Thirty minutes later, they’re in a gondola on a Ferris wheel covered in lights.

Rising slowly above the sights of Navy Pier, Daniela watches the beautiful skyline of their city as Jason holds her close.

At the top of their ride—one hundred and fifty feet above the carnival—Daniela feels Jason touch her chin and turn her face to him.

They have the gondola all to themselves.

Even up here, the night air smells sweet with funnel cakes and cotton candy.

The laughter of children riding the carousel.

A woman screaming with joy at getting a hole-in-one on the miniature golf course below.

Jason’s strong feelings cut through all of it.

When he kisses her, she can feel his heart through his jacket, pounding in his chest.

—

They have dinner in the city at a fancy restaurant they can't really afford and spend the whole time talking like they haven’t talked in years.

Not about people or old times, but ideas.

They finish a bottle of Tempranillo.

Order another.

Thinking they might stay in the city for the night.

It’s been a long time since Daniela has seen her husband this excited, this confident.

He’s full of energy, in love with his life again.

Partway through their second bottle of wine, he sees her looking out the window and asks, “What are you thinking about?”

“That’s a loaded question.”

“I know.”

“I’m thinking about you.”

“What about me?”

“It feels like you’re trying to sleep with me.” She laughs. “I mean, it feels like you’re trying when you don’t need to. We’re like an old married couple, and I feel like you’re, um…”

“Romancing you?”

“Exactly. Don’t get me wrong, I’m not complaining. Not at all. It’s amazing. I guess I just don’t know why. Are you okay? Is something wrong that you’re not telling me?”

“I’m fine.”

“So this is all because you almost got hit by a cab two nights ago?”

He says, “I don’t know if it was my life flashing before my eyes or what, but when I came home, everything felt different. More real. Especially you. Even now, it’s like I’m seeing you for the first time, and I have this nervous feeling in my stomach. I think about you all the time. I think about all the choices we made that led to this moment. Us sitting here together at this beautiful table. Then I think of all the things that could have stopped this moment from happening, and it all feels, I don’t know…”

“What?”

“So fragile.” Now he thinks for a moment. He says finally, “It’s scary when you think that every thought we have, every choice we could make, creates a new world. After the baseball game today, we went to Navy Pier and then came here for dinner, right? But that’s just one thing that could have happened. In another world, instead of the pier, we went to the symphony. In one, we stayed home. In another, we got into a bad accident on Lakeshore Drive and didn't make it anywhere.”

“But those other realities aren’t really there.”

“Actually, they’re just as real as what you and I are going through right now.”

“How can that be?”

“It’s a mystery. But there are hints. Most space scientists think that the force that keeps stars and galaxies together—the thing that makes our whole universe work —comes from a theoretical substance we can’t measure or see directly. Something they call dark matter. And this dark matter makes up most of the universe we know.”

“But what is it, really?”

“No one’s really sure. Physicists have been trying to come up with new ideas to explain where it comes from and what it is. We know it has gravity, like normal matter, but it must be made of something completely new.”

“A new kind of matter.”

“Exactly. Some string theorists think it might be a hint to the existence of the multiverse.”

She looks thoughtful for a moment, then asks, “So all these other realities…where are they?”

“Imagine you’re a fish, swimming in a pond. You can move forward and back, side to side, but never up out of the water. If someone were standing beside the pond, watching you, you’d have no idea they were there. To you, that little pond is an entire universe. Now imagine that someone reaches down and lifts you out of the pond. You see that what you thought was the entire world is only a small pool. You see other ponds. Trees. The sky above. You realize you’re a part of a much larger and more mysterious reality than you had ever dreamed of.”

Daniela leans back in her chair and takes a sip of wine. “So all these other thousands of ponds are all around us, right now—but we just can’t see them?”

“Exactly.”

Jason used to talk like this all the time. He would keep her up late into the night sharing wild theories, sometimes testing things out, but mostly just trying to impress her.

It worked then.

It’s working now.

She looks away for a moment, staring through the window beside their table, watching the water flow by as the light from the nearby buildings swirls in a constant shimmer on the river's surface.

She finally looks back at him over the rim of her wineglass, their eyes meeting, the candlelight flickering between them.

She says, “In one of those ponds out there, do you think there’s another you who stuck with the research? Who achieved all the goals you had in your twenties, before life got in the way?”

He smiles. “It’s crossed my mind.”

“And maybe there’s a version of me that’s a famous artist? Who gave all this up for that?”

Jason leans forward, pushing their plates aside so he can hold both her hands across the table.

“If there are a million ponds out there, with versions of you and me living lives that are alike and different, none is better than this one, right here, right now. I’m more sure of that than anything else.”

Chapter 7

The single lightbulb hanging from the ceiling casts a stark, flickering light on the small cell. I’m strapped to a steel bed, my ankles and wrists chained and locked to bolts in the concrete wall.

Three locks click open in the door, but I’m too drugged to even flinch.

It opens.

Leighton is wearing a tux.

Wire-rim glasses.

As he gets closer, I smell cologne, and then alcohol. Champagne? I wonder where he’s been. A party? A fundraiser? There’s a pink ribbon still on his jacket.

Leighton sits down gently on the edge of the thin mattress.

He looks serious.

And deeply sad.

“I’m sure you have things to say, Jason, but I hope you’ll let me go first. I blame myself a lot for what happened. You came back, and we weren’t ready for you to be as…sick as you were. As you are. We failed you, and I’m sorry. I don’t know what else to say. I just…I hate everything that’s happened. Your return should have been a celebration.”

Even with the strong drugs, I’m shaking with grief.

With rage.

“The man who came to Daniela’s apartment—did you send him after me?” I ask.

“You left me no choice. Even the chance you had told her about this place—”

“Did you tell him to kill her?”

“Jason—”

“Did you?”

He doesn’t answer, but that’s an answer itself.

I stare into Leighton’s eyes, and all I can think about is ripping his face off down to his skull.

“You fucking…”

I break down.

Sobbing.

I can't get the image of blood running down Daniela’s bare foot out of my head.

“I’m so sorry, brother.” Leighton reaches out, puts his hand on my arm, and I almost pull my shoulder out of its socket trying to get away.

“Don’t touch me!”

“You’ve been in this cell almost twenty-four hours. I don’t like keeping you tied down and drugged, but as long as you’re a danger to yourself or others, this can’t change. You need to eat and drink something. Will you do that?”

I stare at a crack in the wall.

I imagine smashing Leighton’s head against it to make another one.

Pounding it into the concrete over and over until there’s nothing left but red goo.

“Jason, either you let them feed you, or I put a feeding tube in your stomach.”

I want to tell him I’m going to kill him. Him and everyone in this lab. I can feel the words rising in my throat, but I stop myself—I’m completely at this man’s mercy.

“I know what you saw in that apartment was horrible, and I’m sorry for that. I wish it had never happened, but sometimes, things get so out of control…Look, please know that I am so, so sorry you had to see that.”

Leighton rises, moves toward the door, and pulls it open.

Standing in the doorway, he looks back at me, his face half in light, half in shadow.

He says, “Maybe you can’t hear this right now, but this place wouldn’t exist without you. None of us would be here if it weren’t for your work, your brilliance. I’m not going to let anyone forget that, especially you.”

—

I calm down.

I pretend to calm down.

Because staying chained up in this tiny cell isn’t doing a damn thing.

From the bed, I stare up at the surveillance camera over the door and ask for Leighton.

Five minutes later, he’s unlocking my restraints and saying, “I think I’m probably as happy as you are to get you out of these things.”

He gives me a hand up.

My wrists are rubbed raw from the leather straps.

My mouth is dry.

I’m lightheaded with thirst.

He asks, “You feeling any better?”

It hits me that my first thought when I woke up here was right. Act like the man they think I am. The only way to do that is to pretend I’ve lost my memories and who I am. Let them guess the rest. Because if I’m not the man they think I am, then they don’t need me.

Then I never leave this lab alive.

I tell him, “I was scared. That’s why I ran.”

"I understand completely."

"I’m sorry I made you all go through this, but you have to realize—I’m lost here. There’s just this huge void where the last ten years should be."

"And we’re going to do everything we can to help you get those memories back. To make you well again. We’re starting the MRI. We’re going to check you for PTSD. Our psychiatrist, Amanda Lucas, will be talking to you soon. I promise you—we will look at everything until we solve this. Until you are completely yourself again."

"Thank you."

"You’d do the same for me. Look, I have no idea what you’ve been through these last fourteen months, but the man I’ve known for eleven years, my colleague and friend who built this place with me? He’s trapped somewhere in your head, and there is nothing I won’t do to find him."

A terrifying thought—what if he’s right?

I think I know who I am.

But there’s a part of me that wonders…What if the memory I have of my real life—husband, father, professor—isn’t real?

What if it’s a result of brain damage I got while working in this lab?

What if I’m actually the man who everyone in this world believes I am?

No.

I know who I am.

Leighton's been sitting on the edge of the mattress.

Now he puts his feet up and leans back against the footboard.

"I have to ask," he says. "What were you doing at that woman's apartment?"

Lie.

"I'm not really sure."

"How did you know her?"

I try to hide the tears and anger.

"I dated her a long time ago."

“Let’s go back to the start. After you got out through the bathroom window three nights ago, how did you get to your place in Logan Square?”

“A cab.”

“Did you tell the driver anything about where you had just been?”

“Of course not.”

“Okay, and after you got away from us at your house, then where did you go?”

Lie.

“I walked around all night. I was confused, scared. The next day I saw this poster for Daniela’s art show. That’s how I found her.”

“Did you talk to anyone else besides Daniela?”

Ryan.

“No.”

“You’re sure about that?”

“Yes. I went back to her apartment, and it was just the two of us until…”

“You have to understand—we’ve given everything to this place. To your work. We’re all in. Any one of us would die to protect it. Including you.”

The gunshot.

The black hole between her eyes.

“It breaks my heart to see you like this, Jason.”

He says this with real sadness and regret.

I can see it in his eyes.

“We were friends?” I ask.

He nods, his jaw tight, like he’s holding back tears.

I say, “I’m having trouble understanding how murdering someone to protect this lab could be okay with you or anyone here.”

“The Jason Dessen I knew wouldn’t have cared about what happened to Daniela Vargas. I’m not saying he would’ve been happy about it. None of us are. It disgusts me. But he would’ve been willing.”

I shake my head.

He says, “You’ve forgotten what we built together.”

“Then show me.”

—

They clean me up, give me new clothes, and feed me.

After lunch, Leighton and I take a service elevator down to sublevel four.

Last time I walked this corridor, it was covered in plastic, and I didn’t know where I was.

I haven’t been threatened.

Haven’t been told I can’t leave.

But I’ve noticed Leighton and I are rarely alone. Two men who look like cops are always nearby. I remember these guards from my first night here.

“It’s four levels,” Leighton says. “Gym, rec room, mess hall, and some dorms on one. Labs, cleanrooms, conference rooms on two. Level three is for making things. Four is the infirmary and mission control.”

We’re heading toward vault doors that look strong enough to protect national secrets.

Leighton stops at a touchscreen on the wall beside them.

He takes out a keycard and holds it under the scanner.

A female computer voice says, Name, please.

He leans in. “Leighton Vance.”

Passcode.

“One-one-eight-seven.”

Voice recognition confirmed. Welcome, Dr. Vance.

The sound of a buzzer startles me, its echo fading down the corridor behind us.

The doors open slowly.

I step into a hangar.

From the rafters high above, lights blaze down, lighting up a twelve-foot cube the color of gunmetal.

My pulse rate quickens.

I can’t believe what I’m looking at.

Leighton must sense my awe, because he says, “Beautiful, isn’t it?”

It is exquisitely beautiful.

At first, I think the hum inside the hangar is coming from the lights, but it can’t be. It’s so deep I can feel it at the base of my spine, like the very low vibration of a huge engine.

I drift toward the box, mesmerized.

I never imagined I would see it in person, this big.

Up close, it isn’t smooth but bumpy, and reflects the light in a way that makes it look like it has many sides, almost see-through.

Leighton points to the clean concrete floor shining under the lights. “We found you passed out right there.”

We walk slowly next to the box.

I reach out, letting my fingers brush the surface.

It’s cold to the touch.

Leighton says, “Eleven years ago, after you won the Pavia, we came to you and said we had five billion dollars. We could have built a spaceship, but we gave it all to you. To see what you could do with unlimited money.”

I ask, “Is my work here? My notes?”

“Of course.”

We reach the other side of the box.

He leads me around the next corner.

On this side, a door has been made in the cube.

“What’s inside?” I ask.

“See for yourself.”

The bottom of the door frame is about a foot above the hangar floor.

I lower the handle, push it open, and start to step inside.

Leighton puts a hand on my shoulder.

“No further,” he says. “For your own safety.”

“It’s dangerous?”

“You were the third person to go inside. Two more went in after you. So far, you’re the only one to come back.”

“What happened to them?”

“We don’t know. Recording devices don’t work inside. The only information we can get now has to come from someone who makes it back. Like you.”

The inside of the box is empty, plain, and dark.

Walls, floor, and ceiling are made of the same material as the outside.
