Puts his shoes on right after getting dressed, not at the front door before going out.

He flosses regularly, and she even saw him plucking his eyebrows three days ago.

He hasn’t worn his favorite sleeping shirt—a faded U2 T-shirt from a concert they saw ten years ago at the United Center—in almost two weeks.

He does the dishes differently—instead of making a wobbly tower in the drying rack, he sets the wet plates and glasses on towels he’s spread on the counter.

He drinks one cup of coffee with breakfast instead of two, and he makes it weaker than before, so weak that she’s been trying to get to the kitchen first each morning to make the coffee herself.

Recently, their family dinner talks have been about ideas, books, and articles Jason is reading, and Charlie’s schoolwork, instead of just talking about what happened that day.

Speaking of Charlie, Jason is also different with their son.

More relaxed, less like a dad.

As if he’s forgotten how to be a father to a teenager.

He’s stopped staying up until two every morning watching Netflix on his iPad.

He never calls her Dani anymore.

He wants her constantly, and it feels like their first time, every time.

He looks at her with a burning intensity that reminds her of how new lovers stare, full of mystery and undiscovered things.

These thoughts, all these little realizations, pile up in her mind as Daniela stands in front of the mirror next to Jason.

It’s morning, and they’re getting ready for their days.

She’s brushing her teeth, he’s brushing his, and when he sees her staring, he grins with toothpaste foam and winks.

She wonders—

Does he have cancer and isn’t telling me?

Is he taking a new antidepressant and isn’t telling me?

Lost his job and isn’t telling me?

A sick, hot feeling erupts in her stomach: is he having an affair with one of his students and she's the one making him feel and act like this new man?

No. None of that feels right.

The thing is, nothing is clearly wrong.

On the surface, they’re actually better. He’s paying her more attention than ever. They haven’t talked and laughed this much since they first got together.

It’s just that he’s…different.

Different in many small ways that might mean nothing and might mean everything.

Jason leans over and spits into the sink.

He turns off the water and steps behind her, puts his hands on her hips, and presses gently against her.

She watches him in the mirror.

Thinking, What secrets are you hiding?

Wanting to say those words.

Those exact words.

But she keeps brushing, because what if the answer costs her this amazing situation?

He says, “I could just watch you do this all day.”

“Brush my teeth?” The words are unclear, the toothbrush still in her mouth.

“Uh-huh.” He kisses the back of her neck, and she shivers, and for a split second it all disappears—the fear, the questions, the doubt.

He says, “Ryan Holder is giving a lecture tonight at six. You want to come with me?”

Daniela leans over, spits, rinses.

“I’d love to, but I have a lesson at five thirty.”

“Then, can I take you to dinner when I get home?”

“I’d love that.”

She turns and kisses him.

Even his kisses feel different now.

Like it's a special thing, every time.

As he starts to pull away, she says, “Hey.”

“Yeah?”

She should ask.

She should talk about all these things she’s noticed.

Just say it all and get it over with.

She desperately wants to know.

She never wants to know.

So she tells herself it's not the right time, plays with his collar, fixes his hair, and sends him off with one last kiss.

Chapter 10

AMPOULES REMAINING: 44

Amanda looks up from the notebook, asks, “Are you sure writing it down is the best idea?”

“When you write something, you really focus on it. It’s hard to write one thing while thinking about something else. Writing it down helps you keep your thoughts and intentions clear.”

“How much should I write?” she asks.

“Maybe start with something easy? A short paragraph?”

She finishes her sentence, closes the notebook, and stands up.

“You’ve got it all in your head?” I ask.

“I think so.”

I put on our backpack. Amanda goes to the door, turns the handle, and opens it. Morning sunlight fills the hallway, so bright that I can’t see anything outside for a moment.

As my eyes get used to the brightness, I begin to see everything around me.

We’re standing in the doorway of the box, on a hilltop looking down at a park.

To the east, green grass slopes down for a few hundred yards to the edge of Lake Michigan. And far away, there's a skyline like nothing I’ve ever seen—the buildings are slender, made of glass and steel that reflects so much light they almost disappear, like a mirage.

The sky is full of moving things, most going every which way above what I guess is Chicago, and some shooting straight up into the blue sky without stopping.

Amanda glances at me and smirks, pointing to the notebook.

I open it to the first page.

She wrote…

I want to go somewhere good, to a good time to be alive. A world I’d want to live in. It’s not the future, but it feels like it…

I say, “Not bad.”

“Is this place really real?” she asks.

“Yes. And you brought us here.”

“Let’s look around. We need a break from the drug anyway.”

She walks down the grassy hill away from the box. We pass a playground and then get on a walking path that goes through the park.

The morning is cold and perfect. I can see my breath.

The grass is white with frost where the sun hasn't reached, and the trees around the park are changing color.

The lake is as still as glass.

A short distance ahead, a line of fancy Y-shaped things crosses the park every fifty meters.

Only when we get closer do I know what they are.

We take a lift up to the platform for trains going north and wait under the warm roof, now forty feet above the park. A digital map with Chicago Transit Authority on it shows this route as the Red Line Express, going from South Chicago to Downtown.

A loud woman's voice shouts from a speaker above us.

Stand clear. A train is arriving. Stand clear. A train is arriving in five…four…three…

I look up and down the track, but I don’t see anything coming.

Two…

A fast-moving shape bursts out of the trees.

One.

A smooth, three-car train slows down as it enters the station, and when the doors open, that computerized female voice says, Please wait to board on green.

The few passengers who get off and walk by us are wearing workout clothes. The red light above each open door turns green.

You may board now for Downtown Station.

Amanda and I look at each other, shrug, and then walk into the first car. It’s almost full of commuters.

This isn’t the El I remember. It’s free. No one is standing. Everyone is fastened into chairs that look like they belong on a rocket.

The word VACANT hangs clearly above each empty seat.

As Amanda and I walk up the aisle, the automated attendant says, Please find a seat. The train cannot leave the station until everyone is safely seated.

We settle into a couple of seats at the front of the car. As I lean back, soft supports come out of the chair and gently hold my shoulders and waist.

Head back against your seat, please. The train is leaving in three…two…one.

The speeding up is smooth but strong. It pushes me back into the soft seat for two seconds, and then we’re gliding along a single rail at an unbelievable speed, with no feeling of friction beneath us as a city view rushes past on the other side of the glass, too fast for me to really see what I’m looking at.

In the distance, that amazing skyline gets closer. The buildings don’t even look real. In the bright morning light, it looks as if someone broke a mirror and stood all the pieces of glass straight up in a row. They’re too beautifully unplanned and uneven to be made by people. Perfect in their flaws and imbalance, like a set of mountains. Or the way a river looks.

The track goes down.

My stomach jumps.

We rush through a tunnel—darkness broken by flashes of light that only make the feeling of confusion and speed stronger.

We come out of the darkness and I hold the sides of my chair tightly, pushed forward into the supports as the train stops suddenly.

The attendant announces, Downtown Station.

Is this your stop? shows up as a hologram right in front of my face above Y? and N?

Amanda says, “Let’s get off here.”

I swipe the Y. She does the same.

Our restraints unlock and vanish into the seats. We get up and leave the car with the other passengers onto the platform of a grand station that makes New York’s Grand Central look small. It’s a towering terminal with a ceiling like cut glass that lets sunlight stream through and spread into the hall as scattered light, casting flickering chevrons of light onto the marble walls.

The place is packed with people.

The long, rough notes of a saxophone fill the air.

On the other side of the hall, we climb a huge flight of steps.

Everyone around us is talking to themselves—phone calls, I guess, but I don’t see any phones.

At the top of the stairs, we go through one of a dozen turnstiles.

The street is packed with people—no cars, no traffic lights. We’re standing at the bottom of the tallest building I’ve ever seen. Even this close, it doesn’t seem real. With no breaks between floors, it looks like a solid piece of ice or crystal.

Drawn by simple curiosity, we cross the street, go into the tower's lobby, and follow the signs to the line for the observation deck.

The elevator is incredibly fast.

I have to keep swallowing to pop my ears from the constant pressure change.

After two minutes, the car stops.

The attendant tells us we have ten minutes to enjoy the view from the top.

As the doors open, we feel a freezing blast of wind. Stepping out of the car, we pass a hologram that says: You are now 7,082 feet above street level.

The elevator shaft is in the middle of the small observation deck, and the very top of the tower is only fifty feet above us, the peak of the glass building twisted into a flame-like shape.

Another hologram appears as we walk toward the edge: The Glass Tower is the tallest building in the Midwest and the third tallest in America.

It’s freezing up here, with a steady breeze coming off the lake. The air feels thinner as I breathe, and I feel a little light-headed, but I’m not sure if it’s from the lack of air or from being so high up.

We reach the anti-suicide railing.

My head swims. My stomach churns.

It’s almost too much to take in—the sparkling city spread out below, the many towers nearby, and the huge lake stretching all the way to southern Michigan.

To the west and south, past the suburbs, the prairie shines in the morning light, a hundred miles away.

The tower sways.

Four states—Illinois, Indiana, Michigan, and Wisconsin—can be seen on a clear day.

Standing on this work of art and imagination, I feel small in a good way.

It’s amazing to breathe the air of a world that could build something so beautiful.

Amanda is beside me, and we’re staring down at the building’s lovely, feminine curve. It’s peaceful and almost silent up here.

The only sound is the lonely whisper of wind.

The noise from the streets below doesn’t reach us.

“Was all of this in your head?” I ask.

“Not knowingly, but it all feels right somehow. Like a dream I barely remember.”

I look toward the northern neighborhoods, where Logan Square should be.

It looks nothing like my home.

Nearby, I see an old man standing behind his old wife, his rough hands on her shoulders as she looks through a telescope. It's pointed down at the most amazing Ferris wheel I’ve ever seen. A thousand feet tall, it towers over the lakeshore, where Navy Pier should be.

I think of Daniela.

Of what this other Jason—Jason2—might be doing right now.

What he might be doing to my wife.

Anger, fear, and homesickness wrap around me like a sickness.

This world, as amazing as it is, isn’t my home.

It isn’t even close.

AMPOULES REMAINING: 42

Down the dark hall through this strange place again, our steps echoing forever.

I’m holding the lantern and thinking about what to write in the notebook when Amanda stops.

“What’s wrong?” I ask.

“Listen.”

It gets so quiet I can hear my heart beating fast.

And then—something impossible.

A sound.

Far, far down the corridor.

Amanda looks at me.

She whispers, “What the fuck?”

I stare into the darkness.

There’s nothing to see but the fading light of the lantern bouncing off the walls.

The sound gets louder and louder.

It’s the shuffling of footsteps.

I say, “Someone’s coming.”

“How is that possible?”

Movement appears at the edge of the light.

A figure coming toward us.

I take a step back, and as they get closer, I want to run, but where would I go?

Might as well face it.

It’s a man.

He’s naked.

His skin covered in mud or dirt or…

Blood.

Definitely blood.

He stinks of it.

As if he rolled around in a pool.

His hair is messy, his face covered in dirt so thick it makes his eyes look big and white.

His hands shake, and his fingers are curled up tight, like he's been trying to grab something.

When he's only ten feet away, I see that this man is me.

I move out of his path, stepping back against the wall to give him plenty of space.

As he walks by, his eyes lock on mine.

I don't even know if he sees me.

He looks like he's been through a war.

Empty inside.

Like he's just come back from hell.

Across his back and shoulders, pieces of his skin have been torn away.

I say, “What happened to you?”

He stops and stares at me, then opens his mouth and makes the most terrifying sound I’ve ever heard—a scream that tears at his throat.

As his voice echoes, Amanda grabs my arm and pulls me away.

He doesn’t follow.

Just watches us go, then shuffles on down the corridor.

Into that endless dark.

—

Thirty minutes later, I’m sitting in front of a door that looks just like all the others, trying to clear my mind of what I just saw in the corridor.

Taking a notebook from my backpack, I open it, pen ready in my hand.

I don’t even have to think.

I simply write the words:

I want to go home.

—

I wonder, Is this how God feels? The thrill of having actually spoken a world into being? Yes, this world already existed, but I linked us to it. From all the possible worlds, I found this one, and it's exactly what I wanted, at least from the box's entrance.

I step down, glass crunches under my shoes as sunlight streams through the high windows, hitting a line of old iron generators.

Even though I've never seen it in daylight, I know this room.

The last time I was here, a harvest moon was rising over Lake Michigan, and I was slumped against one of these old machines, high on drugs, staring at a man in a geisha mask who had forced me into this abandoned power plant at gunpoint.

Staring—though I didn't know it then—at myself.

I couldn’'t have imagined the journey.

The actual hell that was waiting for me.

The box sits in a back corner of the generator room, hidden behind the stairs.

“Well?” Amanda asks.

“I think I did it. This is the last place I remember before waking up in your world.”

—

We head back through the abandoned power plant.

Outside, the sun shines.

Going down.

It’s late afternoon, and the only sound is the lonely cry of seagulls flying over the lake.

We walk west into the neighborhoods of South Chicago, walking along the side of the road like a pair of wanderers.

The skyline in the distance looks familiar.

It’s the one I know and love.

The sun keeps dropping. We’ve been walking for twenty minutes when I realize we haven’t seen a single car.

“Kind of quiet, isn’t it?” I ask.

Amanda looks at me.

The silence wasn’t so obvious out in the industrial area near the lake.

Here it’s shocking.

There are no cars.

No people.

It’s so quiet I can hear the electricity in the power lines above.

The Eighty-Seventh Street CTA station is closed—no buses or trains running.

The only other sign of life is a stray black cat with a twisted tail, sneaking across the road, a rat in its jaws.

Amanda says, “Maybe we should go back to the box.”

“I want to see my home.”

“The feeling here is wrong, Jason. Can’t you feel it?”

“We won’t learn anything about flying the box if we don’t explore where it takes us.”

“Where’s home?”

“Logan Square.”

“Not exactly close enough to walk.”

“So we’ll borrow a car.”

We cross Eighty-Seventh and walk a block of run-down houses. No street sweeper has been by in weeks. There’s trash everywhere. Gross, torn bags of it in big piles up and down the sidewalk.

Many windows are covered with boards.

Some are covered in plastic sheets.

Pieces of clothing hang from most of them.

Some red.

Some black.

The hum of radios and TVs leaks from a few houses.

The cry of a child.

But otherwise, the neighborhood is eerily quiet.

Halfway down the sixth block, Amanda shouts, “Found one!”

I cross the street toward an Oldsmobile Cutlass Ciera from the mid-90s.

White. Rusty around the edges. The wheels have no hubcaps.

Through the dirty glass, I see a pair of keys hanging from the ignition.

Pulling open the driver’s door, I slide in behind the steering wheel.

“So we’re really doing this?” Amanda asks.

I start the engine as she climbs into the passenger side.

There’s a quarter tank of gas left.

Should be enough.

The windshield is so dirty, it takes ten seconds of spraying wiper fluid to clear away the grime, dirt, and stuck-on leaves.

—

The interstate is deserted.

I’ve never seen anything like this.

Empty in both directions as far as I can see.

It’s early evening now, the sun shining off the Willis Tower.

I speed north, and with each mile, the knot in my stomach tightens.

Amanda says, “Let’s go back. Seriously. Something is clearly very wrong.”

“If my family’s here, I need to be with them.”

“How do you even know this is your Chicago?”

She turns on the radio and scrolls through static on the FM dial until the familiar warning pings of the Emergency Alert System screech through the speakers.

The following message is being shared for the Illinois State Police Department. The twenty-four-hour curfew is still in place for Cook County. All residents must stay in their homes until further notice. The National Guard is still watching over all neighborhoods to keep them safe, deliver food, and provide rides to CDC Quarantine Zones.

In the southbound lanes, four camouflaged Humvees speed by.

The risk of the disease spreading is still high. Early signs include fever, bad headache, and muscle aches. If you think you or someone at home is sick, put a red cloth in a window that faces the street. If someone at home has died, put a black cloth in a window facing the street.

CDC workers will help as soon as they can.

More information is coming soon.

Amanda looks at me.

“Why aren’t you turning around?”

—

There’s no place to park on my street, so I leave the car running in the middle of the road.

“You’re crazy,” Amanda says.

I point to the house with a red skirt and black sweater hanging from the bedroom window.

“That’s my home, Amanda.”

“Just hurry. And please be careful.”

I get out of the car.

It’s so quiet, the streets blue in the dusk.

A block away, I see pale figures dragging themselves down the road.

I reach the curb.

The power lines are silent, the light from the houses softer than normal.

Candlelight.

There’s no power in my neighborhood.

Climbing the steps, I look through the large window into the dining room.

It’s dark and gloomy inside.

I knock.

After a long wait, a shadow comes from the kitchen, walking slowly past the table toward the door.

My mouth feels parched.

I shouldn’t be here.

This isn’t even my house.

The chandelier is off.

So is the Van Gogh picture over the fireplace.

I hear three locks unlock.

The door opens a crack, and a smell drifts out that’s nothing like my house.

Just sickness and death.

Daniela holds a candle that shakes in her hand.

Even in the dim light, I see her skin is covered in bumps.

Her eyes look black.

They're bleeding.

Only small bits of white are left.

She says, “Jason?” Her voice is soft and wet. Tears run from her eyes. “Oh my God. Is it you?”

She pulls the door open and stumbles toward me, unsteady on her feet.

It's a heartbreaking thing to feel disgust for the one you love.

I take a step back.

Sensing my horror, she stops.

“How can this be?” she croaks. “You died.”

“What are you talking about?”

“A week ago, they took you out of here in a body bag full of blood.”

“Where’s Charlie?” I ask.

She shakes her head, tears streaming, and coughs a bloody sob into her elbow.

“Dead?” I ask.

“No one’s come for him. He’s still in his room. He’s rotting up there, Jason.”

For a moment, she almost falls, then grabs the door frame.

“Are you real?” she asks.

Am I real?

What a question.

I can’t speak.

My throat aches with grief.

Tears begin to fill my eyes.

As much as I feel sorry for her, the awful truth is that I’m scared of her, my self-preservation recoiling in horror.

Amanda calls from the car, “Someone’s coming!”

I glance up the street, see headlights moving down through the darkness.

“Jason, I will fucking leave you!” Amanda shouts.

The rumble of the approaching engine sounds like a diesel.

Amanda was right. I should have turned around the moment I realized how dangerous this place might be.

This isn’t my world.

And still, my heart feels tied to the second floor of this house, to a bedroom where a version of my son is dead.

I want to run up there and carry him out, but it would kill me.

It’s covered in different symbols—Red Cross, National Guard, CDC.

Amanda is leaning out her window.

“What the hell, Jason?”

The front passenger door of the Humvee opens, and someone in a black biohazard suit and gas mask steps out and aims an assault rifle at me.

The voice from the mask is a woman’s.

I instinctively raise my hands.

Says to Amanda, “Shut that engine off.”

Amanda reaches across the center console and kills the ignition as the driver of the Humvee climbs out.

The sun has gone down.

The street is getting dark.

She nods, tears and blood running down her cheeks.

Daniela barely even flinches as the spring-loaded syringe shoots a dose of morphine into her system.

The effect is almost immediate.

The dusk grows darker.

The first stars appear in the eerily black skies above Chicago.

I think of all the evenings we’ve sat on this porch. Drinking. Laughing. Talking with the neighbors passing by as the streetlamps up and down the block blinked on.

In this moment, my world seems so safe and perfect. I see now—I took all that comfort for granted. It was so good, and there were so many ways it could’ve all fallen apart.

Her voice has become raspy and weak, little more than a whisper.

Her eyes close.

I don’t want to leave her out here, but I know I shouldn’t touch her.

I feel like I have to go up there, like a black hole pulling me in.

Some are dark.

I know she’s right, but it still hurts terribly.

I am built to love and protect that woman.

We’re passing through Bucktown.

In the distance, a whole block of the city throws flames high into the sky.

—

The highway is dark and empty.

Amanda reaches over and takes the mask off my face.

The smell of death from inside my home stays with me.

I can't get rid of it.

I keep thinking about Daniela, dead under a blanket on our front porch.

As we drive west of downtown, I look out my window.

There's just enough starlight to show the shape of the towers.

They’re black, without life.

Amanda says, “Jason?”

“What?”

I look in the rearview mirror.

With no lights, it looks like a ghost riding my bumper.

Blinding high beams and red-and-blues flash, sending shards of light through the inside of the car.

A voice booms through a megaphone behind us: Pull your car over to the side of the road.

Panic rises.

We have nothing to defend ourselves with.

We can’t outrun anything in this piece of junk.

“Yes.”

In the side mirror, I see a black SUV stop behind us.

Turn off your vehicle and drop the keys out the window.

“Jason!”

“Just trust me.”

This is your last warning. Turn off the car and drop the keys out the window. Any attempt to escape will be met with deadly force.

A mile or so back, more headlights appear.

The driver’s-side door to the SUV opens, and a man in a gas mask steps out with his weapon already drawn.

I throw the car back into gear, hit the lights, and floor the accelerator.

I hear a gunshot over the roar of the engine.

Then another.

One rips into the cassette deck.

“A mile or two.”

“There are a lot of them coming.”

“I see them.”

“Jason, if they get us—”

“I know.”

I’m going a little over ninety now, the engine struggling to keep up, the RPMs almost in the red.

We fly by a sign saying our exit is a quarter mile ahead on the right.

At this speed, we get there in seconds.

I take the exit at seventy-five and brake hard.

Neither of us are buckled in.

The force throws Amanda into the glove box and pushes me into the steering wheel.

At the end of the ramp, I turn sharply left through a stop sign—tires squealing, rubber burning. It slams Amanda against her door and almost throws me into her seat.

As I drive across the overpass, I count five sets of flashing lights on the interstate, the closest SUV now speeding onto the exit ramp with two Humvees behind it.

We race through the empty streets of South Chicago.

Amanda leans forward, stares out the windshield.

“What is it?” I ask.

She’s looking at the sky.

“I see lights up there.”

“Like a helicopter?”

“Exactly.”

I speed through empty intersections, past the closed El station, and then we’re out of the ghetto, driving by empty warehouses and train yards.

In the city's backwoods.

“They’re getting close,” Amanda says.

A shot hits the trunk of the car.

Then three more quickly, like someone hammering metal.

She says, “That’s a machine gun.”

“Get down on the floor.”

I hear sirens getting closer.

This old car is no match for what’s coming.

Two more shots break the back window and the windshield.

One tears through the middle of Amanda’s seat.

Through the glass full of bullet holes, I see the lake straight ahead.

I say, “Hang on, we’re almost there.”

I turn hard right onto Pulaski Drive, and as three bullets hit the back passenger door, I cut the lights.

The first few seconds of driving without headlights feels like we’re flying through total darkness.

Then my eyes start to adjust.

I can see the pavement ahead, the black shapes of buildings all around us.

It’s as dark as the countryside out here.

I let off the gas, but I don’t brake.

Looking back, I see two SUVs turn sharply onto Pulaski.

Ahead, I can barely see the two familiar smokestacks pointing into the starry sky.

We’re going less than twenty miles per hour, and even though the SUVs are catching up, I don’t think their headlights have hit us yet.

I see the fence.

We keep slowing down.

I turn across the road, and the grille slams into the locked gate, breaking it open.

We roll slowly into the parking lot, and as I drive around the fallen light poles, I look back at the road.

The sirens are getting louder.

Three SUVs race past the gate, followed by two Humvees with machine guns on top.

I kill the engine.

In the new silence, I listen to the sirens fading away.

Amanda climbs up from the floorboard as I grab our pack from the backseat.

The slams of our doors echo off the brick building straight ahead.

We move toward the crumbling structure and all that’s left of the original sign: CAGO POWER.

A helicopter buzzes overhead, a bright spotlight scraping across the parking lot.

Now I hear an engine revving.

A black SUV skids sideways across Pulaski.

Headlights blind us.

As we run toward the building, a man’s voice through a megaphone orders us to stop.

I step through the hole in the brick wall, help Amanda inside.

It’s completely dark.

I tear open the pack and quickly find the lantern.

The light shows the wrecked front office, and seeing this place in the dark reminds me of that night with Jason2, when he forced me naked into a similar old building.

We leave the first room, the lantern cutting through the darkness.

Down a hallway.

Faster and faster.

Our footsteps slam on the rotting floor.

Sweat pours down my face, burns my eyes.

My heart pounds so hard it shakes my chest.

I’m struggling to breathe.

Voices call after us.

I look back, see lasers slicing through the dark and green blurs from what must be night-vision goggles.

I hear the static of radios and hushed voices and the helicopter rotors cutting through the walls.

A burst of gunfire fills the hallway, and we press ourselves against the ground until the shooting stops.

Scrambling back up, we push on even faster.

At a split, I lead us down another hall, pretty sure it’s the right way, though it’s hard to be certain in the dark.

We finally come out onto the metal platform at the top of the open stairs leading down into the generator room.

We go down.

Our pursuers are so close I can make out three different voices echoing through the last hallway.

Two men, one woman.

I move off the last step, Amanda right behind me as heavy footsteps bang on the stairs above.

Two red dots cross my path.

I step aside and keep running, straight into the darkness ahead, where I know the box must be.

Gunshots explode above us as two figures in full biohazard suits jump off the bottom of the stairs, rushing toward us.

The box is fifty feet ahead, the door open and the metal surface softly spreading the light from our approaching lantern.

Gunshot.

I feel something whiz by my right ear like a hornet.

A bullet hits the door, sending off a spark.

My ear burns.

A man behind us screams, “There’s nowhere to go!”

Amanda is first into the box.

Then I step inside, turn, and push the door shut with my shoulder.

The soldiers are twenty feet away, so close I can hear them panting through their gas masks.

They open fire, and the bright flashes and the bullets hitting the metal box are the last things I see and hear of that awful world.

—

We shoot up right away and start walking down the corridor.

After a while, Amanda wants to stop, but I can’t.

I need to keep going.

I walk for a whole hour.

Through a full round of the drug.

My ear bleeding onto my clothes.

Until the corridor shrinks back into a single box.

I drop the pack.

Cold.

Covered in dried sweat.

Amanda is standing in the middle of the box, her skirt dirty and ripped, sweater torn off during our run through the abandoned power plant.

As she puts the lantern on the floor, something inside me lets go.

The strength, the tension, the anger, the fear.

Everything pouring out at once in a flood of tears and uncontrollable sobbing.

Amanda turns off the lantern.

I crumple against the cold wall, and she pulls me into her lap.

She runs her fingers through my hair.

AMPOULES REMAINING: 40

I wake up in the dark, lying on my side on the floor of the box, my back to the wall. Amanda is pressed close to me, our bodies fitted together, her head resting in the crook of my arm.

I’m hungry and thirsty.

I wonder how long I’ve been asleep.

At least my ear has stopped bleeding.

It’s impossible to ignore how helpless we are.

Besides each other, this box is the only thing we can count on.

A small boat in the middle of a big ocean.

It’s our shelter.

Our prison.

Our home.

—

Carefully, I free us.

Taking off my hoodie, I fold it into a pillow and put it under Amanda’s head.

She moves but doesn’t wake up.

I feel my way to the door, knowing I shouldn’t risk opening it. But I have to know what’s out there, and I can’t stand being trapped in this box anymore.

Turning the handle, I slowly pull it open.

First sensation: the smell of evergreens.

Sunlight streams down through a dense pine forest.

Nearby, a deer stands still, its dark, wet eyes fixed on the box.

As I step out, the deer leaps away silently through the pines.

The forest is surprisingly quiet.

Mist floats above the pine-covered ground.

I walk a short distance from the box and sit in a patch of direct sunlight that warms my face.

A breeze stirs the treetops.

I catch a faint scent of woodsmoke in the wind.

From a campfire?

A chimney?

I wonder, Who lives here?

What kind of world is this?

I hear footsteps.

Looking back, I see Amanda coming toward me through the trees and feel a pang of guilt—I almost got her killed in that last world. She isn’t just here because of me. She’s here because she saved me. Because she did a brave, risky thing.

She sits beside me and turns her face to the sun.

“How’d you sleep?” she asks.

“Hard. Awful crick in my neck. You?”

“Sore all over.”

She leans in close and looks at my ear.

“Bad?” I ask.

“No, the bullet just clipped your earlobe. I’ll clean it up for you.”

She hands me a liter of water we refilled in that futuristic Chicago, and I take a long sip that I wish would last forever.

“You doing okay?” she asks.

“I can’t stop thinking about her. Lying dead on our porch. And Charlie up in his room. We are so lost.”

Amanda says, “I know it’s hard, but the question you should be thinking about—we should both be thinking about—is why did you bring us to that world?”

“All I wrote was, ‘I want to go home.’ ”

“Exactly. That’s what you wrote, but you brought baggage with you.”

“What do you mean?”

“Isn’t it obvious?”

“Obviously not.”

“Your worst fear.”

“That kind of thing isn’t scary to everyone?”

“Maybe. But it seems so perfectly like you, I’m surprised you don’t see it.”

“How is it perfectly like me?”

“Not just losing your family, but losing them to sickness. The same way you lost your mother when you were eight.”

I look at Amanda.

“How did you know that?”

“How do you think?”

Of course. She was Jason2’s therapist.

She says, “Watching his mother die shaped his life. It's a big reason why he never married or had kids, and why he buried himself in work.”

I believe it. Early on, I thought about running from Daniela. Not because I didn’t love her, but because I was afraid of losing her. I felt that fear again when she got pregnant with Charlie.

“Why would I want a life like that?”

“Why do people marry people like their overbearing mothers or absent fathers? To fix old hurts. To fix things as an adult that hurt them as a child. It might not make sense at first, but the subconscious has its own reasons. I think that world taught us a lot about how the box works.”

I hand her the water and say, “Forty.”

“Forty what?”

“Forty ampoules left. Half are yours. That’s twenty tries each to get this right. What do you want to do?”

“I’m not sure. I just know I’m not going back to my world.”

“So, do you want to stay together, or is this goodbye?”

“I don’t know how you feel, but I think we still need each other. I think maybe I can help you get home.”

I lean back against the trunk of a pine tree, a notebook resting on my knees, my thoughts swirling.

What a strange idea—to imagine a world into being with only words, intention, and desire.

It’s a puzzling problem—I have total control, but only if I have control over myself.

My emotions.

My inner storm.

The hidden forces that drive me.

If there are endless worlds, how do I find the one that is truly, specifically mine?

I stare at the page and begin to write down every detail of my Chicago that comes to mind. I paint my life with words.

The sounds of the children in my neighborhood walking to school together, their voices like a stream flowing over rocks—high and bubbly.

Graffiti on the faded white brick of a building three blocks from my house that was so well done it was never painted over.

I think deeply about the small things in my home.

The fourth step on the staircase that always creaks.

The downstairs bathroom with the leaky faucet.

The way my kitchen smells when coffee brews first thing in the morning.

All the tiny, seemingly unimportant details that my world depends on.

Chapter 11

AMPOULES REMAINING: 32

In the study of beauty, there’s an idea called the uncanny valley. It says that when something looks almost human—like a dummy or a robot that seems human—it makes people feel disgusted. This is because it looks so much like a person, but it’s just different enough to make you feel uneasy, like something is both known and foreign.

I feel something similar as I walk around this Chicago that’s almost my own. I’d rather have a nightmare about the end of the world. Broken buildings and empty lands are nothing compared to standing on a corner I know well and seeing that the street names are wrong. Or the coffee shop where I always get my strong coffee with soy milk is now a fancy wine store. Or my house at 44 Eleanor Street is a brownstone where strangers live.

—

This is the fourth Chicago we’ve found since leaving that world of sickness and death. Each one has been like this—almost home.

Night is coming, and because we’ve taken four doses of the drug quickly without stopping to recover, we decide not to go back to the box for the first time.

It’s the same hotel in Logan Square where I stayed in Amanda’s world.

The neon sign is red instead of green, but the name is the same—HOTEL ROYALE—and it’s just as odd, just as stuck in the past, but in many small, different ways.

Our room has two double beds, and like the last room I had here, it faces the street.

I put our plastic bags with toiletries and used clothes on the dresser next to the TV.

Normally, I might have hated this old room that smells like cleaning spray can’t hide the mold and worse.

Tonight it feels great.

Taking off my hoodie and undershirt, I say, “I’m too dirty to care about this place.”

I throw them in the trash can.

Amanda laughs. “You don’t want to see who’s nastier, me or you.”

“I’m surprised they gave us a room at all.”

“That should tell you how good this place is.”

I go to the window and open the curtains.

It’s early evening.

Raining.

The hotel sign outside spills red neon light into the room.

I couldn’t guess the day or date if I tried.

I say, “The bathroom’s all yours.”

Amanda grabs her things from the plastic bag.

Soon, I hear the clear sound of running water bouncing off the tile.

She calls out, “Oh my God, you have to take a bath, Jason! You have no idea!”

I’m too dirty to lie down on the bed, so I sit on the carpet next to the radiator, letting waves of heat wash over me and watching the sky darken through the window.

—

I take Amanda’s advice and draw a bath.

Water trickles down the walls.

The heat really helps my sore back, which has been hurting for days from sleeping in the box.

As I shave, I keep wondering who I really am.

There’s no Jason Dessen working as a physics professor at Lakemont College or any nearby schools, but I still wonder if I'm out there.

In another city.

Another country.

Maybe living under a different name, with a different woman, a different job.

If I am, if I spend my days fixing cars or filling teeth instead of teaching physics, am I still the same person deep down?

And what is that deep down part?

If you take away everything about my personality and life, what's left that makes me who I am?

After an hour, I come out, clean for the first time in days, wearing jeans, a plaid shirt, and old Timberland boots. They're a bit too big, but I'm wearing two pairs of wool socks to make them fit.

Amanda looks me up and down, says, “It works.”

“You look good too.”

She’s wearing black jeans, boots, a white T-shirt, and a black leather jacket from a thrift store that still smells like the last person who owned it smoked a lot.

She’s lying in bed, watching a TV show I don’t know.

She looks up at me. “Guess what I’m thinking?”

“What?”

“A bottle of wine. Tons of food. All the desserts. I mean, I haven’t been this thin since college.”

“The multiverse diet.”

She laughs, and it’s nice to hear.

We walk for twenty minutes in the rain because I want to see if one of my favorite restaurants is really here.

It is, and it feels like seeing a friend while traveling.

This comfy, trendy place is inspired by an old Chicago neighborhood bar.

There’s a long wait for a table, so we watch the bar until two stools are free, and we sit at the end near a wet window.

We order cocktails.

Then wine.

Lots of small plates that keep arriving.

The drinks give us a nice buzz, and we only talk about what’s happening now.

How the food tastes.

How good it feels to be inside and warm.

Neither of us says a word about the box.

Amanda says I look like a lumberjack.

I tell her she looks like a biker chick.

We both laugh too much, too loud, but we need it.

As she gets up to go to the bathroom, she says, “You’ll be right here?”

“I will not move from this spot.”

But she keeps looking back.

I watch her walk down the bar and disappear around the corner.

Alone, the normalness of the moment is almost too much. I look around the restaurant, taking in the faces of the waiters, the customers. Two dozen loud talks mixing into a kind of meaningless noise.

I think, What if you knew what I know?

—

The walk back is colder and wetter.

Near the hotel, I see the sign for my bar, Village Tap, flashing across the street.

I say, “Want a nightcap?”

It’s late, so most of the evening crowd is gone.

We get seats at the bar, and I watch the bartender finish someone’s bill on the touchscreen.

He turns and comes over, looks at Amanda first, then me.

It’s Matt. He’s probably made me a thousand drinks. He served me and Ryan Holder my last night back home.

But he doesn’t seem to recognize me.

Just blank, disinterested politeness.

“What can I get you guys?”

Amanda orders a wine.

I ask for a beer.

As he pulls the tap, I lean over and whisper to Amanda, “I know the bartender. He doesn’t recognize me.”

“What do you mean you know him?”

“This is my regular bar.”

“No, it’s not. And of course he doesn’t recognize you. What did you expect?”

“It’s just weird. This place looks exactly as it should.”

Matt brings our drinks over.

“Want to start a tab?”

I have no credit card, no ID, just a roll of cash in my jacket pocket next to our remaining vials.

“I’ll just pay now.” As I reach for the money, I say, “I’m Jason, by the way.”

“Matt.”

“I like this place. Yours?”

“Yep.”

He doesn’t seem to care at all what I think of his bar, and it makes me feel sad inside. Amanda notices. When Matt leaves us, she raises her wineglass and taps it against my beer.

Says, “To a good meal, a warm bed, and still being alive.”

—

Back in the hotel room, we turn off the lights and undress in the dark. I know I can’t judge our room fairly anymore, because the bed feels amazing.

Amanda asks from her side of the room, “You locked the door?”

“I did.”

I close my eyes. I can hear the rain tapping against the window. Now and then a car goes by on the wet street below.

“It was a nice night,” Amanda says.

“It was. I don’t miss the box, but it feels odd being away from it.”

“I don’t know about you, but my old life feels more and more like a ghost. You know how a dream feels as you wake up? It loses its color and strength and sense. You feel less and less connected to it.”

I ask, “Do you think you’d ever forget it completely? Your world?”

“I don’t know. I think it could get to the point where it didn’t feel real anymore. Because it isn’t. The only thing that’s real right now is this city. This room. This bed. You and me.”

—

In the middle of the night, I realize Amanda is next to me.

It’s not exactly new. We’ve slept like this in the box many times. Holding each other in darkness, as lost as two people could be.

The only difference now is that we’re only in our underwear, and her skin is so soft against mine it's hard to ignore.

Flashes of neon light peek through the curtains.

Reaching over in the dark, she grabs my hand and puts it on her.

Then she turns, faces me.

“You’re better than he ever was.”

“Who?”

“The Jason I knew.”

“I hope so. Jesus.” I smile to show it's a joke. She just stares at me with those dark eyes. We’ve looked at each other a lot lately, but there’s something different in her eyes now.

There’s a connection here, and it’s getting stronger every day.

If I moved even a tiny bit closer to her, we would do this.

I was sure of it.

And if I kissed her, if we slept together, maybe I’d feel guilty and regret it, or maybe I’d realize she could make me happy.

A version of me definitely kissed her right then.

A version knows the answer.

But it won’t be me.

She says, “If you want me to go back over there, just tell me.”

I say, “I don’t want you to, but I have to ask you to.”

AMPOULES REMAINING: 24

Yesterday, I saw myself on the Lakemont campus in a world where Daniela had died—according to a death notice I found online at the library—at thirty-three from brain cancer.

Today, it's a beautiful afternoon in a Chicago where Jason Dessen died in a car accident two years ago.

I walk into an art gallery in Bucktown, trying not to look at the woman behind the counter, who is reading. Instead, I focus on the walls, which are covered in oil paintings that seem to only show Lake Michigan.

In every season.

Every color.

Every time of day.

The woman says without looking up, "Let me know if you need anything."

"Are you the artist?"

She puts the book down and comes out from behind the register.

Walks over.

It's the closest I've been to Daniela since the night I helped her die. She's gorgeous—wearing tight jeans and a black T-shirt with acrylic paint on it.

“I am, yes. Daniela Vargas.”

She clearly doesn’t know me, doesn’t recognize me. I guess in this world, we never met.

“Jason Dessen.”

She offers her hand, and I take it. It feels just like hers—rough and strong and skilled—the hand of an artist. Paint is stuck to her fingernails. I can still feel them running down my back.

“These are amazing,” I say.

“Thank you.”

“I love the focus on one subject.”

“I started painting the lake three years ago. It’s so different from season to season.” She points to the one we’re standing in front of. “This was one of my first tries. That’s from Juneway Beach in August. On clear days in late summer, the water turns this bright, greenish blue. Almost tropical.” She moves down the wall. “Then you get a day like this in October, all clouded over, and it makes the water gray. I love these because there’s almost no difference between the water and the sky.”

“You have a favorite season?” I ask.

“Winter.”

“Really?”

“It’s the most diverse, and the sunrises are spectacular. When the lake froze over last year, those were some of my best paintings.”

“How do you work? Outside, or—”

“From photographs mostly. I sometimes set up my easel on the shore in the summer, but I love my studio so much I rarely paint anywhere else.”

The conversation stops.

She glances back at the register.

Probably wanting to get back to her book.

Most likely having judged my old, thrift-store jeans and hand-me-down shirt and realized I probably won’t buy anything.

“Is this your gallery?” I ask, even though I know the answer.

Just wanting to hear her talk.

To make this moment last as long as it can.

“It’s a co-op, but since my work is showing this month, I’m here to watch the place.”

She smiles.

Just politely.

Starts to walk away.

“If you need anything else—”

“I just think you’re so talented.”

“Oh, that’s very kind. Thank you.”

“My wife is an artist.”

“From around here?”

“Yep.”

“What’s her name?”

“It’s, um, well, you likely don’t know it, and we’re not really together now, so…”

“Sorry to hear that.”

I reach down and touch the loose thread that's still, somehow, tied around my ring finger.

“It’s not that we’re not together. It’s just…”

I don’t finish the thought, because I want her to ask me to finish it. To show a bit of interest, stop looking at me like a stranger, because we aren't strangers.

We've built a life together.

We have a son.

I’ve kissed every part of your body.

I’ve cried with you and laughed with you.

How can something so strong in one world not carry over into this one?

I stare into Daniela’s eyes, but there’s no love or recognition or hint of anything I know in her eyes.

She just looks a little awkward.

Like she wants me to go away.

“Do you want to get coffee?” I ask.

She smiles.

Now really awkward.

“I mean after you’re done here, whenever that is.”

If she says yes, Amanda will kill me. I’m already late meeting her at the hotel. We’re supposed to go back to the box this afternoon.

But Daniela isn’t going to say yes.

She’s biting her lip like she always does when she’s nervous, probably trying to think of some reason other than a flat, crushing “no,” but I can tell she’s stumped, that she’s getting ready to reject me.

“You know what?” I say. “Never mind. I’m sorry. I’ve caught you off guard.”

Fuck.

I’m dying inside.

It’s one thing to be turned down by a total stranger.

It’s much worse to fail with the mother of your child.

“I’m just going to leave now.”

I walk toward the door.

She doesn’t try to stop me.

AMPOULES REMAINING: 16

Each Chicago we've visited this past week has trees that look more and more bare, their leaves gone and stuck to the ground from the rain. I sit on the bench across from my building, wrapped up in a used coat I bought yesterday for $12 with money from another world. It smells like an old man's closet—mothballs and pain relief cream.

Back at the hotel, I left Amanda writing in her notebook.

I lied and told her I was going for a walk to clear my head and get coffee.

I watch myself leave the building, hurry down the steps, and walk to the train station, where I'll take the Purple Line to the Lakemont campus in Evanston. I have noise-canceling headphones on, probably listening to a podcast—a science lecture or an episode of This American Life.

It’s October 30 according to the Tribune, a little less than a month since I was kidnapped and taken from my world.

It feels like I’ve been traveling in the box for years.

I don’t know how many Chicagos we’ve connected to so far.

They’re all starting to look the same.

This one is the closest, but it's still not mine. Charlie goes to a charter school, and Daniela works from home as a graphic designer.

Sitting here, I realize I’ve always seen Charlie’s birth and my choice to build a life with Daniela as the turning point that made our careers less successful.

But that’s too simple.

Yes, Jason2 left Daniela and Charlie and then had his big success. But many Jasons have left and not invented anything great.

There are worlds where I left Daniela and our careers still went nowhere.

Or where I left and we both had some success, but didn’t become hugely famous.

And on the other hand, there are worlds where I stayed and we had Charlie, which led to less-than-ideal outcomes.

Where our relationship fell apart.

Where I chose to end our marriage.

Or Daniela did.

Or we struggled and suffered in a loveless and broken state, staying together for our son's sake.

If I’m the best example of family success for all the Jason Dessens, Jason2 is the best example of professional and creative success. We’re opposites of the same person, and I guess it makes sense that Jason2 wanted my life out of all the options he had.

Even though he’d achieved total success at work, being a family man was as new to him as his life was to me.

It all shows that who I am isn’t simple.

It’s complicated.

And maybe I can stop feeling bitter and angry about the life I didn’t live, because the life I didn’t live isn’t just the opposite of who I am. It’s a never-ending maze that shows all the versions of my life between me and Jason2.

I reach into my pocket and take out the cheap cell phone I bought for $50, money Amanda and I could have used for food for a day, or a cheap motel for another night.

Wearing my fingerless gloves, I unfold the ripped-out piece of yellow paper from the D section of the Chicago Metro phone book and dial the number that’s circled.

There’s something really sad about a place that’s almost home.

From where I’m sitting, I can see the room on the second floor that I think is Daniela’s office in the house. The blinds are open and she’s sitting with her back to me, looking at a big computer screen.

I see her pick up a cordless phone and look at the screen.

Don't know the number.

Please answer.

She puts the phone away.

My voice: “You’ve reached the Dessens. We can’t answer, but if you—”

I hang up before the tone.

Call again.

This time, she answers before the second ring, “Hello?”

For a moment, I say nothing.

Because I can’t speak.

“Hello?”

“Hi.”

“Jason?”

“Yeah.”

“What number are you calling from?”

I knew she’d ask that first thing.

I say, “My phone died, so I borrowed one from a woman on the train.”

“Is everything okay?”

“How’s your morning?” I ask.

“Fine. I just saw you, goofball.”

“I know.”

She spins around on the swivel chair at her desk, says, “So, you really wanted to talk to me so much that you borrowed a stranger’s phone?”

“I did, actually.”

“You’re sweet.”

I just sit there, listening to her voice.

“Daniela?”

“Yes?”

“I really miss you.”

“What’s wrong, Jason?”

“Nothing.”

“You sound strange. Talk to me.”

“I was walking to the train, and it just hit me.”

“What did?”

“I take so many moments with you without appreciating them. I walk out the door to work, already thinking about my day, about the lecture I have to give, whatever, and I just…I suddenly realized on the train how much I love you. How much you mean to me. Because you never know.”

“Never know what?”

“When it could all be taken away. Anyway, I tried to call you, but my phone was dead.”

For a long moment, there’s just silence on the other end of the line.

“Daniela?”

“I’m here. And I feel the same way about you. You know that, right?”

I close my eyes, overwhelmed with emotion.

Thinking, I could cross the street right now, come inside, and tell you everything.

I am so lost, my love.

Daniela steps down off her chair and walks to the window. She’s wearing a long, cream sweater over yoga pants. Her hair is up, and she’s holding a mug of what I think is tea from a local shop.

She holds her belly, which is round with child.

Charlie is going to be a big brother.

I smile through the tears, wondering what he thinks of that.

It’s something my Charlie didn’t get.

“Jason, are you sure everything’s okay?”

“Yes.”

“Well, look, I have a deadline for this client, so…”

“You have to go.”

“I do.”

I don’t want her to. I need to keep hearing her voice.

“Jason?”

“Yes?”

“I love you very much.”

“I love you too. You have no idea.”

“I’ll see you tonight.”

No, you’ll see a very fortunate version of me who has no idea how lucky he is.

She hangs up.

Goes back to her desk.

I put the phone back in my pocket, shivering, my thoughts racing in wild directions, toward grim daydreams.

I imagine the train I’m on to work crashing.

My body wrecked so badly no one would know me.

Or never found at all.

I imagine myself sliding into this life.

It’s not really mine, but maybe it’s good enough.

—

In the evening, I’m still on the bench on Eleanor Street across from the brownstone that isn’t mine, watching our neighbors come home from work and school.

What a gift it is to have people waiting for you every day.

To be loved.

To be expected.

I thought I valued every moment, but sitting here in the cold, I know I didn't appreciate it enough. And how could I? Until everything falls apart, we don't know what we truly have, or how delicately and wonderfully it all balances.

The sky darkens.

Up and down the block, the houses light up.

Jason comes home.

I’m in a bad state.

Haven’t eaten all day.

Haven’t had water since morning.

Amanda must be worried sick about where I am, but I can’t pull myself away. My life, or a heartbreaking version of it, is happening right across the street.

—

It’s well past midnight when I unlock our hotel room.

The lights are on, the television loud.

Amanda gets out of bed, wearing a T-shirt and pajama bottoms.

I close the door quietly behind me.

I say, “I’m sorry.”

“You asshole.”

“I had a bad day.”

“ You had a bad day.”

“Amanda—”

Rushing toward me, she pushes me as hard as she can with both hands, making me stumble back into the door.

She says, “I thought you’d left me. Then I thought something happened to you. I couldn’t reach you. I started calling hospitals, describing you.”

“I would never leave you like that.”

“How can I know that? You scared me!”

“I’m sorry, Amanda.”

“Where were you?”

She has me stuck against the door.

“I just sat on the bench across from my house all day.”

“All day? Why?”

“I don’t know.”

“That isn’t your house, Jason. That isn’t your family.”

“I know that.”

“Do you?”

“I also followed Daniela and Jason on a date.”

“What do you mean you followed them?”

“I stood outside the restaurant where they ate.”

The shame hits me as I say the words.

I push past Amanda into the room, take a seat on the edge of my bed.

She comes over and stands in front of me.

I say, “They went to a movie after. I followed them inside. Sat behind them in the theater.”

“Oh, Jason.”

“I did another dumb thing.”

“What?”

“I used some of our money to buy a phone.”

“Why did you need a phone?”

“So I could call Daniela and act like I was her Jason.”

I expect Amanda to get mad again, but instead she comes closer, holds my neck, and kisses the top of my head.

“Stand up,” she says.

“Why?”

“Just do what I say.”

I stand.

She unzips my jacket and helps me slip my arms out. Then she pushes me back onto the bed and kneels.

Unlaces my boots.

Pulls them off my feet and throws them into the corner.

I say, “For the first time, I think I get why the Jason you knew did what he did to me. I’m having some messed-up thoughts.”

“Our minds aren’t made for this. Seeing all these versions of your wife—I can’t even imagine.”

“He must have followed me for weeks. To work. On dates with Daniela. He probably sat on that same bench and watched us in our house at night, picturing me gone. Do you know what I almost did tonight?”

“What?” She looks scared.

“I bet they keep their spare key where we keep ours. I left the movie early. I was going to find the key and sneak into the house. I wanted to hide in a closet and watch their life. Watch them sleep. It’s sick, I know. And I know your Jason was probably in my house a bunch of times before he finally got the guts to steal my life.”

“But you didn’t do it.”

“No.”

“Because you’re a good man.”

“I don’t feel very good right now.”

I fall back onto the mattress and stare up at the ceiling of this hotel room that, in every unimportant way, has become our home away from the box.

Amanda crawls onto the bed beside me.

“This isn’t working, Jason.”

“What do you mean?”

“We’re just going in circles.”

“I don’t agree. Look where we started. Remember that first world we stepped into, with the buildings crashing down all around us?”

“I’ve lost count of how many Chicagos we’ve been to.”

“We’re getting closer to my—”

“We’re not getting closer, Jason. The world you’re looking for is like one grain of sand on an endless beach.”

“That’s not true.”

“You’ve seen your wife murdered, die of a terrible disease. You’ve seen her not know who you are, married to other men, even to other versions of you. How much more of this can you handle before you lose your mind? You’re already close to it.”

“It’s not about what I can or can’t handle. It’s about finding my Daniela.”

“Really? Is that what you were doing sitting on a bench all day? Looking for your wife? Look at me. We only have sixteen ampoules left. We’re running out of chances.”

My head is pounding.

Spinning.

“Jason.” I feel her hands on my face now. “Do you know what insanity is?”

“What?”

“Doing the same thing over and over and expecting different results.”

“Next time—”

“What? Next time we’ll find your home? How? Are you going to fill another notebook tonight? Would it even matter if you did?” She puts her hand on my chest. “Your heart is racing. You need to calm down.”

She rolls over and turns off the lamp on the table between the beds.

She lies down beside me, but her touch isn't sexual.

My head feels better with the lights off.

The only light in the room is the blue neon sign outside the window, and it’s late, so there aren't many cars passing on the street below.

Sleep is coming. Thankfully.

I shut my eyes, thinking of the five notebooks stacked on my bedside table. Almost every page is filled with my increasingly frantic writing. I keep thinking if I write enough, if I'm detailed enough, I’ll capture a complete enough picture of my world to finally take me home.

But it’s not working.

Amanda is right.

I'm looking for a grain of sand on a never-ending beach.

Chapter 12

In the morning, Amanda is not next to me. I lie on my side, watching the sunlight come through the blinds, listening to the traffic sounds through the walls. The clock is behind me on the bedside table. I can’t see what time it is, but it feels late. We’ve slept in.

I sit up, throw back the covers, look over at Amanda’s bed.

It’s empty.

“Amanda?”

I quickly walk toward the bathroom to see if she’s in there, but I stop when I see something on top of the dresser.

Some cash.

A few coins.

Eight ampoules.

And a piece of paper torn out of a notebook, covered in Amanda’s handwriting.

Jason. After last night, it was clear you've decided on a road I can't take. I wrestled with this all night. As your friend, as a therapist, I want to help you. I want to fix you. But I can’t. And I can’t keep watching you fall. Especially if I’m partly why you keep falling. How much are our shared hidden thoughts pushing us to connect to these worlds? It’s not that I don’t want you to get back to your wife. I want that more than anything. But we’ve been together for weeks now. It’s hard not to get close, especially when you’re all I have.

I read your notebooks yesterday, when I was worried you’d left me, and honey, you’re missing the point. You’re writing down all these things about your Chicago, but not how you feel.

I’ve left you the backpack, half the ampoules, and half the money (a huge $161 and some change). I don’t know where I’ll end up. I’m curious and scared, but excited too. Part of me really wants to stay, but you need to pick your own next door to open. So do I.

Jason, I wish you only happiness. Be safe. Amanda

AMPOULES REMAINING: 7

Alone, the full horror of the corridor hits me.

I have never felt so alone.

—

There’s no Daniela in this world.

Chicago feels wrong without her.

I hate everything about it.

The sky doesn't look right.

The buildings I know make fun of me.

Even the air feels like a lie.

Because it isn’t my city.

It’s ours.

AMPOULES REMAINING: 6

I’m heading out.

All night, I walk the streets alone.

Lost.

Afraid.

Letting the drug wear off.

I eat at an all-night diner and ride the train back to the South Side at dawn.

On my way to the abandoned power plant, three teenagers see me.

They’re across the street, but the streets are empty at this hour.

They shout at me.

Taunts and insults.

I ignore them.

Walk faster.

But I know I’m in trouble when they cross the street, heading straight for me.

For a moment, I think about running, but they’re young and definitely faster. Besides, it hits me as my mouth goes dry and fear floods me with adrenaline that I might need my strength.

On the edge of a neighborhood, where the houses end and a train yard starts, they reach me.

There’s no one else out at this hour.

No help in sight.

They’re even younger than I thought, and the smell of cheap beer hangs around them like bad cologne. The wild look in their eyes tells me they’ve been out all night, maybe looking for something like this to happen.

The beating really starts.

They don’t even bother to trash talk.

I’m too tired, too worn out to fight back.

Before I know it, I’m on the ground getting kicked in the stomach, the back, the face.

I black out for a second, and when I wake up, I feel their hands all over me, searching—I guess—for a wallet that I don't have.

They finally grab my backpack, and as I bleed on the ground, they run off down the street, laughing.

—

I lie there for a long time, listening to the traffic get louder and louder.

The day gets brighter.

People walk past me on the sidewalk without stopping.

Each breath sends a stab of pain through my bruised ribs, and my left eye is swollen shut.

After a while, I manage to sit up.

Shit.

The ampoules.

Using a chain-link fence, I pull myself onto my feet.

Please.

I slide my hand up inside my shirt, my fingers touching the piece of duct tape stuck to my side.

It hurts like hell to peel it back slowly, but everything hurts like hell.

The ampoules are still there.

Three crushed.

Three intact.

—

I stumble back into the box and shut myself inside.

My money is gone.

My notebooks are gone.

My syringes and needles.

I have nothing but my broken body and three more chances to fix this.

AMPOULES REMAINING: 2

I spend the first half of the day begging on a South Side street corner to get enough money for a train into the city.

I spend the rest of it four blocks from my brownstone, sitting on the pavement behind a cardboard sign that reads:

HOMELESS. DESPERATE. ANYTHING HELPS.

My bruised face must really get people's attention, because I collect $28.15 by the time the sun goes down.

I’m hungry, thirsty, and in pain.

I pick a diner that looks run-down enough to let me in, and as I pay for my meal, I feel completely exhausted.

I have nowhere to go.

No money for a motel room.

Outside, the night has turned cold and rainy.

I walk to my house and go around the block to the alley, trying to find a place where I can sleep without being bothered or seen.

There’s a space between my garage and the neighbor’s, hidden behind the trash and recycling bins. I squeeze between them, carrying a flattened box, which I prop against my garage wall.

Underneath it, I listen to the rain hitting the cardboard above, hoping my flimsy shelter will hold up through the night.

From where I am, I can see over the tall fence around my backyard, through a window, into the second floor of my house.

It’s the master bedroom.

Jason walks by.

This isn’t Jason2. I know this isn’t my world. The stores and restaurants near my house are all wrong. These Dessens have different cars than my family. And he’s heavier than I’ve ever been.

Daniela peeks into view for a second, reaches up, and closes the blinds.

Good night, my love.

The rain gets heavier.

The box droops.

I start to shiver.

—

On my eighth day living on the streets of Logan Square, Jason Dessen himself puts a $5 bill in my collection box.

There’s no risk.

I’m impossible to identify.

Sunburned, with a beard, and smelling like utter poverty.

The people around here are kind. Every day, I earn enough to buy a cheap dinner and save a little.

Every night, I sleep in the alley behind 44 Eleanor Street.

It turns into a sort of game. When the lights go off in the main bedroom, I close my eyes and pretend I’m him.

With her.

Some days, I feel like I'm losing my mind.

Amanda once said her old life felt like a ghost, and I think I get it. We link reality to what we can touch—everything we sense. I keep telling myself there’s a box in Chicago that can take me to a world where I have it all, but I don’t believe it anymore. My reality—more and more—is this world. Where I have nothing. Where I’m a dirty, homeless thing that just makes people feel sorry, sad, and sick.

A homeless man is standing on the sidewalk, loudly talking to no one.

I wonder, Am I any different? Aren’t we both lost in worlds that, through no fault of our own, don't fit who we are anymore?

The scariest times seem to be happening more often. Times when the idea of a magic box sounds crazy, even to me.

—

One night, I pass a liquor store and see I have enough for a bottle.

I drink a whole pint of J&B.

I'm standing in the main bedroom of 44 Eleanor Street, looking down at Jason and Daniela, asleep under the blankets.

The clock says 3:38 a.m. The house is silent, but I’m so drunk I can feel my heart in my ears.

I can’t understand how I got here.

All I can think is that I had this.

Once.

This beautiful dream of a life.

And now, with the room spinning and tears on my face, I honestly don’t know if that life was real or just a dream.

I step toward Jason’s side of the bed, my eyes getting used to the dark.

He sleeps calmly.

I want what he has so much I can taste it.

I’d do anything to have his life. To be him.

I imagine killing him. Choking him or shooting him in the head.

I see myself trying to be him.

Trying to accept this version of Daniela as my wife. This Charlie as my son.

Would this house ever feel like mine?

Could I sleep at night?

Could I ever look Daniela in the eyes and not think about the fear on her real husband’s face just before I killed him?

No.

No.

Understanding hits hard—painful, shameful, but exactly when I need it most.

The guilt and all the small differences would turn my life here into hell. Into a reminder of what I’d done, and what I still didn’t have.

This would never feel like my world.

I can’t do this.

I don’t want this.

I am not this man.

I shouldn’t be here.

As I stumble out of the bedroom and down the hall, I realize that even thinking about this meant giving up on finding my Daniela.

Like I’m letting her go.

That she’s out of reach.

And maybe that’s true. Maybe I have no chance of ever getting back to her and Charlie and my perfect world. To that single grain of sand on an endless beach.

But I still have two ampoules left, and I won’t stop fighting until they’re gone.

I go to a thrift store and buy new clothes—jeans, flannel shirt, a black peacoat.

Then toiletries at a drugstore, along with a notebook, pack of pens, and a flashlight.

I check into a motel, throw my old clothes away, and take the longest shower of my life.

The water running off my body is gray.

Standing in front of the mirror, I look almost like myself again, though my cheekbones stick out more because I haven't been eating well.

—

I sleep until the afternoon and then take the train down to the South Side.

The power plant is quiet, sunlight coming in at an angle through the windows of the generator room.

Sitting in the doorway of the box, I open the notebook.

Ever since I woke up, I’ve been thinking about what Amanda said in her goodbye note, how I haven’t really written about what I feel.

I’m twenty-seven years old. I’ve worked all morning at the lab, and things are going so well I almost ignore the party. I’ve been doing that a lot lately—neglecting friends and social events to steal just a few more hours in the cleanroom.

I first notice you in the far corner of the small backyard as I stand on the deck, sipping a Corona-and-lime, my thoughts still back at the lab. I think it’s the way you’re standing that catches my attention—stuck with a tall, lanky guy in tight black jeans who I recognize from this circle of friends. He’s an artist or something. I don’t even know his name, only that my friend Kyle has said to me recently, Oh, that guy sleeps with everyone.

I can’t explain it, even to this day, but as I watch him chatting up this dark-haired, dark-eyed woman in a cobalt-blue dress—you—a flash of jealousy hits me. For some reason, insanely, I want to hit him. Something in your body language suggests you're not comfortable. You aren’t smiling, your arms are crossed, and it seems like you’re trapped in a bad conversation, and that for some reason, I care. You hold an empty wineglass, streaked with the remains of a red. Part of me urges, Go talk to her, save her. The other half screams, You know nothing about this woman, not even her name. You are not that guy.

I find myself moving toward you through the grass, carrying a new glass of wine, and when your eyes look at mine, it feels like something has just broken in my chest. Like worlds colliding. As I get closer, you take the glass out of my hand as if you had previously sent me off to get it and smile with an easy familiarity, like we’ve known each other forever. You try to introduce me to Dillon, but the skinny-jeaned artist, now blocked, makes his excuses and leaves.

Then it’s just the two of us standing in the shade of the hedgerow, and my heart is going like mad. I say, “I’m sorry to interrupt, but it looked like you might need rescuing,” and you say, “Good instincts. He’s pretty, but awful.” I introduce myself. You tell me your name. Daniela. Daniela.

I only remember pieces of what was said in our first moments together. Mainly how you laugh when I tell you I’m an atomic physicist, but not in a mean way. As if the news truly delights you. I remember how the wine had stained your lips. I’ve always known, on a purely intellectual level, that our separateness and isolation are an illusion. We’re all made of the same thing—the blown-out pieces of matter formed in the fires of dead stars. I’ve just never felt that knowledge in my bones until that moment, there, with you. And it’s because of you.

Yes, maybe I just want to get laid, but I also wonder if this sense of connection might be evidence of something deeper. This thought I wisely keep to myself. I remember the pleasant buzz from the beer and the warmth of the sun, and then, as it begins to drop, realizing how badly I want to leave this party with you but not having the guts to ask. And then you say, “I have a friend whose gallery opening is tonight. Want to come?”

And I think: I will go anywhere with you.

AMPOULES REMAINING: 1

I walk the infinite corridor, the beam of my flashlight glancing off the walls.

After a while, I stop in front of a door like all the rest.

One in a trillion, trillion, trillion.

My heart is racing, my palms sweating.

There is nothing else I want.

Just my Daniela.

I want her in a way I can’t explain.

That I never want to explain, because its mystery is perfect.

I want the woman I saw at that party years ago.

The one I chose to build a life with, even if it meant losing other things I loved.

I want her.

Nothing else.

I breathe in.

I breathe out.

And I open the door.

Chapter 13

Snow from a recent storm has lightly covered the concrete and the generators under the open, upper windows.

Even now, snow flurries blow in from the lake, falling like cold confetti.

I walk away from the box, trying not to get my hopes up too high.

This could be any deserted power plant in South Chicago, in countless possible worlds.

As I slowly walk along the line of generators, something shiny on the floor catches my eye.

I go closer.

Lying in a crack in the concrete, six inches from the generator's base: an empty glass vial with its top broken off. In all the deserted power plants I’ve been in during the last month, I’ve never seen one of these.

Maybe the one Jason2 used right before I passed out, the night he stole my life.

—

I hike out of the industrial ghost town.

Hungry, thirsty, exhausted.

The skyline appears to the north, and even though the low winter clouds cut off the top, it’s clearly the one I know.

—

I get on the Red Line heading north at Eighty-Seventh Street as dusk falls.

There are no seat belts, no holograms on this El.

Just a slow, shaky ride through South Chicago.

Then the spread of downtown.

I change trains.

The Blue Line takes me into the updated northern neighborhoods.

For the past month, I've been in similar Chicagos, but this one feels different. It's not just the empty vial. It's something deeper that I can't explain, except to say it feels like where I belong. It feels like mine.

As we pass the jammed rush-hour traffic on the expressway, the snow gets heavier.

I wonder—

Is Daniela, my Daniela, alive and okay under the snowy clouds?

Is my Charlie breathing in this world?

—

I get off the train at the Logan Square El platform and shove my hands into my coat pockets. Snow is sticking to the familiar streets of my neighborhood. To the sidewalks. To the cars parked along the curbs. Headlights from rush-hour traffic cut through the heavy snowfall.

Up and down my block, the houses glow, looking beautiful in the storm.

A thin layer has already gathered on my porch steps, where one set of footprints leads to the door.

Through the front window of the brownstone, I see the lights are on, and from the sidewalk, it looks exactly like home.

I keep expecting to find something small that’s wrong—the wrong front door, the wrong street number, a piece of furniture on the porch I don’t know.

But the door is right.

The street number is right.

There’s even a tesseract chandelier above the dinner table in the front room, and I can almost see the big picture on the mantel—Daniela, Charlie, and me at Inspiration Point in Yellowstone National Park.

Through the open door from the dining room to the kitchen, I see Jason standing at the island, holding a bottle of wine. He reaches over and pours some into someone’s wineglass.

I feel happy all of a sudden, but it doesn’t last.

From where I am, I only see a pretty hand holding the wineglass, and it hits me again what this man did to me.

All that he took.

Everything he stole.

I can’t hear anything out here in the snow, but I see him laugh and take a drink of wine.

What are they talking about?

When was the last time they had sex?

Is Daniela happier now than she was a month ago, with me?

Can I bear to know the answer to that question?

The calm, sensible voice in my head is wisely suggesting that I leave the house right now.

I’m not ready to do this. I have no plan.

Only anger and jealousy.

And I shouldn’t jump to conclusions. I still need more proof that this is my life.

A little ways down the block, I see the familiar back end of our Suburban. Walking over, I brush off the snow that’s sticking to the Illinois license plate.

The license plate number is mine.

The paint is the right color.

I clear the back windshield.

The purple Lakemont Lions sticker looks perfect, even though it’s half ripped off. I instantly regretted putting the sticker on the glass the moment I did it. I tried to tear it off, but only managed to remove the top half of the lion’s face, so all that’s left is a growling mouth.

But that was three years ago.

I need something more recent, more definite.

Several weeks before I was abducted, I accidentally backed the Suburban into a parking meter near campus. It didn’t do much damage besides cracking the right rear taillight and denting in the bumper.

I wipe the snow off the red plastic of the taillight and then the bumper.

I touch the crack.

I touch the dent.

No other Suburban in all the Chicagos I’ve visited has these markings.

Standing up, I look across the street at the bench where I once spent a whole day watching another life I could have had play out. It’s empty now, snow quietly gathering on the seat.

Shit.

A few feet behind the bench, someone watches me in the snowy darkness.

I start walking fast down the sidewalk, probably looking like I was stealing the license plate off the Suburban.

I need to be more careful.

—

The blue neon sign in the Village Tap’s front window flashes in the storm, like a lighthouse signal, telling me I’m almost home.

There’s no Hotel Royale here, so I check into the run-down Days Inn across from my bar.

I can only afford two nights, which leaves me with just over $120.

The business center is a small room down the hall on the first floor, with an almost outdated computer, fax machine, and printer.

Online, I check three things.

Jason Dessen is a physics professor at Lakemont.

Ryan Holder just won the Pavia award for his neuroscience research.
