Daniela Vargas-Dessen isn’t a famous Chicago artist, and she doesn’t have a graphic-design business. Her simple website shows some of her best art and says she teaches art.

As I walk up the stairs to my room on the third floor, I start to believe it.

This is my world.

—

I sit by the window in my hotel room, looking at the flashing neon sign for Village Tap.

I am not a violent person.

I’ve never hit anyone.

Never even tried to.

But if I want my family back, there’s just no other way.

I have to do something awful.

Have to do what Jason2 did to me, but without the easy way out of just putting him back in the box. Even though I have one vial left, I won’t make the same mistake.

He should’ve killed me when he could.

I feel the part of my brain that's a physicist trying to take over.

I’m a scientist, after all. I think in terms of steps and procedures.

So I think of this like a lab experiment.

There’s a result I want.

What steps do I need to take to get there?

First, define what I want to happen.

Get rid of the Jason Dessen who’s living in my home and hide him where no one will ever find him.

What do I need to do this?

A car.

A gun.

Something to tie him up.

A shovel.

A safe place to bury his body.

I hate thinking about this.

Yes, he took my wife, my son, my life, but the thought of planning and hurting him is horrible.

There’s a forest preserve an hour south of Chicago. Kankakee River State Park. I’ve been there several times with Charlie and Daniela, usually in the fall when the leaves are changing and we’re eager for nature and peace and a day out of the city.

I could drive Jason there at night, or make him drive, just like he did to me.

Lead him down one of the trails I know on the north side of the river.

I will have been there a day or two before, so his grave will already be dug in some quiet, hidden spot. I’ll have looked up how deep to make it so animals can’t smell the decay. Make him think he’s going to dig his own grave, so he thinks he has more time to find a way out or to talk me out of doing this. Then, when we’re close to the hole, I’ll drop the shovel and say that it’s time to start digging.

As he bends down to pick it up, I’ll do the thing I can’t imagine.

I will fire a bullet into the back of his head.

Then I’ll drag him over to the hole and roll him into it and cover him up with dirt.

The good news is that no one will be looking for him.

I’ll slide back into his life the same way he slid into mine.

Maybe years later, I’ll tell Daniela the truth.

Maybe I’ll never tell her.

—

The sporting-goods store is three blocks away and still an hour from closing. I used to come here once a year for cleats and balls when Charlie played soccer in middle school.

Even then, the gun counter always interested me.

A mystery.

I could never understand why someone would want to own a gun.

I’ve only shot a gun two or three times, in high school in Iowa. Even then, shooting at rusted oil drums on my best friend’s farm, I didn’t feel the same excitement as the other kids. It scared me too much. As I stood facing the target, aiming the heavy pistol, I couldn’t shake the feeling that I was holding death.

The store is called Field and Glove, and I’m one of three customers this late.

Walking past windbreakers and running shoes, I head toward the counter in the back.

Shotguns and rifles hang on the wall above boxes of ammunition.

Handguns shine under glass at the counter.

Black ones.

Chrome ones.

Ones with cylinders.

Ones without.

Ones that look like they belong in a 1970s action movie with tough cops.

A woman walks over in a black T-shirt and old blue jeans. She looks like Annie Oakley with her frizzy red hair and a tattoo that circles her freckled right arm and says: …the right of the people to keep and bear Arms, shall not be infringed.

“Can I help you?” she asks.

“Yeah, I want to buy a handgun, but I don’t know anything about them.”

“Why do you want one?”

“Home defense.”

She pulls a set of keys from her pocket and unlocks the cabinet I’m standing in front of. I watch her arm reach under the glass and lift out a black pistol.

“So this is a Glock 23. Forty caliber. Made in Austria. Really strong. I could also get you a smaller version if you want something easier to hide.”

“And this will stop an intruder?”

“Oh yeah. This’ll take them down, and they won’t get back up.”

She pulls the slide, checks that it’s empty, then locks it back and takes out the magazine.

“How many bullets does it hold?”

“Thirteen.”

She offers me the gun.

I’m not sure what I should do with it. Aim? Feel how heavy it is?

I hold it awkwardly in my hand, and even though it isn’t loaded, I still feel that same uneasy feeling of holding death.

The price tag hanging from the trigger guard reads $599.99.

I need to figure out my money situation. I could probably go to the bank and use Charlie’s savings account. It had around $4,000 in it the last time I checked. Charlie never uses that account. No one does. If I took out a couple thousand dollars, I doubt anyone would notice. At least, not right away. Of course, I’d need to get a driver’s license somehow first.

“What do you think of it?” she asks.

“Yeah. I mean, it feels like a gun.”

“I could show you a few others. I have a really nice Smith and Wesson .357 if you wanted a revolver.”

“No, this would do fine. I just need to get some money together. What do I need to do for a background check?”

“Do you have a FOID card?”

“What’s that?”

“A firearm owners’ identification card that’s given out by the Illinois State Police. You have to apply for it.”

“How long does that take?”

She doesn’t answer.

Just stares at me oddly, then reaches out and takes the Glock from my hand and puts it back under the glass.

I ask, “Did I say something wrong?”

“You’re Jason, right?”

“How do you know my name?”

“I’ve been standing here trying to figure things out, to make sure I wasn’t crazy. You don’t know my name?”

“No.”

“See, I think you’re kidding me, and it’s not a good—”

“I’ve never spoken to you before. In fact, I haven’t been in this store in probably four years.”

She locks the cabinet and puts the keys back in her pocket.

“I think you should go now, Jason.”

“I don’t understand—”

“If this isn’t a joke, then you’ve got a head injury or Alzheimer’s or you’re just crazy.”

“What are you talking about?”

“You really don’t know?”

“No.”

She leans her elbows on the counter. “Two days ago, you came in here, said you wanted to buy a handgun. I showed you the same Glock. You said it was for protecting your home.”

What does this mean? Is Jason2 getting ready in case I come back, or is he really expecting me?

“Did you sell me a gun?” I ask.

“No, you didn’t have a FOID card. You said you needed to get cash. I don’t think you even had a driver’s license.”

Now I feel a tingle down my back.

My knees get shaky.

She says, “And it wasn’t just two days ago. I felt uneasy about you, so yesterday, I asked Gary, who also works at the gun counter, if he’d ever seen you here before. He had. Three other times in the last week. And now, you’re back.”

I hold onto the counter for support.

“So, Jason, I don’t want you in this store again. Not even to buy a jockstrap. If you come back, I’ll call the police. Do you understand me?”

She looks scared but firm, and I wouldn’t want to mess with her if she thought I was a threat.

I say, “I understand.”

“Get out of my store.”

I step out into the pouring snow, the flakes hitting my face hard, my head spinning.

I glance down the street, see a cab coming. When I raise my arm, it turns toward me, stopping at the curb. I pull open the back door and get in.

“Where to?” the cabbie asks.

Where to.

Good question.

“A hotel, please.”

“Which one?”

“I don’t know. Something close by. Something cheap. You pick it.”

He looks back through the plastic between the front and backseats.

“You want me to pick it?”

“Yes.”

For a moment, I think he won’t. Maybe it’s too strange a request. Maybe he’ll tell me to get out. But instead, he starts the meter and pulls back into traffic.

—

I stare through the window at the snow falling through headlights, taillights, streetlights, flashing lights.

My heart pounding, my thoughts racing.

I need to calm down.

Think about this logically, rationally.

The cab stops in front of a run-down hotel called the End o’ Days.

The cabbie glances back, asks, “Is this okay?”

I pay the fare and head for the front office.

There’s a Bulls game on the radio and a large hotel clerk behind the desk eating Chinese food from many white cartons.

Brushing the snow off my shoulders, I check in using my mother’s father’s name—Jess McCrae.

I pay for one night.

That leaves me with $14.76.

I go up to the fourth floor and lock myself in the room using the deadbolt and the chain.

It feels completely lifeless.

A bed with a sad floral comforter.

Formica table.

Dressers made of particleboard.

At least it’s warm.

I move to the curtains and peek outside.

It’s snowing so hard that the streets are starting to empty, and the pavement is frosting over, showing the tire tracks of passing cars.

I undress and hide my last vial in the Gideon Bible in the bottom drawer of the bedside table.

Then I jump in the shower.

I need to think.

—

I ride the elevator down to the first floor and use my keycard to enter the business center.

I have an idea.

Opening the free email service I use here, I type in the first username that comes to mind.

My name spelled out in Pig Latin: asonjayessenday.

Not surprisingly, someone already took it.

The password is easy to guess.

It’s the one I’ve used for almost everything for the last twenty years—the make, model, and year of my first car: jeepwrangler89.

I try to log in.

It works.

I find myself in a new email account. The inbox has some welcome emails from the provider and one recent email from “Jason” that’s already been opened.

The subject: Welcome Home The Real Jason Dessen

I open it.

The email is empty.

Just a link.

The new page loads and a box pops up on the screen:

Welcome to UberChat!

There are currently three people talking.

Are you new here?

I click Yes.

Your username is Jason9.

I need to make a password before I can log in.

A big window shows the whole conversation.

Some smiley faces to use.

A small box to type and send messages to everyone or private messages to one person.

I scroll to the top of the conversation, which started about eighteen hours ago. The last message was forty minutes ago.

JasonADMIN: I’ve seen some of you around the house. I know there are more of you.

Jason3: Is this really happening?

Jason4: Is this really happening?

Jason6: Crazy.

Jason3: So how many of you went to field & glove?

JasonADMIN: Three days ago.

Jason4: Two.

Jason6: I bought one in South Chicago.

Jason5: You have a gun?

Jason6: Yes.

JasonADMIN: Who thought about Kankakee?

Jason3: Me.

Jason4: Me.

Jason6: I drove there last night and dug a hole. I was ready to go. Had a car, shovel, rope. Planned everything. Tonight, I went to the house to wait for the Jason who did this to us to leave. But then I saw myself behind the Suburban.

Jason8: Why didn’t you do it, jason6?

Jason6: What’s the point? If I got rid of him, one of you would just do the same to me.

Jason3: Did everyone think about what could happen?

Jason4: Yes.

Jason6: Yes.

Jason8: Yes.

JasonADMIN: Yes.

Jason3: So we all know this won’t end well.

Jason4: You could all just kill yourselves and let me have her.

JasonADMIN: I made this chat room and I’m in charge. Five more Jasons are watching, just so you know.

Jason3: Why don’t we all work together and take over the world? Can you imagine what would happen with this many of us working together? (Just kidding…sort of)

Jason6: Can I imagine it? Yeah. They’d put us in a lab and test us forever.

Jason4: Can I just say what we’re all thinking? This is so weird.

Jason5: I have a gun too. None of you tried as hard as I did to get home. None of you saw what I saw.

Jason7: You don't know what we went through.

Jason5: I saw hell. Really. Hell. Where are you, Jason7? I’ve already killed two of us.

Another alert flashes across the screen:

You have a private message from Jason7.

I open the message, my head pounding, about to burst.

I know this is crazy, but do you want to team up? Two heads are better than one. We can work together to take out the others, and when it's all over, I’m sure we can sort things out. We don't have much time. What do you say?

What do I say?

I can barely breathe.

I leave the business center.

Sweat runs down my sides, but I'm freezing.

The hallway on the first floor is empty and quiet.

I rush to the elevator and go up to the fourth floor.

Stepping onto the beige carpet, I hurry down the hall and lock myself in my room.

Spiraling.

How did I not see this coming?

Looking back, it was unavoidable.

I wasn’t jumping into alternate realities in the corridor, but I was in every world I entered. That means other versions of me were created in those worlds of ash, ice, and plague.

The endless nature of the corridor meant I wouldn’t run into many versions of myself, but I did see one—the Jason with his back torn open.

Most of those Jasons were probably killed or lost in other worlds, but some, like me, made the right choices. Or got lucky. Their paths may have changed from mine, through different doors, different worlds, but they eventually found their way back to this Chicago.

We all want the same thing—to get our lives back.

Jesus.

Our life.

Our family.

What if most of these other Jasons are just like me? Good men who want back what they lost. And if that’s true, what right do I have to Daniela and Charlie over the others?

This isn’t just a game of chess. It’s a game of chess against myself.

I don’t want to see it this way, but I can’t help it. The other Jasons want the most precious thing in the world to me—my family. That makes them my enemy. I ask myself what I would do to get my life back. Would I kill another version of me if it meant I could spend the rest of my days with Daniela? Would they?

I picture these other versions of me sitting in their lonely hotel rooms, or walking the snowy streets, or watching my brownstone, struggling with this exact thought.

Asking themselves these same questions.

Trying to guess what their doubles will do next.

There can be no sharing. It’s strictly a competition, a game where only one of us can win.

If anyone is careless, if things get out of control and Daniela or Charlie is hurt or killed, then no one wins. That must be why things seemed normal when I looked inside the front window of my house several hours ago.

No one knows which move to make, so no one has made a move against Jason2.

It’s a classic setup, pure game theory.

A terrifying take on the Prisoner’s Dilemma that asks, Can you outsmart yourself?

I’m not safe.

My family isn’t safe.

But what can I do?

If every move I consider will be predicted or made before I can act, what hope do I have?

I feel like crawling out of my skin.

The worst days in the box—volcanic ash falling on my face, almost freezing, seeing Daniela in a world where she never knew me—none of it compares to the storm inside me right now.

I’ve never felt so lost.

The phone rings, pulling me back to reality.

I walk to the table, pick up the phone on the third ring.

“Hello?”

No answer, just soft breathing.

I hang up.

Go to the window.

Pull back the curtains.

Four floors down, the street is empty, snow still falling hard.

The phone rings again, but just once this time.

Strange.

As I sit back on the bed, the phone call keeps bothering me.

What if another me is trying to make sure I’m in my room?

First, how could he find me at this hotel?

The answer comes quickly, and it’s scary.

Right now, there must be many versions of me in Logan Square doing exactly what he’s doing—calling every motel and hotel in my area to find other Jasons. It’s not luck that he found me. It’s likely to happen. Even a few Jasons, making a dozen calls each, could cover all the hotels near my house.

But would the clerk give out my room number?

Maybe not on purpose, but the guy downstairs watching the Bulls game and eating Chinese food could be tricked.

How would I trick him?

If anyone other than me was looking for me, the name I used to check in would probably keep me hidden. But all these other versions know my grandfather’s name. I messed up there. If using that name was my first thought, it would also be another Jason’s first thought. So if I knew the name I might have used to check in, what would I do next?

The front desk wouldn’t just give out my room number.

I’d have to pretend I knew I was staying here.

I would call the hotel and ask for Jess McCrae’s room.

When I heard my own voice, I'd know I was here and hang up fast.

Then I'd call back half a minute later and tell the clerk, “Sorry to bother you again, but I just called and got cut off. Could you reconnect me to…Oh shoot, what room was that?”

If I was lucky, and the clerk was a clueless fool, he might just blurt out my room number before putting me through.

The first call would confirm it was me who answered.

The second, to get my room number before the caller hung up.

I get out of bed.

The idea is crazy, but I can't shake it.

Is someone coming to kill me?

I put on my wool coat and go to the door.

I feel dizzy with fear, even as I doubt myself, thinking maybe I’m crazy. Maybe I’m jumping to some wild explanation for a normal thing—the phone ringing twice in my room.

Perhaps.

But after that chat room, nothing would shock me.

What if I’m right and ignore my instincts?

Go.

Right now.

I slowly open the door.

Step out into the hall.

It’s empty.

Silent except for the low hum of the fluorescent lights above me.

Stairs or elevator?

At the end of the hall, the elevator bell rings.

I hear the doors slide open, and a man in a wet jacket walks out.

For a moment, I freeze.

I can’t look away.

It’s me walking toward myself.

Our eyes lock.

He isn’t smiling.

His face is cold and intense.

He raises a gun, and I turn and run, racing down the hall to the door, hoping it isn't locked.

I burst out under the glowing Exit sign, glancing back as I enter the stairwell.

My double runs toward me.

Down the steps, my hand sliding along the guardrail to keep steady, thinking, Don’t fall, don’t fall, don’t fall.

As I reach the third-floor landing, I hear the door bang open above me, the echo of his footsteps filling the stairwell.

I keep going down.

Reach the second floor.

Then the first, where one door with a window in the center leads into the lobby and another without a window leads somewhere else.

I choose elsewhere, bursting through…

Into a wall of freezing, snow-filled air.

I stumble down some steps into several inches of fresh snow, my shoes slipping on the icy pavement.

Just as I steady myself, a figure appears from the dark alley between two Dumpsters.

Wearing a coat like mine.

His hair covered in snow.

It’s me.

The knife in his hand flashes light from the streetlamp, and he comes toward me, stabbing the knife toward my stomach—the knife that came with the Velocity Laboratories backpack.

I dodge him just in time, grabbing his arm and throwing him as hard as I can into the steps leading up to the hotel.

He slams into the stairs as the door above us bursts open, and two seconds before I run for my life, I remember the most unbelievable sight: one version of myself stepping out of the stairwell with a gun, the other version getting up off the stairs, frantically searching for his knife, which is lost in the snow.

Are they a team?

Working together to kill every Jason they can find?

I run between the buildings, snow hitting my face, my lungs burning.

Stepping onto the sidewalk of the next street, I glance back down the alley and spot two shadows coming my way.

I walk on through the swirling snow.

The streets are deserted.

Not far off, I hear a sudden burst of noise—people cheering.

I hurry toward it, pushing through a worn, wooden door into a packed dive bar, everyone watching the TVs above the bar where the Bulls are battling the visiting team in the fourth quarter.

I push into the crowd, letting it surround me.

There are no seats, hardly any space to stand, but I manage to squeeze out a tiny spot near a dartboard.

Everyone is focused on the game, but I’m watching the entrance.

The Bulls’ point guard sinks a three-pointer, and the room explodes with cheers, strangers high-fiving and hugging.

The bar door swings open.

I see myself standing in the doorway, covered in snow.

He steps inside.

I lose sight of him briefly, then spot him again as the crowd moves.

What has this Jason Dessen been through? What worlds did he see? What struggles did he endure to get back to this Chicago?

He looks over the crowd.

Behind him, I see the snow falling outside.

His eyes seem hard and cold, but I wonder if he would say the same about me.

As he looks toward where I’m standing at the back of the room, I crouch beneath the dartboard, hidden among the many legs.

I wait a full minute.

When the crowd roars again, I slowly stand up.

The door to the bar is closed now.

My double is gone.

—

The Bulls win.

People stay, happy and drunk.

It takes an hour for a seat to open up at the bar, and since I have nowhere to go, I climb onto a stool and order a light beer that leaves me with less than $10.

I’m starving, but this place doesn’t serve food, so I eat several bowls of Chex mix as I sip my beer.

A drunk man tries to talk to me about the Bulls’ playoff chances, but I just stare into my beer until he insults me and starts bothering two women behind us.

He’s loud and aggressive.

A bouncer appears and pulls him outside.

The crowd shrinks.

As I sit at the bar, trying to block out the noise, one thought keeps hitting me: I need to get Daniela and Charlie away from our house on 44 Eleanor Street. As long as they’re home, the danger of these Jasons doing something crazy remains.

But how?

Jason2 is probably with them right now.

It’s the middle of the night.

Going anywhere near our house is too risky.

I need Daniela to leave, to come to me.

But for every idea I have, another Jason is having it too, or already has, or soon will.

There’s no way for me to win.

As the bar door swings open, I glance over.

Someone who looks like me—backpack, peacoat, boots—walks in, and when we make eye contact, he seems surprised and raises his hands as if to surrender.

Good. Maybe he’s not here for me.

If there are as many Jasons in Logan Square as I think, he probably just came in from the cold, looking for warmth and safety. Like I did.

He walks to the bar and sits on the empty stool next to me, his bare hands shaking from the cold.

Or fear.

The bartender walks over and looks at both of us with curiosity—as if she wants to ask—but she simply says to the new guy, “What can I get you?”

“Whatever he’s having.”

We watch her pour a beer and bring it over, foam running down the sides.

Jason raises his beer.

I lift mine.

We stare at each other.

He has a scar on the right side of his face, like a knife cut.

The thread around his ring finger looks just like mine.

We drink.

“When did you get—?”

“When did you get—?”

We smile.

I say, “This afternoon. You?”

“Yesterday.”

“I have a feeling it’s going to be kind of hard—”

“—not to finish each other’s sentences?”

“Do you know what I’m thinking right now?”

“I can’t read your mind.”

It’s strange—I’m talking to myself, but his voice doesn’t sound like what I think I sound like.

I say, “I’m wondering how long ago you and I split apart. Did you see the world of falling ash?”

“Yes. And then the ice. I barely got away from that one.”

“What about Amanda?” I ask.

“We were separated in the storm.”

I feel a pang of loss like a small explosion in my gut.

I say, “We stayed together in mine. Took shelter in a house.”

“The one that was buried up to the top windows?”

“Exactly.”

“I found that house too. With the dead family inside.”

“So then where—?”

“So then where—?”

“You go,” he says.

As he sips his beer, I ask, “Where did you go after the ice world?”

“I walked out of the box into this guy’s basement. He panicked. He had a gun, tied me up. He probably would have killed me, but he took one of the vials and decided to check out the corridor himself.”

“So he went in and never came out.”

“Exactly.”

“And then?”

His eyes glaze over for a moment.

He takes another long sip of his beer.

“Then I saw some terrible things. Really terrible. Dark worlds. Evil places. What about you?”

I share my story, and though it feels good to get it off my chest, it feels strange to do so with him.

This man and I were the same person until a month ago. Which means almost all of our past is the same.

We’ve said the same things. Made the same choices. Felt the same fears.

The same love.

As he buys our next round of beers, I can’t stop looking at him.

I’m sitting next to myself.

There’s something about him that doesn’t seem quite real.

Maybe it’s because I’m watching from an impossible place—looking at myself from outside myself.

He looks strong, but also tired, hurt, and scared.

It’s like talking to a friend who knows everything about you, but there’s an added layer of painful familiarity. Except for the last month, there are no secrets between us. He knows every bad thing I’ve done. Every thought I’ve had. My weaknesses. My secret fears.

“We call him Jason2,” I say, “which means we think of ourselves as Jason1. As the original. But we can’t both be Jason1. And there are others out there who think they’re the original.”

“None of us are.”

“No. We’re pieces of something bigger.”

“Facets,” he says. “Some very similar, like I think you and I are. Some completely different.”

I say, “It makes you think about yourself differently, doesn’t it?”

“Makes me wonder, who is the perfect Jason? Does he even exist?”

“All you can do is be the best you can be, right?”

“You said it.”

The bartender calls for last orders.

I say, “Not many people can say they’ve done this.”

“What? Have a beer with themselves?”

“Yeah.”

He finishes his beer.

I finish mine.

Getting off his stool, he says, “I’ll go first.”

“Which way are you going?”

He hesitates. “North.”

“I won’t follow you. Can I expect the same?”

“Yes.”

“We can’t both have them.”

He says, “The question is who should get them, and there may be no answer. But if it’s you or me, I won’t let you keep me from Daniela and Charlie. I won’t want to, but I’ll kill you if I have to.”

“Thanks for the beer, Jason.”

I watch him go.

Wait five minutes.

I’m the last to leave.

It’s still snowing.

There’s a half foot of fresh snow on the streets, and the snowplows are out.

Stepping onto the sidewalk, I pause to take it all in.

Some customers from the bar are stumbling away, but the streets seem deserted.

I don’t know where to go.

I truly have nowhere to go.

Two working hotel keycards in my pocket, but using either would be dangerous. Other Jasons could have easily made copies. They could be in my room right now, waiting for me.

It hits me—my last ampoule is back at that second hotel.

Lost now.

I start walking down the sidewalk.

It’s two in the morning, and I’m running on empty.

How many other Jasons are walking these streets right now, feeling the same fears, asking the same questions?

How many have died?

How many are out searching?

I can’t shake the feeling that I’m not safe in Logan Square, even at night. Every alley I pass, every dark doorway, I’m watching for movement, for someone chasing me.

A half mile gets me to Humboldt Park.

I walk through the snow.

Out into a quiet field.

I’m more than tired.

My legs hurt.

My stomach rumbles with hunger.

I can’t go on.

A large evergreen stands tall in the distance, its branches heavy with snow.

The lowest branches are four feet off the ground, but they offer some shelter from the storm.

Near the trunk, there’s only a little snow. I brush it away and sit in the dirt against the tree on the side away from the wind.

It’s so quiet.

I can hear the faint sound of snowplows in the city.

The sky is bright pink from the lights reflecting off the low clouds.

I pull my coat tighter and make fists with my hands to keep warm.

From where I sit, I see an open field with trees scattered around.

The snow falls through the streetlights on a path far away, making bright circles of flakes near the light.

Nothing moves out there.

It’s cold, but not as bad as it could be if the skies were calm and clear.

I don’t think I’m going to freeze to death.

But I don’t think I’m going to sleep either.

As I shut my eyes, an idea hits me.

Randomness.

How do you beat someone who is built to predict everything you might do?

You do something completely random.

Unplanned.

You make a move you haven’t thought about much before.

Maybe it’s a bad move that backfires and loses you the game.

But maybe it’s a play the other you didn’t expect, giving you a surprise advantage.

So how do I use that idea in my own life?

How do I do something totally random that no one expects?

—

Somehow, I fall asleep.

I wake up cold to a gray and white world.

The snow and wind have stopped. Through the bare trees, I see bits of the skyline far away, the tallest buildings just reaching the clouds above the city.

The open field is white and quiet.

It’s dawn.

The streetlamps fade.

I sit up, incredibly stiff.

There’s a light dusting of snow on my coat.

My breath shows in the cold.

Of all the Chicagos I’ve known, none match the peace of this morning.

Where the empty streets keep everything quiet.

Where the sky is white and the ground is white and the buildings and the trees stand out against it all.

I think of the seven million people still in bed or looking out their windows at what the storm left.

Something so safe and comforting in imagining it.

I get to my feet with effort.

I woke up with a wild idea.

Something that happened in the bar last night, just before the other Jason arrived, gave me the idea. It's not something I would have ever come up with myself, which almost makes me believe in it.

Going back across the park, I walk north toward Logan Square.

Toward home.

—

At the first convenience store I find, I go in and buy one Swisher Sweets cigar and a small BIC lighter.

$8.21 left.

—

My coat is wet from the snow.

I hang it at the rack by the entrance and head down the counter.

This place feels wonderfully real, like it’s always been here. The 1950s feel isn’t just from the red vinyl on the booths and stools or the framed photos of regulars on the walls over the years. It comes, I think, from never changing. The smell is all bacon grease and brewing coffee and faint memories of a time when I would’ve walked through clouds of cigarette smoke to get to a table.

Besides a few customers at the counter, I see two cops in one booth, three nurses just done with their shift in another, and an old man in a black suit staring with a bored focus into his coffee.

I sit at the counter just to feel the heat from the open grill.

An old waitress comes over.

I know I must look homeless and worn-out, but she doesn’t show it, doesn’t judge, just takes my order with a tired, polite manner.

It feels good to be inside.

The windows are fogging up.

The cold is leaving my bones.

This all-night diner is only eight blocks from my house, but I’ve never eaten here.

When the coffee comes, I wrap my dirty fingers around the ceramic mug and soak in the warmth.

I had to figure it out ahead of time.

All I can afford is this cup of coffee, two eggs, and some toast.

I try to eat slowly, to make it last, but I’m starving.

The waitress feels sorry for me and brings more toast for free.

She’s nice.

It makes me feel even worse about what’s going to happen.

I check the time on my drug-dealer cell phone, the one I bought to call Daniela in another Chicago. It won’t make calls here—I guess minutes don’t work across different worlds.

8:15 a.m.

Jason2 probably left for work twenty minutes ago to catch the train to his 9:30 lecture.

Or maybe he hasn’t left at all. Maybe he’s sick, or staying home today for some reason I didn’t expect. That would be awful, but it’s too risky to go anywhere near my house to check if he’s there.

I take the $8.21 out of my pocket and put it on the counter.

It barely covers my breakfast and a small tip.

I take one last sip of coffee.

Then I reach into the pocket of my flannel shirt and take out the cigar and the lighter.

I look around.

The diner is now full.

The two cops who were here when I got here are gone, but there’s another one sitting in the corner booth at the far end.

My hands shake slightly as I tear open the wrapper.

Like it says on the label, the end of the cigar tastes a little sweet.

It takes me three tries to light the match.

I light the end of the cigar, take a puff, and blow smoke toward the cook who's making pancakes.

For ten seconds, no one notices.

Then the older woman next to me in a jacket covered in cat hair turns and says, “You can’t do that in here.”

And I say something I would never even imagine saying: “But a cigar is great after a meal.”

She looks at me like I’m crazy through her thick glasses.

The waitress comes over with a coffee pot, looking very disappointed.

Shaking her head, she says like a scolding mother, “You know you can’t smoke that in here.”

“But it’s so good.”

“Do I need to get the manager?”

I take another puff.

Exhale.

The cook—a big, muscular guy with tattooed arms—turns and glares at me.

I say to the waitress, “That’s a great idea. You should go get the manager now, because I am not putting this out.”

As the waitress leaves, the old woman next to me, whose meal I’ve ruined, mutters, “What a rude young man.”

And she throws down her fork, gets off the stool, and heads for the door.

Some of the other customers near me have started to notice.

But I keep smoking, until a skinny man comes from the back of the restaurant with the waitress. He wears black jeans and a white shirt with sweat stains and a plain tie that’s coming undone.

By how messy he looks, I’m guessing he’s worked all night.

Stopping behind me, he says, “I’m Nick, the manager. You can’t smoke that inside. You’re bothering the customers.”

I turn a bit on my stool and look him in the eye. He seems tired and bothered, and I feel awful making him deal with this, but I can't quit now.

I look around; everyone's staring, like a pancake burning on the stove.

I ask, “Is my cigar bothering you all?”

Yeses everywhere.

Someone calls me a jerk.

Something moving at the back of the diner gets my attention.

Finally.

The police officer comes out of the corner booth, and as he walks toward me down the aisle, I hear his radio buzz.

He's young.

Maybe late twenties.

Short and sturdy.

His eyes held a Marine's toughness, and intelligence too.

The manager steps back, relieved.

Now the officer is next to me, saying, “We have a clean indoor air law in the city, which you're breaking right now.”

I take another puff of the cigar.

The cop says, “Look, I’ve been up all night. So have many of these other customers. Why do you want to spoil everyone’s breakfast?”

“Why do you want to spoil my cigar?”

A flash of anger crosses the cop’s face.

His pupils get bigger.

“Put that cigar out now. Last warning.”

“Or what?”

He sighs.

“That’s not what I wanted to hear. Get up.”

“Why?”

“Because you’re going to jail. If that cigar isn’t out in five seconds, I’ll think you’re fighting me, which means I can be a lot rougher.”

I drop my cigar in my coffee cup, and as I step off the stool, the officer quickly pulls handcuffs off his belt and locks them on my wrists.

“Do you have any weapons or needles? Anything that could hurt me or that I should know about?”

“No, sir.”

“Are you on any drugs or medication right now?”

“No, sir.”

He pats me down, then takes my arm.

As we walk toward the entrance, the other customers clap.

His police car is parked right out front.

He opens the back door and tells me to mind my head.

It’s almost impossible to smoothly get into the back of a police car with your hands cuffed behind you. The officer gets in behind the wheel.

Buckling his seat belt, he starts the engine and pulls out onto the snowy street.

The backseat seems to have been made especially to be uncomfortable. There’s no legroom at all, my knees are jammed into the cage, and the seats are made of a hard plastic material that feels like I’m sitting on concrete.

As I stare through the bars that cover the window, I watch the familiar buildings of my neighborhood go by, wondering if this has any chance of working.

—

We pull into the parking garage of the 14th District Police Station.

Officer Hammond pulls me out of the backseat and leads me through steel doors into a booking room.

There’s a row of desks, with chairs for prisoners on one side and a clear plastic wall that separates them from a workstation on the other.

The room smells like vomit and desperation, with a strong smell of cleaner trying to hide it.

This early in the morning, there’s only one other prisoner besides me—a woman at the far end of the room, chained to a desk. She’s rocking wildly, scratching herself, twitching.

Hammond searches me again, then tells me to sit down.

He unlocks the bracelet on my left wrist, cuffs it to a hook in the desk, and says, “I need to see your driver’s license.”

“I lost it.”

He writes this down and then goes to the other side of the desk and logs in to the computer.

He asks for my name.

Social Security number.

Address.

Employer.

I ask, “What am I accused of?”

“Disorderly conduct and disturbing the peace.”

Hammond starts filling out the arrest report.

After a few minutes, he stops typing and looks at me through the scratched Plexi. “You don’t seem crazy or mean. You don’t have a record. You’ve never been in trouble. So what happened? It’s like…you were trying to get arrested. Anything you want to tell me?”

“No. I’m sorry I ruined your breakfast.”

He shrugs. “I’ll get more.”

I’m fingerprinted.

Photographed.

They take my shoes and give me slippers and a blanket.

When he's done putting me in the system, I ask, "When can I use the phone?"

"You can call now." He picks up the phone. "Who do you want to call?"

"My wife."

I tell him the number and watch him dial.

When it rings, he gives me the phone over the wall.

My heart is racing.

Answer, please. Come on.

Voicemail.

I hear my voice, but it's not my message. Did Jason rerecord it to show it's his now?

I say to Officer Hammond, “She’s not answering. Could you hang up, please?”

He ends the call right before the beep.

“Daniela probably didn’t know the number. Would you mind trying again?”

He dials again.

It rings again.

I’m wondering—if she doesn’t answer, should I risk leaving a message?

No.

What if Jason2 heard it? If she doesn’t answer this time, I’ll have to find another way to—

“Hello?”

“Daniela.”

“Jason?”

Tears sting my eyes at the sound of her voice. “Yeah, it’s me.”

“Where are you calling from? It says Chicago Police on the phone. I thought it was one of those police donation calls, so I didn’t—”

“I just need you to listen for a minute.”

“Is everything okay?”

“Something happened on my way to work. I’ll explain everything when—”

“Are you okay?”

“I’m fine, but I’m in jail.”

For a moment, it gets so quiet on the other end of the line that I can hear the NPR show she’s listening to in the background.

She says finally, “You got arrested?”

“Yeah.”

“For what?”

“I need you to bail me out.”

“Jesus. What did you do?”

“Look, I don’t have time to explain now. This is like my one phone call.”

“Should I call a lawyer?”

“No, just get here fast. I’m at the Fourteenth District Precinct on…” I look at Hammond for the street address.

“North California Avenue.”

“North California. And bring your checkbook. Has Charlie left for school yet?”

“Yeah.”

“I want you to get him and bring him with you when you come to pick me up. This is very—”

“Absolutely not.”

“Daniela—”

“I am not bringing my son to get his father out of jail. What the hell happened, Jason?”

Officer Hammond knocks on the glass and makes a cutthroat gesture.

I say, “My time’s up. Please get here as soon as you can.”

“Okay.”

“Honey.”

“What?”

“I love you so much.”

She hangs up.

—

My small, bare cell has a thin mattress on a concrete base.

Toilet.

Sink.

Camera above the door, watching me.

I lie in bed with the jail blanket over me and stare at a spot on the ceiling that I bet has been stared at by many people feeling sad, lost, and regretting their choices.

I keep thinking about all the things that could go wrong, things that could easily stop Daniela from coming to me.

She could call Jason2 on his cell phone.

He could call her between classes just to say hi.

Another Jason might act.

If any of that occurs, my whole plan will fail completely.

My stomach aches.

My heart pounds.

I try to relax, but I can’t stop being afraid.

I wonder if any of my doubles have guessed my plan. I want to believe they haven’t. If I hadn’t seen that loud drunk at the bar last night, rudely bothering those women and getting kicked out by the bouncer, I wouldn’t have thought to get arrested to make Daniela and Charlie come to me somewhere safe.

This decision came from a unique thing that only happened to me.

But, I might be wrong.

I could be wrong about everything.

I get up, walk back and forth between the toilet and the bed, but there’s not much room in this small cell, and the more I walk, the closer the walls seem to get until I really feel how cramped this room is as my chest tightens.

It’s getting harder to breathe.

I finally move to the small window at eye level in the door.

I look through it into a sterile white hallway.

The sound of a woman crying in a nearby cell bounces off the concrete walls.

She sounds so hopeless.

I wonder if it’s the same woman I saw in the booking room when I got here.

A guard walks by, holding another inmate by the arm.

I go back to the bed, curl up under the blanket, face the wall, and try not to think, but I can’t.

It feels like hours have gone by.

Why is this taking so long?

I can only think of one reason.

Something occurred.

She won’t be here.

—

The door to my cell unlocks with a mechanical jerk that makes my heart race.

I sit up.

The young guard in the doorway says, “You’re free to go home, Mr. Dessen. Your wife just paid your bail.”

—

He takes me back to the booking room, where I sign some forms without reading them.

They give me back my shoes and lead me through several hallways.

As I push through the doors at the end of the last hallway, my breath catches in my throat and my eyes fill with tears.

Of all the places I imagined our reunion finally happening, the lobby of the 14th District Precinct wasn’t one of them.

Daniela rises from her chair.

Not a Daniela who doesn’t know me, or is married to another man, or another version of me.

My Daniela.

The one, the only.

She’s wearing the shirt she sometimes paints in—a faded blue button-down spattered with oil and acrylic—and when she sees me her face twists with confusion and disbelief.

I rush to her across the lobby, wrapping my arms around her, and she’s saying my name, saying it like something isn’t right, but I don’t let go, because I can’t let go. Thinking—the worlds I’ve come through, the things I’ve done, endured, suffered, to get back into the arms of this woman.

I can’t believe how good it feels to touch her.

To breathe the same air.

To smell her.

Feel my skin tingle against hers.

I hold her face in my hands.

I kiss her lips.

Those lips—so incredibly soft.

But she pulls back.

And then pushes me away, her hands on my chest, her brow deeply wrinkled.

“They said you were arrested for smoking a cigar in a restaurant, and that you wouldn’t…” She loses her thought. She looks closely at my face like something is wrong, her fingers brushing my two-week-old beard. Of course something is wrong—it’s not the face she saw this morning. “You didn’t have a beard this morning, Jason.” She looks me up and down. “You’re so thin.” She touches my torn, dirty shirt. “You didn’t leave the house in these clothes.”

I can see her trying to understand and failing.

“Did you bring Charlie?” I ask.

“No. I told you I wasn’t going to. Am I crazy or—?”

“You’re not crazy.”

Gently, I take her arm and lead her to a couple of chairs in a small waiting area.

I say, “Let’s sit down for a minute.”

“I don’t want to sit, I want you to—”

“Please, Daniela.”

We sit.

“Do you trust me?” I ask.

“I don’t know. This is all…scaring me.”

“I’ll explain everything, but first I need you to call a cab.”

“My car is parked two blocks—”

“We’re not walking to your car.”

“Why?”

“It’s not safe out there for us.”

“What are you talking about?”

“Daniela, will you please just trust me on this?”

I think she’s going to object, but instead she takes out her phone, opens an app, and orders a car.

Looking up at me finally, she says, “Done. It’s three minutes away.”

I glance around the lobby.

The officer who walked me here from the booking room is gone, and right now, we’re the only people here besides the woman at the welcome window. But she’s sitting behind a thick wall of glass, so I’m pretty sure she can’t hear us.

I look at Daniela.

I say, “What I’m about to tell you will sound crazy. You’re going to think I’m out of my mind, but I’m not. Remember Ryan’s party at Village Tap? For winning that award?”

“Yeah. That was more than a month ago.”

“When I walked out of our house that night, that’s the last time I saw you, until five minutes ago when I walked in.”

“Jason, I’ve seen you every day since then.”

“That man isn’t me.”

Her face darkens.

“What are you talking about?”

“He’s another version of me.”

She just stares into my eyes, blinking.

“Is this a trick? Or a game you’re playing? Because—”

“Not a trick. Not a game.”

I take her phone and check the time. “It’s 12:18. I have office hours now.”

I type my campus direct line and give Daniela the phone.

It rings twice, then I hear my voice say, “Hi, beautiful. I was just thinking about you.”

Daniela’s mouth opens slowly.

She looks sick.

I put it on speaker and mouth, “Say something.”

She says, “Hey. How’s your day going?”

“Great. Finished my lecture, and now I’m meeting students over lunch. Everything okay?”

“Um, yeah. I just…wanted to hear your voice.”

I grab the phone from her and mute it.

Jason says, “I can’t stop thinking about you.”

I look at Daniela, say, “Tell him you’ve been thinking, and that since we had such an amazing time in the Keys last Christmas, you want to go back.”

“We didn’t go to the Keys last Christmas.”

“I know, but he doesn’t. I want to prove he’s not the man you think he is.”

My look-alike says, “Daniela? Am I still there?”

She unmutes the phone. “No, I’m right here. So, the real reason I called—”

“Wasn’t just to hear my sweet voice?”

“I was thinking about our trip to the Keys for Christmas last year, and how much fun we had. I know money’s tight, but what if we went back?”

Jason doesn’t miss a beat.

“Absolutely. Whatever you want, my love.”

Daniela stares into my eyes as she says into the phone, “Do you think we can get the same house we had? The pink-and-white one that was right on the beach? It was so perfect.”

Her voice cracks on the last word, and I think she’s about to fall apart, but she somehow manages to keep it together.

“We’ll make it work,” he says.

The phone begins to shake in her hand.

I want to tear him slowly apart.

Jason says, “Honey, someone’s waiting out in the hall to see me, so I better go.”

“Okay.”

“I’ll see you tonight.”

No you won’t.

“See you tonight, Jason.”

She ends the call.

Reaching down, I squeeze her hand and say, “Look at me.”

She looks lost, confused.

I say, “I know your head is spinning right now.”

“How can you be at Lakemont and also sitting here right in front of me at the same moment?”

Her phone beeps.

A message appears on the touchscreen, saying our car is arriving.

I say, “I’ll explain everything, but right now we need to get in this car and pick our son up from school.”

“Is Charlie in danger?”

“We all are.”

That seems to pull her back to reality.

I stand and help her up from the chair.

We walk across the lobby toward the precinct entrance.

A black Escalade is parked at the curb, a short distance away.

Going through the doors, I guide Daniela along the sidewalk to the waiting SUV.

There's no sign of last night’s storm, at least not in the sky. A strong north wind has swept away the clouds, leaving a bright winter day behind.

I open the back door and get in after Daniela, who tells the driver, dressed in black, the address of Charlie’s school.

“Please get there as fast as you can,” she says.

The windows are darkly tinted, and as we drive away from the station, I look at Daniela and say, “Text Charlie, tell him we’re coming, so he’s ready.”

She flips her phone over, but her hands are shaking too much to write a text.

“Here, I’ll do it.”

I take her phone, open the messaging app, and find her last conversation with Charlie.

I type:

Dad and I are coming to get you from school now. No time to sign you out, just go to the bathroom and come out front. We’ll be in the black Escalade. See you in 10.

Our driver leaves the parking lot and turns onto a street cleared of snow, the road drying in the bright winter sun.

A few blocks later, we pass Daniela’s navy Honda.

Two cars ahead, I see a man who looks just like me driving a white van.

I look through the back window.

There’s a car behind us, but it’s too far for me to see who’s driving.

“What is it?” Daniela asks.

“I want to make sure no one’s following us.”

“Who would be following us?”

Her phone vibrates with a new text, which stops me from having to answer that question.

CHARLIE now

Everything ok?

I reply:

All good. I’ll explain when we see you.

Putting my arm around Daniela, I pull her in close.

She says, “I feel like I’m in a nightmare and I can’t wake up. What’s happening?”

“We’ll go somewhere safe,” I whisper. “Where we can talk alone. Then I’ll tell you and Charlie everything.”

—

Charlie’s school is a big brick building that looks like a mix of a mental hospital and a steampunk castle.

He’s sitting on the front steps when we pull into the pickup lane, looking at his phone.

I tell Daniela to wait, and then I get out of the car and walk toward my son.

He stands, confused that I’m coming.

At how I look.

I hug him hard and say, “God, I’ve missed you,” before I even think about it.

“What are you doing here?” he asks. “What’s with the car?”

“Come on, we have to go.”

“Where?”

But I just grab his arm and pull him to the open passenger door of the Escalade.

He gets in first, and I follow, shutting the door.

The driver looks back and asks with a thick Russian accent, “Where to now?”

I thought about it on the drive from the police station—somewhere big and busy, where we could easily disappear into the crowd if one of the other Jasons followed us. Now I doubt that choice. I think of three other options—Lincoln Park Conservatory, the Willis Tower observation deck, and Rosehill Cemetery. Rosehill feels safest, most unexpected. And I also like Willis and Lincoln Park. So I ignore my gut and go back to my first idea.

I tell him, “Water Tower Place.”

We ride into the city in silence.

As the downtown buildings get closer, Daniela’s cell phone vibrates.

She looks at the screen and hands it to me so I can see the text she just got.

It’s a 773 number I don’t know.

Daniela looks terrified.

The air inside the car feels electric.

Our driver turns onto Michigan Avenue, which is jammed with lunchtime traffic.

The old, yellow stone of the Chicago Water Tower appears in the distance, made small by the tall buildings around it on the wide street of the Magnificent Mile.

The Escalade stops at the main entrance, but I ask the driver to let us out in the underground parking area instead.

From Chestnut Street, we go down into the darkness of a parking garage.

Four levels down, I tell him to stop at the next set of elevators.

As far as I can see, no other cars have come in after us.

The sounds of our doors closing loudly bounce off the concrete walls and posts as the SUV drives away.

Water Tower Place is a tall mall, with eight floors of small and fancy stores around a chrome and glass center.

We go up to the mezzanine, where all the restaurants are, and get off the glass elevator.

The snow has brought many people inside.

Right now, I feel completely unknown.

We find a bench in a quiet corner, away from the people walking by.

Sitting between Daniela and Charlie, I think of all the other Jasons in Chicago who would do anything, even kill, to be where I am.

I take a breath.

Where should I even start?

I look Daniela in the eye and move a strand of hair behind her ear.

I look into Charlie’s eyes.

I tell them how much I love them.

That I've been through so much to be sitting here between them.

I start with when I was taken on a cold October night, forced to drive at gunpoint to an empty power plant in South Chicago.

I tell them about my fear, how I thought I was going to be killed, about waking up in a strange science lab, where people I didn't know seemed to know me and were waiting for me.

They listen closely as I explain how I escaped from Velocity Laboratories that first night, and came back to our house on Eleanor Street, to a home that wasn't my home, where I lived alone as a man who had chosen to focus on his research.

A world where Daniela and I had never married and Charlie was never born.

I tell Daniela about meeting someone who looked just like her at the art show in Bucktown.

Being captured and held in the lab.

Escaping with Amanda into the box.

I describe the multiverse.

Every door I walked through.

Every ruined world.

Every Chicago that was a little off, but got me closer to home.

There are things I leave out.

Things I can’t quite say yet.

The two nights with Daniela after the art show opening.

The two times I saw her die.

I’ll share these moments later, when it feels right.

I try to think how Daniela and Charlie must feel hearing this story.

When Daniela starts to cry, I ask, “Do you believe me?”

“Of course I believe you.”

“Charlie?”

My son nods, but his eyes show he's far away. He’s staring blankly at the shoppers walking by, and I wonder how much of what I’ve said has actually registered.

How can anyone even start to understand something like this?

Daniela wipes her eyes and says, “I just want to make sure I understand exactly what you’re saying. So the night you went to Ryan Holder’s party, this other Jason stole your life? He took you into the box and left you in his world so he could live in this one? With me?”

“That’s what I’m telling you.”

“That means the man I’ve been living with is a stranger.”

“Not completely. I think he and I were the same person until fifteen years ago.”

“What happened fifteen years ago?”

“You told me you were pregnant with Charlie. The multiverse exists because every choice we make creates a split, which leads to another world. That night you told me you were pregnant didn’t just happen the way you and I remember it. It happened in many different ways. In one world, the one we live in now, you and I decided to build a life together. We got married. Had Charlie. Made a home. In another, I decided that becoming a father in my late twenties wasn’t right for me. I worried my work would be lost, that I wouldn't reach my goals.”

So there’s a version of our life where we didn’t keep the baby. Charlie. You went after your art. I went after my science. And eventually, we split up. That man, the version of me you’ve been living with for the last month—he built the box.”

“Which is a big version of that thing you were working on when we first met—the cube?”

“Exactly. And at some point, he realized everything he’d lost by letting his work be his whole life. He regretted the choice he made fifteen years ago. But the box can’t take you back or forward in time. It only connects all possible worlds right now, in the present. So he searched until he found my world. And he traded my life for his.”

The look on Daniela’s face is complete shock and disgust.

She gets up from the bench and runs toward the restrooms.

Charlie starts after her, but I put my hand on his shoulder and say, “Just give her a minute.”

“I knew something was off.”

“What do you mean?” I ask.

“You—well, not you, him —he had this different kind of energy. We talked more, especially at dinner. He was just, I don’t know…”

“What?”

“Different.”

There are things I want to ask my son, questions burning in my mind.

Was he more fun?

A better father?

A better husband?

Was life more exciting with the fake?

But I’m afraid the answers might break me.

Daniela comes back.

So pale.

As she sits down, I ask, “Are you okay?”

"I have a question for you."

"What?"

"This morning, when you got arrested—was it to make me come to you?"

"Yes."

"Why? Why not just come to the house after…God, I don’t even know what to call him."

"Jason2."

"After Jason2 left?"

I say, "This is where it gets really wild."

Charlie asks, "Isn't it already wild?"

"I wasn't the only..." It sounds crazy just saying it.

But I have to tell them.

“What?” Daniela asks.

“I wasn’t the only me to return to this world.”

“What does that mean?” she asks.

“Other Jasons also made it back.”

“What other Jasons?”

“Versions of me who escaped into the box in that lab, but went different ways through the multiverse.”

“How many?” Charlie asks.

“I don’t know. Maybe a lot.”

I explain what happened at the sporting-goods store and in the chat room. I tell them about the Jason who followed me to my room and the one who attacked me with a knife.

My family’s confusion turns into clear fear.

I say, “This is why I got arrested. For all I know, many Jasons have been watching you, following you, tracking everything you do to figure out what to do. I needed you to come to me in a safe place. That’s why I had you call the car service. I know at least one version of me followed you to the police station. I saw him as we drove past your Honda. This is why I wanted you to bring Charlie with you. But it doesn’t matter. We’re here together, and safe, and now you both know the truth.”

It takes Daniela a moment to speak.

She says softly, “These other…Jasons…what are they like?”

“What are you asking?”

“Do they all have your past? Are they basically you?”

“Yes. Until I stepped into the multiverse. Then we all went different ways, had different experiences.”

“But some are just like you? Versions of my husband who have fought hard to get back to this world. Who just want to be with me again. With Charlie.”

“Yeah.”

Her eyes narrow.

What must this be like for her?

I can see her trying to understand how this could even be possible.

“Dani, look at me.”

I stare into her bright eyes.

I say, “I love you.”

“I love you too. But so do the others, right? Just as much as you do.”

It guts me to hear those words.

I have no response.

I look at the people close to us, wondering if we’re being watched.

The balcony has gotten more crowded since we sat down.

I see a woman pushing a stroller.

Young lovers walking slowly through the mall, holding hands and ice-cream cones, lost in their happiness.

An old man shuffling along behind his wife, with a look on his face that says, Take me home, please.

We’re not safe here.

We’re not safe anywhere in this city.

I ask, “Are you with me?”

She hesitates, looks at Charlie.

Then back at me.

“Yeah,” she says. “I’m with you.”

“Good.”

"So, what should we do now?"

Chapter 14

We leave with just the clothes we're wearing and a bank envelope full of cash from our empty checking and savings accounts. Daniela uses our credit card for the rental car, but after this, we'll only use cash to make it harder to find us.

By midafternoon, we’re driving through Wisconsin.

Rolling pasture

Small hills.

Red barns.

Silos create a simple skyline.

Smoke floats from farmhouse chimneys.

Everything shines under a new layer of snow, and the sky is a bright winter blue.

We’re moving slowly, but I avoid the highways.

Stay on the back roads.

Take turns without planning, going nowhere special.

When we stop for gas, Daniela shows me her phone. There are many missed calls and new texts, all from Chicago phone numbers.

I open the messaging app.

Dani—It’s Jason, please call me back at this number right away.

Daniela, this is Jason. First, I love you. I have so much to tell you. Please call me as soon as you see this.

Daniela, you're probably hearing from other Jasons already. You must be confused. I am yours. You are mine. I love you forever. Call me the second you get this.

Daniela, the Jason you’re with is a fake. Call me.

Daniela, you and Charlie are in danger. The Jason you’re with isn’t who you think he is. Call me now.

None of them love you like I do. Call me, Daniela. Please. I’m begging you. Love you.

I will kill them all for you and make this right. Just say the word. I will do anything for you.

I stop reading, block each number, and delete the messages.

But one text stands out to me.

It’s not from a number I don’t know.

It’s from Jason.

My own phone number. He’s had my phone the whole time. Since the night he took me from the street.

You’re not home, not answering your phone. You must know. All I can say is that I love you. That’s why. My time with you has been the best of my life. Please call me. Hear me out.

I power off her phone and tell Charlie to turn his off too. “We have to keep them off,” I say. “From now on. They could find us if the phones are on.”

As the afternoon turns into evening and the sun starts to set, we drive into the huge Northwoods.

The road is empty.

Just ours.

We’ve gone on many summer trips to Wisconsin but never this far north. And never in winter. We drive for miles without seeing any signs of people, and each town we pass is smaller than the last—just a crossroads in the middle of nowhere.

A heavy silence has filled the Jeep Cherokee, and I don’t know how to break it.

Or if I’m brave enough to.

All your life you’re told you’re special. That you’re one of a kind. That no one else is exactly like you.

It’s what everyone believes.

But that's not true for me anymore.

How can Daniela love me more than the other Jasons?

I look at her in the passenger seat, wondering what she thinks of me now, what she feels for me.

Hell, what I think of myself is even a question.

She sits quietly next to me, just watching the forest fly by outside.

I reach across the center console and hold her hand.

She looks at me, and then back out the window.

—

As dusk falls, I drive into a town called Ice River, which feels suitably far away.

We grab some fast food and then stop at a grocery store to get food and basics.

Chicago never ends.

There's no room to breathe, even in the suburbs.

But Ice River does end.

One moment we’re in town, driving by an empty strip mall with boarded-up shops. The next, the buildings and lights are fading in the mirror, and we’re driving through forest and darkness, the headlights shining brightly through a narrow path of tall pines close to the road.

Pavement flows under the lights.

We don't pass any cars.

I take the third turn, a little over a mile north of town, onto a narrow, snowy road that twists through spruce and birch trees to the tip of a small peninsula.

After a few hundred yards, the headlights hit the front of a log house that looks exactly like what I want.

Like most houses on the lake in this area, it’s dark and looks empty.

Closed up for the winter.

I stop the Cherokee in the circular drive and turn off the engine.

It’s very dark, very quiet.

I look at Daniela.

I say, “I know you don’t like the idea, but breaking in is safer than renting a place and leaving a record.”

She’s hardly spoken the entire six-hour drive from Chicago.

Like she's in shock.

She says, “I understand. We’re past breaking-and-entering now, right?”

I open the door and step into a foot of fresh snow.

The cold is sharp.

The air is still.

One of the bedroom windows isn’t latched, so I don’t even have to break glass.

—

We carry the plastic grocery bags up onto the covered porch.

It’s freezing inside.

I hit the lights.

Straight ahead, a staircase goes up into the darkness of the second floor.

Charlie says, “This place is gross.”

It isn’t gross so much as it smells like mold and being ignored.

A vacation home when it’s not vacation time.

We carry our bags into the kitchen and drop them on the counter and wander through the house.

The inside is somewhere between cozy and old-fashioned.

The appliances are old and white.

The kitchen linoleum is cracked, and the hardwood floors are scuffed and creaky.

In the living room, a big bass is mounted over the brick fireplace, and the walls are covered with framed fishing lures—at least a hundred.

There’s a master bedroom downstairs and two bedrooms upstairs, one of them packed with triple bunk beds.

We eat Dairy Queen out of greasy paper bags.

The kitchen light shines harshly on the table, but the rest of the house is dark.

The heater can barely warm the place up enough.

Charlie looks cold.

Daniela is quiet and far away.

Like she's slowly falling into a dark place.

She hardly eats.

After dinner, Charlie and I carry in piles of wood from the porch, and I use our fast-food bags and a newspaper to start the fire.

The wood is dry and gray, from seasons ago, and it catches fire quickly.

Soon the living room walls are glowing.

Shadows flicker on the ceiling.

We open the sofa bed for Charlie and put it near the fireplace.

Daniela goes to get our room ready.

I sit by Charlie on the edge of the mattress, letting the fire's heat warm me.

I say, "If you wake up, put another log on the fire. Maybe we can keep it burning until morning, and warm this place."

He kicks off his sneakers and pulls his arms out of his hoodie. As he crawls under the covers, it hits me that he’s fifteen years old now.

His birthday was October 21.

“Hey,” I say. He looks at me. “Happy birthday.”

“What are you talking about?”

“I missed it.”

“Oh. Yeah.”

“How was it?”

“Fine, I guess.”

“What’d you do?”

“We went to the movies and out to dinner. Then I hung out with Joel and Angela.”

“Who’s Angela?”

“Friend.”

“Girlfriend?” He blushes in the firelight. “I’m really curious—did you pass your driving test?”

He gives a small smile. “I now have a learner’s permit.”

“That’s great. So did he take you?”

Charlie nods.

Damn. That hurts.

I pull the sheets and blankets up to Charlie’s shoulders and kiss him on the forehead. It’s been years since I tucked my son into bed, and I try to enjoy the moment, to make it last. But like all good things, it goes by so fast.

Charlie stares up at me in the firelight, asks, “Are you okay, Dad?”

“No. Not really. But I’m with you guys now. That’s all that matters. This other version of me…you liked him?”

“He’s not my father.”

“I know, but did you—?”

“He’s not my father.”

I get up from the sofa, throw another log on the fire, and walk back through the kitchen toward the other side of the house, the wood floor creaking as I walk.

It’s almost too cold to sleep in this room, but Daniela has taken all the sheets off the beds upstairs and found extra blankets in the closets.

The walls are covered in wood.

A space heater glows in the corner, smelling like burning dust.

I hear a sound coming from inside the bathroom.

Crying.

I knock on the thin door.

"Daniela?"

I hear her gasp.

"What?"

"Can I come in?"

She's quiet for a moment.

Then the lock clicks open.

I find Daniela curled up in the corner by an old clawfoot tub, her knees pulled to her chest, eyes red and puffy.

I've never seen her like this—actually shaking, falling apart in front of me.

She says, "I can't. I just…I can't."

"Can't what?"

“You’re right here in front of me, and I love you so much, but then I think about all those other versions of you, and—”

“They aren’t here, Daniela.”

“They want to be.”

“But they’re not.”

“I don’t know how to think or feel about this. And then I wonder…”

She loses what little control she had left.

It’s like watching ice break.

“What do you wonder?” I ask.

“I mean…are you even you?”

“What are you talking about?”

“How do I know you’re my Jason? You say you left our house in early October, and that you didn’t see me again until this morning at the police station. But how do I know you’re the man I love?”

I move down onto the floor.

“Look in my eyes, Daniela.”

She does.

Through tears.

“Can’t you see it’s me? Can’t you tell?”

She says, “I can’t stop thinking about the last month with him. It makes me sick.”

“What was it like?”

“Jason, don’t do that to me. Don’t do it to you.”

“Every day I was in that corridor, in the box, trying to find my way home—I thought about the two of you. I tried not to, but try to imagine what it was like for me.”

Daniela opens her knees, and as I crawl between them, she pulls me in close against her chest and runs her fingers through my hair.

She asks, “Do you really want to know?”

No.

But I have to.

I say, “I’ll always wonder.”

I rest my head against her.

Feel the rise and fall of her chest.

She says, “To be honest, it was amazing at first. The reason I remember that night you went to Ryan’s party so clearly is because of how you— he —acted when you got home. At first, I thought you were drunk, but it wasn’t that. It was like…like you were looking at me in this new way.

“I still remember, all those years ago, the first time we made love in my loft. I was lying in bed, naked, waiting for you. And you just stood at the end of the bed for a minute and stared at me. It felt like it was the first time you had really seen me. Maybe the first time anyone had ever really seen me. It was the hottest thing.

“This other Jason looked at me like that, and there was this new energy between us. Kind of like how it feels when you come home after a weekend at one of your conferences, but much more intense.”

I ask, “So with him, it must’ve been like the first time we were together?”

She doesn’t answer right away.

Just breathes for a while.

Then says finally, “I’m so sorry.”

“It’s not your fault.”

“After a couple weeks, it hit me that this wasn’t just a one-night, or even one-weekend, thing. I realized that something in you had changed.”

“What was different?”

“A million little things. The way you dressed. The way you got ready in the morning. The things you talked about at dinner.”

“The way I fucked you?”

“Jason.”

“Please don’t lie to me. I can’t handle that.”

“Yes. It was different.”

“Better.”

“Like it was new again. You did things you never did. Or hadn’t in a long time. It was like I was something you needed, not just wanted. Like I was your oxygen.”

“Do you want this other Jason?”

“No. I want the man I’ve built a life with. The man I made Charlie with. But I need to know you’re that man.”

I sit up and look at her in this small, windowless bathroom in the middle of nowhere that smells a little moldy.

She looks at me.

So tired.

I struggle to my feet and help her up.

We move into the bedroom.

Daniela gets into bed, and I turn off the lights and climb in beside her under the cold sheets.

The bed is creaky, and even a small movement makes the headboard hit the wall, which shakes the picture frames.

She’s wearing underwear and a white T-shirt, and she smells like she’s been in the car all day without a shower—deodorant wearing off mixed with a bit of stink.

I love it.

She whispers in the dark, “How do we solve this, Jason?”

“I’m trying.”

“What does that mean?”

“It means ask me again in the morning.”

Her breath on my face is sweet and warm.

What feels like home to me.

She falls asleep fast, breathing deeply.

I think I will too, but when I close my eyes, my thoughts race. I see myself stepping out of elevators. In parked cars. Sitting on the bench across the street from our brownstone.

I see myself everywhere.

The room is dark except for the glowing space heater in the corner.

The house is quiet.

I can’t sleep.

I need to solve this.

Quietly, I get out of bed. At the door, I stop and look back at Daniela, safe under a pile of blankets.

I walk down the noisy hardwood floor of the hallway, the house getting warmer as I get closer to the living room.

The fire is already low.

I add some more wood.

For a long time, I just sit staring into the flames, watching the wood slowly turn into glowing embers as my son snores softly behind me.

The idea first came to me on the drive north today, and I’ve been thinking about it ever since.

It seemed crazy at first.

But the more I think it through, the more it seems like my only choice.

In the living room next to the TV stand, there’s a desk with an old Mac and a very old printer. I turn the computer on. If it needs a password or there’s no Internet, this will have to wait until tomorrow, when I can find a café in town.

I’m in luck. There’s a guest login.

I open the web browser and go to that asonjayessenday email account.

The link still works.

Are you a new user?
