Light from the window wakes me up.

Daniela is in my arms.

For the longest time, I just lie there.

Holding her.

This amazing woman.

After a while, I pull away and grab my clothes off the floor.

I dress by the dying fire—just glowing embers—and throw on the last two logs.

We’ve overslept.

The clock on the stove reads 9:30, and through the window above the sink, I see sunlight slanting down through the evergreens and birches, creating patches of light and shadow across the forest floor as far as I can see.

I go outside into the cool morning and step off the porch.

Past the back of the cabin, the land slopes gently to the edge of a lake.

I walk out to the end of a snowcapped pier.

There’s a bit of ice near the shore, but it’s too early in the year for the rest of the lake to freeze, even after the storm.

I brush the snow off a bench, sit down, and watch the sun rise behind the pine trees.

The cold feels good, like a shot of espresso.

Mist rises from the water.

I hear footsteps in the snow behind me.

I turn and see Daniela walking down the pier, following my tracks.

She’s carrying two hot mugs of coffee, her hair is beautifully messy, and she has blankets around her shoulders like a shawl.

As I watch her come closer, I realize this might be the last morning I’ll ever have with her. I’m going back to Chicago tomorrow. By myself.

She gives me both mugs, then wraps one of her blankets around me. She sits on the bench, and we drink our coffee and look out at the lake.

I say, “I always thought we’d end up somewhere like this.”

“I didn’t know you wanted to move to Wisconsin.”

“When we’re older. Find a cabin to fix up.”

“ Can you fix things?” She laughs. “I’m kidding. I know what you mean.”

“Maybe spend summers here with the grandchildren. You could paint by the lake.”

“What would you do?”

“I don’t know. Finally read all my New Yorker magazines. Just be with you.”

She reaches down and touches the piece of thread that’s still tied around my ring finger. “What’s this?”

“Jason2 took my wedding ring, and early on, I started to forget what was real. Who I was. If I’d ever been married to you. So I tied this string around my finger to remember that you, this you, existed.”

She kisses me.

For a long time.

I say, “I need to tell you something.”

“What?”

“In that first Chicago I woke up in—the one where I found you at that art show about the multiverse—”

“What?” She smiles. “Did you sleep with me?”

“Yeah.”

The smile fades.

She just looks at me for a moment, and there’s almost no feeling in her voice when she asks, “Why?”

“I didn’t know where I was or what was happening. Everyone thought I was crazy. I was starting to think so too. Then I found you—the only thing I knew in a world that was all wrong. I really wanted that Daniela to be you, but she wasn’t. She couldn’t be. Just like the other Jason isn’t me.”

“So you were just sleeping with people as you went through the multiverse then?”

“That was the only time, and I didn’t know where I was when it happened. I didn’t know if I was going crazy or what.”

“And how was she? How was I?”

“Maybe we shouldn’t—”

“I told you.”

“Okay. It’s just how you told the story about this other Jason coming home that first night. It felt like being with you before I knew I loved you. Like feeling that amazing connection again for the first time. What are you thinking right now?”

“I’m deciding how angry I should be at you.”

“Why should you be angry at all?”

“Oh, is that what you’re saying? It isn’t cheating if it’s another version of me?”

“I mean, it’s a new idea, at least.”

This makes her laugh.

That it makes her laugh says everything about why I love her.

“What was she like?” Daniela asks.

“She was you if we hadn't met. Without Charlie. She was sort of seeing Ryan Holder.”

“Shut up. And I was this successful artist?”

“You were.”

“Did you like what I created?”

“It was amazing. You were amazing. Do you want to hear about it?”

“I’d love to.”

I tell her about the Plexiglas maze, what it felt like to walk through it. The striking images. The impressive design.

It makes her eyes light up.

And it makes her sad.

“Do you think I was happy?” she asks.

“What do you mean?”

“With everything I’d given up to be this woman.”

“I don’t know. I was with this woman for forty-eight hours. I think, like you, like me, like everyone, she had regrets. I think sometimes she woke up at night wondering if she’d chosen the right path. Afraid she hadn’t. Wondering what a life with me might have been like.”

“I wonder those things sometimes.”

“I’ve seen so many versions of you. With me. Without me. Artist. Teacher. Graphic designer. But it’s all, in the end, just life. We see it as a big picture, like one long story, but when you’re living it, it’s just day-to-day, right? And isn’t that what you have to accept?”

Out in the middle of the lake, a fish jumps, its splash making perfect, even ripples across the smooth water.

I say, “Last night, you asked me how we solve this.”

“Any good ideas?”

My first thought is to shield her from what I’m thinking about, but we don’t keep secrets in our marriage. We share everything. Even the toughest things. It’s a core part of who we are as a couple.

So, I tell her what I suggested to the chat room last night and see her face change with anger, horror, shock, and fear.

She finally says, “You want to raffle me off? Like some cheap prize?”

“Daniela—”

“I don’t need you to be a hero.”

“No matter what, you’ll still have me.”

“But a different version of you. That’s what you mean, right? What if he’s like that jerk who destroyed our lives? What if he isn’t as good as you?”

I look away from her, out at the lake, and fight back tears.

She asks, “Why would you give yourself up so someone else can be with me?”

“We all have to give up something, Daniela. It’s the only way you and Charlie can be okay. Please. Just let me make your lives in Chicago safe again.”

When we walk back inside, Charlie is at the stove making pancakes.

“Smells great,” I say.

He asks, “Will you make your fruit thing?”

“Sure.”

It takes me a moment to find the cutting board and a knife.

I stand next to my son, peeling and cutting the apples and adding them to a pan of warm maple syrup.

Through the windows, the sun rises higher, and the forest brightens.

We eat and talk easily, and sometimes it feels almost normal, like I’m not about to have my last breakfast with them.

In the early afternoon, we walk to town, down the middle of the old country road, the pavement dry in the sun, snowy in the shadows.

We buy clothes at a thrift shop and then see an old movie at the small downtown cinema. It came out six months ago.

It’s a silly romantic comedy.

It’s exactly what we need.

We stay until the end, until the lights come on, and when we leave the theater, the sky is already getting dark.

At the edge of town, we stop at the only restaurant that’s open—the Ice River Roadhouse.

We sit at the bar.

Daniela orders a glass of pinot noir. I order a beer for myself, a Coke for Charlie.

The place is busy, the only thing happening on a weeknight in Ice River, Wisconsin.

We order food.

I drink a second beer, and then a third.

Soon, Daniela and I are tipsy and the roadhouse is getting louder.

She puts her hand on my leg.

Her eyes are shiny from the wine, and it feels so good to be near her again. I’m trying not to think about how everything is my last time experiencing it, but the thought is hard to ignore.

The roadhouse gets more crowded.

It’s wonderfully loud.

A band starts setting up on a small stage in the back.

I’m drunk.

Not angry or messy.

Just perfectly happy.

If I think about anything else, I get upset, so I only think about right now.

The band plays country music with four people, and soon Daniela and I are slow-dancing in a crowd on a small dance floor.

Her body is close to mine, my hand on her lower back, and with the steel guitar music and how she’s looking at me, I just want to take her back to our old bed and make all the pictures fall off the walls.

—

Daniela and I are laughing, and I don’t even know why.

Charlie says, “You guys are drunk.”

That might be a bit much, but not really.

I say, “We needed to let loose.”

He says to Daniela, “Things haven’t felt like this in the last month, have they?”

She looks at me.

“No, it hasn’t.”

We walk unsteadily up the highway in the dark, no headlights behind or ahead.

The woods completely silent.

Not even a breeze.

As still as a painting.

—

I lock our room.

Daniela helps me lift the mattress off the bed.

We put it on the floor and turn off the lights and take off all our clothes.

It’s cold in the room, even with the heater on.

We climb naked and shivering under the blankets.

Her skin is smooth and cool against mine, her mouth soft and warm.

I kiss her.

She says she needs me inside her so much it hurts.

Being with Daniela isn’t like being home.

It is home.

I remember thinking that the first time I made love to her fifteen years ago. Thinking I’d found something I didn’t even know I’d been searching for.

It’s even more true tonight as the hardwood floor groans softly beneath us and the moonlight peeks through the gap in the curtains just enough to light her face as her mouth opens and her head tilts back and she whispers my name, so urgently.

—

We’re sweaty, our hearts racing in the silence.

Daniela runs her fingers through my hair, and she’s staring at me in the dark the way I love.

“What is it?” I ask.

“Charlie was right.”

“About?”

“What he said on the walk home. It hasn't been like this since Jason2 came here. You aren’t replaceable. Not even by you. I keep thinking about how we met. At that point in our lives, we could’ve met anyone. But you showed up at that backyard party and saved me from that jerk. I know part of our story is the spark between us, but the other part is just as amazing. It’s the simple fact that you walked into my life at the exact moment you did. You instead of someone else. In some ways, isn’t that even more incredible than the spark itself? That we found each other at all?”

“It’s amazing.”

“What I realized is that the same thing happened yesterday. Of all the versions of Jason, it was you who pulled that crazy stunt at the diner, which landed you in jail, which brought us safely together.”

“So you’re saying it’s fate.”

She smiles. “I think I’m saying we found each other, for a second time.”

We make love again and fall asleep.

In the deep night, she wakes me, whispers in my ear, “I don’t want you to go.”

I turn to face her.

Her eyes are wide open in the dark.

My head hurts.

My mouth is dry.

I’m stuck in that confusing shift from drunk to hungover, when the fun slowly turns into pain.

“What if we just kept driving?” she says.

“To where?”

“I don’t know.”

“What are we going to tell Charlie? He has friends. Maybe a girlfriend. We just tell him to forget all that? He’s finally happy at school.”

“I know,” she says, “and I hate it, but yes, that’s what we tell him.”

“Where we live, our friends, our jobs—those things show who we are.”

“They’re not all that shows who we are. As long as I’m with you, I know exactly who I am.”

“Daniela, I want nothing more than to be with you, but if I don’t do this tomorrow, you and Charlie will never be safe. And no matter what happens, you will still have me.”

“I don’t want some other version of you. I want you.”

—

I wake in the dark with my pulse pounding in my head and my mouth bone-dry.

Pulling on my jeans and shirt, I stumble down the hall.

With no fire tonight, the only light on the entire ground floor is a small nightlight plugged into an outlet above the kitchen counter.

I grab a glass from the cabinet and fill it with water from the faucet.

Drink it all.

Fill it again.

The heater shuts off.

I stand at the sink, drinking the cold water.

The cabin is so quiet I can hear the floorboards creaking as the wood expands and shrinks in the far parts of the house.

Through the window above the kitchen sink, I stare into the woods.

I'm happy Daniela wants me, but I don’t know what happens next. I don’t know how to protect them.

My head is spinning.

A short distance past the Jeep, something catches my eye.

A shadow moving across the snow.

Adrenaline surges.

I set the glass down, head to the front door, and step into my boots.

On the porch, I button my shirt and walk into the packed snow between the steps and the car.

Then out past the Jeep.

There.

I see what caught my eye from the kitchen.

As I approach, it’s still moving.

Larger than I first thought.

The size of a man.

No.

Jesus.

It is a man.

The trail where he has pulled himself is easy to see from the streaks of blood that seem black in the starlight.

He's groaning as he crawls toward the front porch. He will never make it.

I get to him, kneel next to him.

It’s me, even the coat and the Velocity Laboratories backpack and the thread ring.

He's holding his stomach with one hand, which is covered in steaming blood, and he looks up at me with the most desperate eyes I've ever seen.

I ask, “Who did this to you?”

“One of us.”

“How’d you find me here?”

He coughs up a mist of blood. “Help me.”

“How many of us are here?”

“I think I’m dying.”

I look around. It only takes a second to spot the bloody footprints leading away from this Jason toward the Jeep, and then around the side of the cabin.

The dying Jason is saying my name.

Our name.

Begging for my help.

And I want to help him, but all I can think is—they found us.

Somehow, they found us.

He says, “Don’t let them hurt her.”

I look back at the car.

I didn’t see it at first, but now I notice all the tires are slashed.

Somewhere nearby, I hear footsteps in the snow.

I search the woods for movement, but the starlight doesn’t reach deep into the forest away from the cabin.

He says, “I’m not ready for this.”

I look into his eyes as my panic grows. “If this is the end, be brave.”

A gunshot breaks the silence.

It came from behind the cabin, near the lake.

I rush back through the snow, past the Jeep, running toward the front porch, trying to understand what’s happening.

From inside the cabin, Daniela calls my name.

I climb the steps.

Burst through the front door.

Daniela is coming down the hallway, wrapped in a blanket and lit from behind by the light spilling out of the master bedroom.

My son approaches from the kitchen.

I lock the front door behind me as Daniela and Charlie meet in the entryway.

She asks, “Was that a gunshot?”

“Yeah.”

“What’s happening?”

“They found us.”

“Who?”

“I did.”

“How is that possible?”

“We have to leave right now. Both of you go to our bedroom, get dressed, and start packing our things. I’m going to lock the back door, then I’ll come join you.”

They go down the hallway.

The front door is locked.

The only other way into the house is through the French doors from the screened porch into the living room.

I go through the kitchen.

Daniela and Charlie will expect me to tell them what to do next.

And I have no idea.

We can’t take the car.

We’ll have to walk.

As I enter the living room, my thoughts rush through my head.

What do we need to take?

Phones.

Money.

Where’s our money?

In an envelope in the bottom drawer of our bedroom dresser.

What else do we need?

What can’t we forget?

How many versions of me followed us here?

Am I going to die tonight?

By my own hand?

I feel my way through the darkness, past the sleeper sofa, to the French doors. As I reach down to test the handles, I realize—it shouldn’t be this cold in here.

Unless these doors were just opened.

Like, a few seconds ago.

They’re locked now, and I don’t remember locking them.

Through the glass panes, I can see something on the patio, but it’s too dark to see what it is. I think it’s moving.

I need to get back to my family.

As I turn away from the French doors, a shadow rises from behind the sofa.

My heart stops.

A lamp blinks on.

I see myself standing ten feet away, one hand on the light switch, the other pointing a gun at me.

He’s wearing nothing but boxer shorts.

His hands are covered in blood.

Coming around the sofa, gun aimed at my face, he says quietly, “Take your clothes off.”

The slash across his face tells me who he is.

I glance behind me through the French doors.

The lamplight shows just enough of the patio for me to see a pile of clothes—Timberlands and a peacoat—and another Jason lying on his side, his head in a pool of blood, throat cut open.

He says, “I won’t tell you again.”

I start unbuttoning my shirt.

“We know each other,” I say.

“Obviously.”

“No, that cut on your face. We had beers together two nights ago.”

I watch that information sink in, but it doesn’t stop him like I'd hoped.

He says, “That doesn’t change what has to happen. This is the end, brother. You’d do the same and you know it.”

“I wouldn’t, actually. I thought so at first, but I wouldn’t.”

I slip my arms out of the sleeves, toss him the shirt.

I know what he’s planning: dress himself in my clothes. Go to Daniela pretending to be me. He’ll have to cut his face again to make it look like a fresh wound.

I say, “I had a plan to protect her.”

“Yeah, I read it. I’m not giving myself up so someone else can be with my wife and son. Jeans too.”

I unbutton them, thinking, I was wrong. We’re not all the same.

“How many of us have you killed tonight?” I ask.

“Four. I’ll kill a thousand more if that’s what it takes.”

As I pull off the jeans, one leg at a time, I say, “Something changed you in the box, in those worlds you talked about. What made you like this?”

“Maybe you don’t want them back enough. And if that’s true, you don’t deserve them any—”

I throw the jeans at his face and charge at him.

Wrapping my arms around Jason’s legs, I lift with all my strength and slam him into the wall, knocking the air out of him.

The gun hits the floor.

I kick it into the kitchen as Jason falls and smash my knee into his face.

I hear bone break.

Grabbing his head, I bring my knee back to hit him again, but he kicks my left leg out from under me.

I slam into the hardwood floor, hitting the back of my head so hard I see flashes of light, and then he’s on top of me, blood dripping off his ruined face, one hand squeezing my throat.

When he hits me, I feel my cheek break in a burst of pain below my left eye.

He hits me again.

I blink through tears and blood, and when I can see clearly, he’s holding a knife in the hand he was hitting me with.

Gunshot.

My ears ringing.

A small black hole through his chest and blood spilling out of it and down the center of his chest. The knife drops from his hands onto the floor beside me. I watch him put a finger in the hole and try to close it, but the blood won’t stop.

He takes a wet, shaky breath and looks up at the man who shot him.

I stretch my neck, just enough to see another Jason pointing a gun at him. This one is clean-shaven, and he’s wearing the black leather jacket Daniela gave me ten years ago for our anniversary.

On his left hand, a gold wedding band shines.

My ring.

Jason2 pulls the trigger again, and the next bullet tears off the side of my attacker’s head.

He falls.

I turn over and sit up slowly.

Spitting blood.

My face burning.

Jason2 aims the gun at me.

He’s going to pull the trigger.

I actually see my death coming, and I have no words, just a quick picture of me as a kid on my grandparents’ farm in western Iowa. A warm spring day. A huge sky. Cornfields. I’m kicking a soccer ball through the yard toward my brother, who’s guarding the “goal”—a space between two maple trees.

I think, Why this last memory as I'm about to die? Was I the happiest then? The most truly myself?

“Stop it!”

Daniela is standing in the kitchen nook, now dressed.

She looks at Jason2.

She looks at me.

At the Jason with a bullet in his head.

The Jason on the porch with his throat cut.

And somehow, without her voice even shaking, she asks, “Where is my husband?”

Jason2 looks surprised for a moment.

I wipe the blood out of my eyes. “Right here.”

“What did we do tonight?” she asks.

“We danced to bad country music, came home, and made love.” I look at the man who stole my life. “You’re the one who kidnapped me?”

He looks at Daniela.

“She knows everything,” I say. “There’s no point in lying.”

Daniela asks, “How could you do this to me? To our family?”

Charlie appears beside his mother, taking in the horror all around us.

Jason2 looks at her.

Then at Charlie.

Jason2 is only six or seven feet away, but I’m sitting on the floor.

I couldn’t reach him before he pulled the trigger.

I think, Keep him talking.

“How did you find us?” I ask.

“Charlie’s phone has a find-my-phone app.”

Charlie says, “I only turned it on for one text late last night. I didn’t want Angela to think I’d ignored her.”

I look at Jason2. “And the other Jasons?”

“I don’t know. I guess they followed me here.”

“How many?”

“I have no idea.” He turns to Daniela. “I got everything I ever wanted, except you. And you stayed in my thoughts. What we could’ve been. That’s why—”

“Then you should’ve stayed with me fifteen years ago when you had the chance.”

“Then I wouldn’t have built the box.”

“And why would that be so bad? Look around. Has your life's work done anything but cause pain?”

He says, “Every moment, every breath, has a choice. But life isn't perfect. We make the wrong choices. So we live always regretting things, and is there anything worse? I built something that could erase regret. Let you find worlds where you made the right choice.”

Daniela says, “Life doesn’t work that way. You live with your choices and learn. You don’t cheat.”

Slowly, I shift my weight to my feet.

But he stops me, says, “Don’t.”

“Are you going to kill me in front of them?” I ask. “Really?”

“You had such big dreams,” he says to me. “You could’ve stayed in my world, in the life I made, and lived them.”

“Oh, is that how you excuse what you did?”

“I know what you think. The awful feeling you have every day walking to the train to teach, thinking, Is this all there is? Maybe you're brave enough to admit it. Maybe you’re not.”

I say, “You don’t get to—”

“Actually, I do get to judge you, Jason, because I am you. Maybe we split into different paths fifteen years ago, but we think the same. You weren’t meant to teach college physics. To watch people like Ryan Holder get the praise that should’ve been yours. There is nothing you can’t do. I know, because I’ve done it all. Look at what I created. I could wake up in your home every morning and be happy with myself because I reached every goal I ever had. Can you say the same? What have you done?”

“I made a life with them.”

“I gave you, gave both of us, what everyone secretly wants. The chance to live two lives. Our best two lives.”

“I don’t want two lives. I want them.”

I look at Daniela. I look at my son.

Daniela says to Jason2, “And I want him. Please. Let us have our life. You don’t have to do this.”

His face becomes stern.

His eyes get smaller.

He moves toward me.

Charlie screams, “No!”

The gun is inches from my face.

I look up into my double’s eyes, ask, “So you kill me, then what? What do you gain? It won’t make her love you.”

His hand shakes.

Charlie moves toward Jason2.

“Don’t touch him.”

“Stay there, son.” I stare at the gun. “You’re beaten, Jason.”

Charlie keeps coming, Daniela tries to hold him back, but he pulls away.

As Charlie gets closer, Jason2 looks away from me for a moment.

I knock the gun away, grab the knife from the floor, and stab him in the stomach, the blade going in easily.

Standing, I yank the knife out, and as Jason2 falls into me, grabbing my shoulders, I stab him again.

Over and over and over.

So much blood pouring through his shirt and onto my hands, and the rusty smell filling the room.

He’s holding me, the knife still stuck in his gut.

I think about him with Daniela as I twist the blade and pull it out and push him away from me.

He wobbles.

Wincing.

Holding his stomach.

Blood leaking through his fingers.

His legs give out.

He sits, then with a groan, lies on his side, resting his head on the floor.

I look at Daniela and Charlie. Then I go to Jason2 and search his pockets as he moans, finding my car keys.

“Where’s the Suburban?” I ask.

He answers so quietly I have to lean in to hear: “A quarter mile past the turnoff. On the shoulder.”

I hurry to the clothes I took off earlier and get dressed quickly.

When my shirt is buttoned, I bend to tie my boots, glancing at Jason2, bleeding on the cabin floor.

I pick up the gun and wipe the handle on my jeans.

We need to leave.

Who knows how many more are coming.

My double says my name.

I glance over—he’s holding my wedding band in his bloody fingers.

I walk to him, and as I take the ring and slide it onto my finger over the thread, Jason2 grabs my arm and pulls me down to his face.

He’s trying to speak.

I say, “I can’t hear you.”

“Look…in…the glove box.”

Charlie comes over, hugging me tightly, trying not to cry, but his shoulders shake and he starts sobbing. As he cries in my arms like a child, I think about the awful thing he’s just seen, and it makes me cry too.

I hold his face in my hands.

I say, “You saved my life. If you hadn’t tried to stop him, I wouldn’t have had a chance.”

“Really?”

“Really. Also I’m going to smash your damn phone. Now we have to go. Back door.”

We rush through the living room, dodging puddles of blood.

I unlock the French doors, and as Charlie and Daniela step onto the screened-in porch, I glance back at the man who caused all this.

His eyes are still open, blinking slowly, watching us leave.

Stepping outside, I pull the doors closed behind me.

I have to walk through the blood of one more Jason to reach the screen door.

I’m not sure which way to go.

We head down to the shoreline, follow it north through the trees.

The lake as smooth and black as glass.

I keep scanning the woods for other Jasons—one could step out from behind a tree and kill me at any second.

After a hundred yards, we turn from the shoreline and head toward the road.

Four gunshots echo at the cabin.

We’re running now, struggling through the snow, all of us gasping for breath.

The rush of adrenaline keeps the pain of my bruised face away, but I wonder how long that will last.

We burst out of the forest onto the road.

I stand on the double-yellow line, and for a moment, the woods are silent.

“Which way?” Daniela asks.

“North.”

We jog down the middle of the road.

Charlie says, “I see it.”

Straight ahead, off the right-side shoulder, I spot the back of our Suburban pulled halfway into the trees.

We pile inside, and as I put the key in the ignition, I see movement in the side mirror—a shadow running toward us on the road.

I start the engine, release the brake, and shift into gear.

Swinging the Suburban around, I push the gas pedal all the way down.

I say, “Get down.”

“Why?” Daniela asks.

“Just do it!”

We speed into darkness.

I turn on the lights.

They shine right on Jason, standing in the road, pointing a gun at the car.

A shot rings out.

A bullet punches through the windshield and tears through the headrest, missing my right ear by an inch.

Another flash, another shot.

Daniela screams.

How damaged am I to risk hitting Daniela and Charlie?

Jason tries to dodge, but he's a split second too slow.

The bumper's edge hits his side hard, the impact brutal.

It whips him around fast, his head smashing into the passenger window hard enough to shatter it.

In the rearview mirror, I see him rolling across the road as we speed away.

“Is anyone hurt?” I ask.

“I’m fine,” Charlie says.

Daniela sits up.

“Daniela?”

“I’m okay,” she says, starting to brush bits of glass from her hair.

—

We speed down the dark highway.

No one speaks.

It’s three in the morning, and we’re the only car on the road.

The night air rushes through the bullet holes in the windshield, the noise of the road loud through the broken window next to Daniela’s head.

I ask, “Do you still have your phone?”

“Yeah.”

“Give it to me. Yours too, Charlie.”

They hand them over, and I lower my window a bit and throw the phones out of the car.

“They’re going to keep coming, aren’t they?” she asks. “They’re never going to stop.”

She’s right. The other Jasons can’t be trusted. I was wrong about the lottery.

I say, “I thought there was a way to solve this.”

“So what do we do?”

Exhaustion crushes me.

My face hurts more every second.

I look over at Daniela. “Open the glove box.”

“What am I looking for?” she asks.

“I’m not sure.”

She pulls out the Suburban’s instruction book.

Our insurance papers and registration.

A tire-pressure tool.

A flashlight.

And a small leather bag I know well.

Chapter 15

We’re sitting in our bullet-ridden Suburban in the empty parking lot.

I drove all night.

I look at my face in the mirror. My left eye is purple, very swollen, and the skin on my left cheek is black from the blood underneath.

It all hurts a lot when I touch it.

I look back at Charlie, and then at Daniela.

Reaching across the center console, she runs her fingernails down the back of my neck.

She says, “What else can we do?”

“Charlie? You need to decide too.”

“I don’t want to leave.”

“I know.”

“But I guess we have to.”

A strange thought crosses my mind like a quick summer cloud.

We’re so clearly at the end. Everything we’ve built—our house, our jobs, our friends, our life together—it’s all gone. We have nothing left but each other, and yet, right now, I’m happier than ever.

—

Morning sun shines through cracks in the roof, lighting spots along the dark, empty hallway.

“This place is cool,” Charlie says.

“Do you know where you’re going?” Daniela asks.

“Sadly, I could take us where we need to go blindfolded.”

As I lead us through the abandoned paths, I’m beyond tired. Running on caffeine and fear. The gun I took from the cabin is stuck in the back of my pants, and Jason2’s leather bag is tucked under my arm. It hits me that as we drove down to the South Side at dawn, I never even looked at the skyline as we passed just west of downtown.

One last look would’ve been nice.

I feel a pang of regret, but quickly dismiss it.

I think of all the nights I lay in bed, wondering how things might be if I’d made different choices, if I hadn’t become a father and a so-so physics professor instead of a star in my field. I guess it’s just wanting what I didn’t get. What I thought could have been mine if I’d chosen differently.

But the truth is, I did make those different choices.

Because I am not just me.

My idea of who I am has been broken—I am just one part of an endless being called Jason Dessen who has made every choice and lived every life possible.

I can't help but think we're more than just our choices, that all the paths we could have taken somehow add to who we are.

But none of the other Jasons matter.

I don’t want their lives.

I want mine.

Because even though everything is messed up, I wouldn’t want to be anywhere else but with this Daniela, this Charlie. If anything was even a little different, they wouldn’t be the people I love.

We move slowly down the stairs toward the generator room, our steps echoing in the big, open space.

One flight up from the bottom, Daniela says, “Someone’s down there.”

I stop.

My mouth feels dry as I look into the darkness below.

I see a man stand up from where he was sitting on the floor.

Then another next to him.

And another.

All through the darkness between the last generator and the box, versions of me are getting to their feet.

Fuck.

They came early for the lottery.

Dozens of them.

All watching us.

I look back up the stairs, my ears ringing so loudly it drowns everything out in a flood of panic.

Daniela says, “We’re not running.” She pulls the gun from my pants and loops her arm through mine. “Charlie, grab your father’s arm and don’t let go, no matter what.”

“You sure?” I ask.

“Completely.”

With Charlie and Daniela holding onto me, I slowly walk down the last few steps and move across the broken concrete.

My doubles stand between us and the box.

I can’t breathe.

Only the sound of our steps and the wind through the empty window frames above.

I hear Daniela take a shaky breath.

Charlie’s hand is sweaty in mine.

“Just keep walking,” I say.

One of them moves ahead.

He says to me, “This isn’t what you said would happen.”

I say, “Things changed. Some of us tried to kill me last night, and—”

Daniela cuts in, “One of you shot at our car with Charlie in it. It’s over. Done.”

She pulls me forward.

We’re getting closer to them.

They’re not moving out of our way.

Someone says, “You’re here now. Let’s have that lottery.”

Daniela squeezes my arm even tighter.

She says, “Charlie and I are going into the box with this man.” Her voice breaks. “If there were another way…We’re all just doing the best we can.”

I can’t avoid it—I make eye contact with the closest Jason, his envy a living thing. In ragged clothes, he reeks of homelessness and despair.

He says to me in a low growl, “Why should you get her?”

The Jason next to him says, “This isn’t about him. It’s about what she wants. What our son needs. That’s all that matters. Let them pass. All of you.”

The crowd begins to move apart.

We move slowly through the path of Jasons.

Some are crying.

Hot, angry, desperate tears.

I am too.

So is Daniela.

So is Charlie.

Others stand still and tense.

Finally, the last one moves aside.

The box looms right in front.

The door wide open.

Charlie enters first, then Daniela.

I keep waiting for something to happen as my heart hammers in my chest.

At this point, I expect anything.

I step through the doorway, touch the door, and see my world one last time.

It’s a picture I will always remember.

Light from the high windows shines on the old generators as fifty of me all stare at the box in a shocked and strange and heartbroken silence.

—

The door locks.

The bolt slides into place.

I turn on the flashlight and look at my family.

For a second, Daniela looks like she’s going to fall apart, but she stays strong.

I take out the syringes, the needles, the vials.

Set everything up.

Just like old times.

I help Charlie push his sleeve up.

“First time’s a little intense. You ready?”

He nods.

Holding his arm still, I push the needle into the vein, pull back on the plunger, see blood mix in the syringe.

As I shoot all of Ryan’s drug into my son’s blood, Charlie’s eyes roll back and he slumps against the wall.

I tie the band around my arm.

“How long does it last?” Daniela asks.

“About an hour.”

Charlie sits up.

“You okay?” I ask.

“That was weird.”

I inject myself. It’s been a few days since I last used, and the drug hits me harder than usual.

When I feel better, I pick up the last syringe.

“Your turn, my love.”

“I hate needles.”

“Don’t worry. I’m pretty good at this now.”

Soon we’re all feeling the drug.

Daniela takes the light from me and moves away from the door.

As it lights up the hallway, I watch her face. I watch my son’s face. They look afraid. Amazed. I think back to when I first saw the hallway, to the horror and wonder that took over me.

The feeling of being nowhere.

And in between.

“How far does it go?” Charlie asks.

“It never ends.”

—

We walk together down this hallway that stretches forever.

I still can’t believe I’m here again.

That I’m here with them.

I’m not sure what I’m feeling, but it isn’t the pure fear I felt before.

Charlie says, “So each of these doors…”

“Opens into another world.”

“Wow.”

I look at Daniela, ask, “You okay?”

“Yes. I’m with you.”

We’ve been walking for a while, and we don't have much time left.

I say, “The drug will stop working soon. We should probably go.”

And so we stop in front of a door that looks just like the others.

Daniela says, “I was thinking—all these other Jasons made it back to their world. What if one of them comes into wherever we go? They should all think like you, right?”

“Yeah, but I’m not going to open a door, and neither are you.”

I turn to Charlie.

He says, “Me? What if I mess up? What if I take us to some terrible place?”

“I trust you.”

“I do too,” Daniela says.

I say, “Even though you’ll be opening the door, we’re making the way to this next world together. The three of us.” Charlie looks at the door, tense. “Look,” I say, “I’ve tried to explain how the box works, but forget all that for a minute. The box is like life. If you go in with fear, you’ll find fear.”

“But I don’t even know where to start,” he says.

“It’s a blank canvas.”

I embrace my son.

I tell him I love him.

Tell him I’m so proud.

Then Daniela and I sit on the floor, our backs against the wall, facing Charlie and the door. She rests her head on my shoulder and holds my hand.

Driving here last night, I thought I’d be terrified to enter a new world, but I’m not scared at all.

I’m filled with a child's excitement to see what happens next.

As long as my people are with me, I’m ready for anything.

Charlie walks to the door and grabs the handle.

Just before he opens it, he takes a breath and looks back at us, as brave and strong as I’ve ever seen him.

A man.

I nod.

He turns the handle, and I hear the lock click open.

A line of light cuts into the corridor, so bright I have to shield my eyes for a moment. When I can see again, Charlie is standing in the doorway of the box, lit from behind.

Getting up, I help Daniela to stand, and we go to our son as the cold, empty hall fills with warmth and light.

A wind from the door brings the smell of wet ground and strange flowers.

A world right after a storm.

I put my hand on Charlie’s shoulder.

“Ready?” he asks.

“We’re right behind you.”

Acknowledgments

Dark Matter was the toughest job of my career, and I couldn’t have finished it without the help and support of the many generous, talented, and amazing people who supported me while I was writing it.

My agent and friend David Hale Smith worked some real magic this time, and the entire team at Inkwell Management has supported me every step of the way. Thanks to Richard Pine for his wise advice when we needed it most, to Alexis Hurley for her skill and determination in selling my work internationally, and to Nathaniel Jacks, deal-maker extraordinaire.

My film and TV manager, Angela Cheng Caplan, and entertainment attorney, Joel VanderKloot, are exceptional in every way. I’ve been so lucky to have them on my side.

The team at Crown are some of the smartest people I’ve ever worked with. Their passion and dedication to this book have been truly amazing. Thank you Molly Stern, Julian Pavia, Maya Mavjee, David Drake, Dyana Messina, Danielle Crabtree, Sarah Bedingfield, Chris Brand, Cindy Berman, and everyone at Penguin Random House for supporting this book.

And a second thank you to my brilliant editor, Julian Pavia, who pushed me harder than ever before and made this book better on every page.

I couldn’t ask for a better group trying to make Dark Matter the movie, a reality. Huge thanks to Matt Tolmach, Brad Zimmerman, David Manpearl, Ryan Doherty, and Ange Giannetti at Sony. And also to Michael De Luca and Rachel O’Connor, who were wonderful supporters of the book from the start.

Jacque Ben-Zekry edited all my Wayward Pines novels, and even though this wasn’t her book, she gave it the same care and attention as if it were. Dark Matter would be much less without her insight.

The physics and astronomy professor Clifford Johnson, Ph.D., helped me to not seem completely clueless when discussing the basic ideas of quantum mechanics. If I’ve made any mistakes, it’s my fault.

I could not have written Dark Matter without the work of many physicists, astronomers, and cosmologists who have dedicated their lives to finding fundamental truths about our existence. Stephen Hawking, Carl Sagan, Neil deGrasse Tyson, Michio Kaku, Rob Bryanton, and Amanda Gefter were essential in helping me begin to understand all things quantum. In particular, Michio Kaku’s clear comparison of a pond, carp, and hyperspace shaped my understanding of dimensionality and became the basis of Jason2’s explanation of the multiverse to Daniela.

My first readers stuck with me through many versions and gave me helpful feedback. Special thanks to my writing partner and great friend, Chad Hodge; my close friend, Jordan Crouch; my friends, Joe Konrath and Barry Eisler; the wonderful Ann Voss Peterson; and my insightful soul mate Marcus Sakey, who, when I visited Chicago two years ago, helped me see the potential of this book among many failing ideas, and pushed me to write it even though it scared me. Because it scared me. And a shout-out to the bar at the great Longman & Eagle in Logan Square (Chicago), where the shape and idea of Dark Matter came together.

And last but not least, thanks to my family: Rebecca, Aidan, Annslee, and Adeline. For everything. I love you.

Sinopsis

“Are you happy with your life?” These are the last words Jason Dessen hears before the masked person knocks him out. He wakes up strapped to a bed, surrounded by strangers in protective suits. A man Jason has never met smiles at him and says, “Welcome back, my friend.”

In this world he's woken up to, Jason’s life is not the one he knows. His wife is not his wife. His son was never born. And Jason is not just a college physics professor but a famous genius who has done something amazing--something impossible.

Is this world or the other one the dream? And even if the home he remembers is real, how can Jason ever get back to the family he loves? The answers are in a journey more amazing and scary than anything he could've imagined—one that will make him face the worst parts of himself as he fights a frightening enemy that seems impossible to beat.
